Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
The Dichotomy of Speed and Slowness
Al-Iji states the dichotomy and deduces atomic divisibility from observed speed disparity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the existence of speed differences in motion forces the conclusion that atoms are divisible.

Core Idea
Speed differences require atoms to be divisible.

Al-Iji begins his discussion of speed and slowness by setting up a crucial either/or: either all motions are equal in speed (which contradicts observation), or indivisible atoms are divisible. This logical move uses the principle of prevention of vacuity (man' al-khuluw). In this lesson, we will explore this dichotomy and why it compels the conclusion that atoms are divisible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possibilities
Read the passage and locate the two matters Al-Iji presents: either no disparity in speed or divisibility of atoms. Note the phrase 'one of two matters'.
2
Understand the logical link
Notice how non-divisibility necessarily implies no disparity, and existence of disparity implies divisibility. This is a biconditional relationship.
The two cannot be together in falsehood.
3
Apply observation
Al-Iji states that the first possibility (no disparity) is false because we clearly see motions differ in speed. So the second must be true.
4
Conclude the divisibility
Once the first possibility is negated, the second—divisibility of atoms—is established. This is the main conclusion of the passage.
This sets the stage for the two proofs to follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث : ما يتعلق بالسرعة والبطء وحاصله أحد الأمرين (لازم أي ثابت في الواقع على سبيل منع الخلو ( إما انتفاء تفاوت الحركات بالسرعة والبطء وإما تجزؤ الأجزاء ) التي لا تتجزأ فإنهما لا يجتمعان في الكذب لأن عدم التجزؤ يستلزم انتفاء التفاوت وعدم الانتفاء أعني وجود التفاوت يستلزم المتجزئ ) والأول ) وهو انتفاء تفاوت الحركات ( منتف ( ضرورة أن الحركات متفاوتة في السرعة والبطء ) فثبت الثاني)، وهو تجزؤ الأجزاء (بيان لزوم أحد الأمرين من طريقين: أحدهما أنه إذا تركبت المسافة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ فإذا ( قطع السريع جزءا منها ( فالبطيء لا يقف لما بينا من قبل ( أن البطء ليس لتخلل السكنات فهو ) أي البطء إذن يتحرك فإما أن يتحرك جزءاً أيضاً فالسريع كالبطيء وهو الأول) أعني انتفاء التفاوت فيما بين الحركات ( أو أقل من جزء) إذ لا مجال لتوهم حركته أكثر من جزء ( فيتجزأ ) الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ لثبوت ما هو أقل منه ( وهو الثاني ) من الأمرين اللذين أدعينا لزوم أحدهما وثانيهما) أي ثاني الطرفين المذكورين أن نبين أن ثمة حركة سريعة وبطيئة متلازمتين) بحيث يستحيل انفكاك إحداهما عن الأخرى ( فيستغني) حينئذ ( عن الاستعانة بأن البطء ليس لتخلل السكنات بل يكون ذلك ) أي تلازم هاتين الحركتين ( دليلاً على ذلك ) أي على أن البطء ليس للتخلل ( مستأنفاً ) كما نبهت عليه فيما مر وإذا كانت الحركتان متلازمتين ( فعندما تقطع السريعة جزأين قطعت البطيئة مثلها لزم تساوي السريعة والببطيئة ) وهو الأمر الأول ، ( أو أقل لزم التجزؤ ) وهو الأمر الثاني .
قوله : (على سبيل منع الخلو فإن التجزؤ والانتفاء متحققان معاً.
قوله : ( بأنهما يقفان من وقف وقوفاً أي لا نسلم أنهما يلتقيان في الوسط بحيث يكون ذلك الوسط .
The third type: What pertains to speed and slowness. Its sum is one of two matters (necessary, i.e., established in reality on the basis of prevention of vacuity), either the non-existence of disparity of motions in speed and slowness, or the divisibility of parts that are indivisible; for they [the two] are not together in falsehood, because non-divisibility necessitates non-existence of disparity, and non-non-existence, i.e., the existence of disparity, necessitates the divisible. And the first (i.e., non-existence of disparity of motions) is non-existent of necessity, because motions are disparate in speed and slowness, so the second is established, i.e., the divisibility of parts. (Explanation of the necessity of one of the two matters in two ways: One of them: If the distance is composed of indivisible parts, then when the fast [moving body] cuts a part of it, the slow [moving body] does not stop—because we have previously shown that slowness is not due to interpolation of rests—so it (i.e., the slowness) then moves. So either it also moves a part, then the fast is like the slow, which is the first [matter] (i.e., non-existence of disparity between motions); or less than a part, since there is no scope to imagine its motion more than a part, then the indivisible part becomes divisible due to the establishment of what is less than it, which is the second [matter] of the two matters we claimed one of them is necessary. The second [way] (i.e., the second of the two mentioned extremes): that we show that there is a fast motion and a slow motion that are inseparable, such that it is impossible for one to be separated from the other. Then one may dispense with seeking help from [the claim] that slowness is not due to interpolation of rests; rather that (i.e., the inseparability of these two motions) becomes an independent proof for that (i.e., that slowness is not due to interpolation), as I have pointed out previously. And when the two motions are inseparable, then when the fast [body] cuts two parts, the slow [body] cuts the like of them, so equality of the fast and the slow follows—which is the first matter; or less, then divisibility follows—which is the second matter.
His saying: (On the basis of prevention of vacuity, for divisibility and non-existence are both realized together.)
His saying: (That they both stop—from w-q-f, 'to stop'—meaning: we do not accept that they meet in the middle such that that middle exists.)
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument on motion and atomic divisibility. It begins by stating the third type (النوع الثالث) concerning speed and slowness. The author presents a dichotomy: either motions are not different in speed (i.e., all are equal) or indivisible atoms are divisible. He notes that these two cannot be false together, because if atoms are indivisible then motions cannot differ in speed (since differing would require dividing the atom), and if they are not equal—i.e., there is disparity—then atoms must be divisible. He asserts that disparity obviously exists, so divisibility is proven. He then gives two proofs (طريقان) to establish that one of the two must hold. The first proof assumes distance is composed of indivisible atoms. When a fast body and a slow body move along such a distance, the slow body cannot stop (since slowness is not due to rests). It must move; either it moves an entire atom—making it equal in speed to the fast one (negating disparity, the first option)—or it moves less than an atom, which divides the atom (establishing divisibility, the second option). The second proof shows there exist two motions that are inseparable (متلازمتين), like those in the subsequent six examples. From their inseparability, when the fast one covers two atoms, the slow one must cover at least one atom (equality) or less (divisibility). The commentary adds clarifications: the phrase 'prevention of vacuity' (منع الخلو) means the two possibilities are not both false, and the objection about meeting in the middle is rejected by reinterpretation.

Key Terms
السرعة
البطء
التفاوت
التجزؤ
منع الخلو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Al-Iji assumes atoms are divisible from the start.
Correction: He does not assume divisibility; he deduces it from the observed fact of speed differences, using the logical necessity that the two options cannot both be false.

Checkpoint
What are the two possible outcomes the author sets up in the third type?
A) Either motions are all equal in speed or atoms are divisible.
B) Either motions are all different or atoms are indivisible.
C) Either distance is continuous or motion is impossible.
D) Either the fast stops or the slow moves.

Key Takeaway
The observed disparity in speed forces the conclusion that atoms must be divisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument forces you to accept a conclusion based on a simple observation. When have you dismissed a logical argument in your own beliefs because it felt uncomfortable? Are you ready to let clear reasoning challenge what you think you know about the world?
?
If every disparity in speed points to a deeper truth about the structure of creation, what does that tell you about the consistency and wisdom of the Creator? How will you train your mind to see such signs in everyday phenomena?
?
The principle of 'prevention of vacuity' means that truth cannot be avoided: one of two statements must be true. In your own life, are there areas where you try to hold onto both options to avoid a difficult conclusion? What would it cost you to embrace the truth that emerges from this argument?

Causality and Created Things

First Proof: Motion on a Distance of Indivisible Parts
Al-Iji gives the first proof for atomic divisibility using the motion of fast and slow bodies on a distance of indivisible parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for atomic divisibility based on the movement of fast and slow bodies through a distance composed of indivisible parts.

Core Idea
If distance is made of atoms, a slow body must either move an atom (equality) or less than an atom (divisibility).

In this lesson, we examine the first of two proofs Al-Iji provides for atomic divisibility. Assuming the distance is composed of indivisible parts (atoms), he considers a fast body and a slow body moving together. Since slowness is not due to rests, the slow body must move continuously. The only options lead to either equality of speed or division of the atom—both problematic for the atomist.

Guided Path
1
Assume distance of indivisible parts
Start by assuming the distance the bodies travel is made of atoms (indivisible parts). This is the hypothesis to be examined.
2
Observe the fast body cuts a part
When the fast body cuts one such part, the slow body does not stop—just as was established earlier that slowness is not due to interpolation of rests.
3
Consider the slow body's movement
Since the slow body moves, it must move either an entire part or less than a part.
There is no possibility of moving more than a part because the fast only moved one.
4
Draw the consequences
If the slow moves a whole part, then both are equal in speed (the first possibility negated). If it moves less than a part, then that part must be divisible (the second possibility).
Thus, the assumption of indivisible parts leads to a contradiction or to divisibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث : ما يتعلق بالسرعة والبطء وحاصله أحد الأمرين (لازم أي ثابت في الواقع على سبيل منع الخلو ( إما انتفاء تفاوت الحركات بالسرعة والبطء وإما تجزؤ الأجزاء ) التي لا تتجزأ فإنهما لا يجتمعان في الكذب لأن عدم التجزؤ يستلزم انتفاء التفاوت وعدم الانتفاء أعني وجود التفاوت يستلزم المتجزئ ) والأول ) وهو انتفاء تفاوت الحركات ( منتف ( ضرورة أن الحركات متفاوتة في السرعة والبطء ) فثبت الثاني)، وهو تجزؤ الأجزاء (بيان لزوم أحد الأمرين من طريقين: أحدهما أنه إذا تركبت المسافة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ فإذا ( قطع السريع جزءا منها ( فالبطيء لا يقف لما بينا من قبل ( أن البطء ليس لتخلل السكنات فهو ) أي البطء إذن يتحرك فإما أن يتحرك جزءاً أيضاً فالسريع كالبطيء وهو الأول) أعني انتفاء التفاوت فيما بين الحركات ( أو أقل من جزء) إذ لا مجال لتوهم حركته أكثر من جزء ( فيتجزأ ) الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ لثبوت ما هو أقل منه ( وهو الثاني ) من الأمرين اللذين أدعينا لزوم أحدهما وثانيهما) أي ثاني الطرفين المذكورين أن نبين أن ثمة حركة سريعة وبطيئة متلازمتين) بحيث يستحيل انفكاك إحداهما عن الأخرى ( فيستغني) حينئذ ( عن الاستعانة بأن البطء ليس لتخلل السكنات بل يكون ذلك ) أي تلازم هاتين الحركتين ( دليلاً على ذلك ) أي على أن البطء ليس للتخلل ( مستأنفاً ) كما نبهت عليه فيما مر وإذا كانت الحركتان متلازمتين ( فعندما تقطع السريعة جزأين قطعت البطيئة مثلها لزم تساوي السريعة والببطيئة ) وهو الأمر الأول ، ( أو أقل لزم التجزؤ ) وهو الأمر الثاني .

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument on motion and atomic divisibility. It begins by stating the third type (النوع الثالث) concerning speed and slowness. The author presents a dichotomy: either motions are not different in speed (i.e., all are equal) or indivisible atoms are divisible. He notes that these two cannot be false together, because if atoms are indivisible then motions cannot differ in speed (since differing would require dividing the atom), and if they are not equal—i.e., there is disparity—then atoms must be divisible. He asserts that disparity obviously exists, so divisibility is proven. He then gives two proofs (طريقان) to establish that one of the two must hold. The first proof assumes distance is composed of indivisible atoms. When a fast body and a slow body move along such a distance, the slow body cannot stop (since slowness is not due to rests). It must move; either it moves an entire atom—making it equal in speed to the fast one (negating disparity, the first option)—or it moves less than an atom, which divides the atom (establishing divisibility, the second option). The second proof shows there exist two motions that are inseparable (متلازمتين), like those in the subsequent six examples. Misconception & Correction

Checkpoint
In the first proof, what does the author say about the slow body when the fast cuts one part?
A) It stops completely.
B) It does not stop.
C) It moves faster.
D) It reverses direction.

Key Takeaway
If distance were made of atoms, a slow body would have to move less than an atom, thereby making the atom divisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If physical slowness is not caused by rests (as the source states), what about your spiritual slowness? What 'rests' have you allowed to interrupt your journey toward Allah, and how can you eliminate them?
?
This proof forces you to choose: either all speeds are equal (impossible) or atoms are divisible. In your life, when faced with an impossible choice between two false options, do you seek the third path of truth? Or do you settle for a comfortable lie?
?
The argument concludes that the smallest particle must be divisible. How does this remind you that nothing in creation is self-sufficient? When you examine your own heart, what parts of it are you treating as indivisible—too small for Allah’s scrutiny?

Second Proof: Inseparable Motions and Concluding Remarks
Al-Iji gives the second proof using inseparable motions, followed by commentator's clarifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for atomic divisibility using inseparable motions and the concluding clarifications.

Core Idea
Inseparable fast and slow motions prove atoms are divisible.

Al-Iji offers a second proof that does not rely on the earlier claim about slowness not being due to rests. Instead, he points to pairs of motions that are inseparable (mutalāzimatayn). If two such motions exist, then when the fast covers two atoms, the slow must either match (equality) or lag (divisibility). The lesson also includes the commentator's clarifications on the principle of prevention of vacuity and a rebuttal of an objection about meeting in the middle.

Guided Path
1
Understand inseparable motions
Al-Iji says there exist fast and slow motions that cannot be separated; they always occur together. This is a given from the six examples mentioned elsewhere.
2
Apply inseparability to two parts
Consider the fast body moving through two parts. Since the motions are inseparable, the slow body must also move through some distance simultaneously.
3
Consider two possibilities
The slow body either moves the same amount (two parts) or less. If the same, equality (first matter) results. If less, divisibility (second matter) results.
Equality is impossible because we know motions differ, so divisibility follows.
4
Examine the commentator's clarifications
The commentary notes that the principle 'prevention of vacuity' ensures the two outcomes are mutually exclusive and collectively exhaustive. Also, an objection that the bodies might meet in the middle is dismissed by reinterpreting 'stop'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث : ما يتعلق بالسرعة والبطء وحاصله أحد الأمرين (لازم أي ثابت في الواقع على سبيل منع الخلو ( إما انتفاء تفاوت الحركات بالسرعة والبطء وإما تجزؤ الأجزاء ) التي لا تتجزأ فإنهما لا يجتمعان في الكذب لأن عدم التجزؤ يستلزم انتفاء التفاوت وعدم الانتفاء أعني وجود التفاوت يستلزم المتجزئ ) والأول ) وهو انتفاء تفاوت الحركات ( منتف ( ضرورة أن الحركات متفاوتة في السرعة والبطء ) فثبت الثاني)، وهو تجزؤ الأجزاء (بيان لزوم أحد الأمرين من طريقين: أحدهما أنه إذا تركبت المسافة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ فإذا ( قطع السريع جزءا منها ( فالبطيء لا يقف لما بينا من قبل ( أن البطء ليس لتخلل السكنات فهو ) أي البطء إذن يتحرك فإما أن يتحرك جزءاً أيضاً فالسريع كالبطيء وهو الأول) أعني انتفاء التفاوت فيما بين الحركات ( أو أقل من جزء) إذ لا مجال لتوهم حركته أكثر من جزء ( فيتجزأ ) الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ لثبوت ما هو أقل منه ( وهو الثاني ) من الأمرين اللذين أدعينا لزوم أحدهما وثانيهما) أي ثاني الطرفين المذكورين أن نبين أن ثمة حركة سريعة وبطيئة متلازمتين) بحيث يستحيل انفكاك إحداهما عن الأخرى ( فيستغني) حينئذ ( عن الاستعانة بأن البطء ليس لتخلل السكنات بل يكون ذلك ) أي تلازم هاتين الحركتين ( دليلاً على ذلك ) أي على أن البطء ليس للتخلل ( مستأنفاً ) كما نبهت عليه فيما مر وإذا كانت الحركتان متلازمتين ( فعندما تقطع السريعة جزأين قطعت البطيئة مثلها لزم تساوي السريعة والببطيئة ) وهو الأمر الأول ، ( أو أقل لزم التجزؤ ) وهو الأمر الثاني .

The author presents a dichotomy: either motions are not different in speed (i.e., all are equal) or indivisible atoms are divisible. He notes that these two cannot be false together, because if atoms are indivisible then motions cannot differ in speed (since differing would require dividing the atom), and if they are not equal—i.e., there is disparity—then atoms must be divisible. He asserts that disparity obviously exists, so divisibility is proven. He then gives two proofs (طريقان) to establish that one of the two must hold. The first proof assumes distance is composed of indivisible atoms. When a fast body and a slow body move along such a distance, the slow body cannot stop (since slowness is not due to rests). It must move; either it moves an entire atom—making it equal in speed to the fast one (negating disparity, the first option)—or it moves less than an atom, which divides the atom (establishing divisibility, the second option). The second proof shows there exist two motions that are inseparable (متلازمتين), like those in the subsequent six examples. From their inseparability, when the fast one covers two atoms, the slow one must cover at least one atom (equality) or less (divisibility). The commentary adds clarifications: the phrase 'prevention of vacuity' (منع الخلو) means the two possibilities are not both false, and the objection about meeting in the middle is rejected by reinterpretation.

Key Terms
متلازمتين
الاستعانة
منع الخلو
الوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the slow body could stop or that the two bodies could meet at a point in the distance.
Correction: Al-Iji assumes inseparability, so the slow cannot stop independently, and the commentator rejects the idea of a meeting point in the middle.

Checkpoint
What is the key feature of the motions used in the second proof?
A) They are independent.
B) They are inseparable.
C) They are equal in speed.
D) They stop periodically.

Key Takeaway
Inseparable fast and slow motions, when one covers two atoms, force the slow to cover less, thus dividing the atom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof demonstrates that from inseparable fast and slow motions, divisibility must follow. In your relationship with Allah, are you trying to separate the outward fast motions of worship from the inward slow motions of sincerity? Do you treat them as inseparable, or do you allow one to lag behind? Today, commit to aligning your external actions with your internal state.
?
The commentator denies that the two bodies meet in the middle. Is there any 'middle ground' in your faith where you are compromising core beliefs for convenience? Are you hoping that your disobedient actions and your intention to repent will meet somewhere? This lesson reminds us that some things cannot be reconciled. What is one area where you need to stop seeking a middle and make a decisive choice?

The Millstone Example of Inseparable Motions
Al-Iji presents the millstone example as the first case of inseparable motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first example of inseparable motions: the millstone's concentric rings, and why their motions must be tied together or the structure disintegrates.

Core Idea
Two physically attached circles cannot move independently without breaking the millstone.

Al-Iji introduces six forms of inseparable motions. The first involves a millstone with an outer circumferential ring and an inner polar circle. He uses a geometric argument based on lines from the center to the rim, composed of indivisible parts, to show that if one ring moves faster than the other, disintegration follows—even if the millstone were iron. This example supports the mutakallimun's view of atomism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: Al-Iji states the claim, gives the example, explains the geometric proof, and concludes with a remark about rational judgment.
The passage assumes the existence of indivisible parts.
2
Understand the geometric proof
Visualize the lines from the center to the outer rim, composed of indivisible parts, forming rings. If one ring moves and its neighbor does not, the connection breaks, leading to disintegration.
The proof relies on the idea that rings are composed from the ends of these lines.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Al-Iji asserts that while such disintegration is not impossible for God, reason judges it highly improbable in normal experience, similar to other customary impossibilities.
This shows the example is meant to demonstrate logical necessity, not physical occurrence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( وإن كان مما لا يمتنع في قدرة الله تعالى فالعقل حازم بعدمه كسائر العاديات ومعلوم ) لكل عاقل ( أن الله تعالى لم يخلق في الرحى كل هذه الغرائب و العجائب ليثبت مذهبكم . الصورة الثانية فرجار له شعب

قوله : ( فالعقل جازم إلخ ) أصحاب الجزء لا يمنعون الجزم بعدمه بل يقولون إنه مستبعد عادة وإذا ساق البرهان إلى تركب الجسم من الأجزاء فلزوم المستبعدات لا يضره، كما قال الحكماء إن البرهان يتعلق إلى اتصال الجسم في نفسه فلزوم انعدام البحر بشق البعوضة ووجود البحرين الأخيرين المستبعد لا يضره أن ثبوت سكون بين كل حركتين يستلزم وقوف الجبل في الجو لممانعة الخردلة المستبعد ولا ضير في ذلك.
قوله : ( لزم التفكك وانقسام الرحى هاهنا منع مبني على قولهم أن محور الكرة لم يكن متحركاً حين ما يتحرك تلك الكرة على ذلك المحور مع أنه لم يلزم التفكك حينئذ أصلاً فعلى هذا لم لا يجوز أن يتحرك الرحى ولا يتحرك قطبه أصلا ويتحرك الدائرة القطبية منه تارة وتقف أخرى فترى حركتها أبطأ من حركة الدائرة الطوقية؟ ويكون ذلك بواسطة استعدادات شرائط مختلفة ومع ذلك لم يلزم تفكك الرحى أصلاً وهذا ليس بأبعد من القول باستمرار حركة الدحرجة زماناً على نقطة واحدة كما مر وكذا الكلام في سائر الصور الست، فتأمل. وقوله : ولو كانت الرحى
هذا وصل متعلق بقوله : لزم التفكك وقوله : ثم التصادق مرفوع عطفاً على التفكك المذكور. قوله : كل من هذه الغرائب والعجائب ومن الغرائب هو ما أعطي كل من أجزاء الرحى
And that (i.e., the inseparability of the fast and the slow) occurs in six forms. The first: the circumferential circle of the millstone with the polar circle of it, for the motion of the first is fast due to the length of its distance, and the motion of the second is slow due to the shortness of its distance, and they are inseparable. For if the circumferential [circle] moved, for example, and the polar stopped, disintegration would follow, and the millstone would split into multiple circles according to its parts. And this is clarified by drawing contiguous lines from the center of the millstone to its great circumference in all directions. For those lines are composed of indivisible parts, and from the parts of those lines are composed interlocking rings differing in size, the great one of them being composed of the ends of these lines. So if this ring moves and the ring adjacent to it does not move, then one is separated from the other. Similarly, if the second ring moves and the third does not, and so on up to the smallest ring. Hence the millstone disintegrates upon its motion on the model of surrounding circles, even if the millstone were of iron or something stronger. Then its adhesion upon stopping, such that it is impossible for a part to be separated from it with the utmost effort. And that which we have mentioned of the disintegration of the millstone while moving and its adhesion while at rest, even if it is not impossible in the power of God Most High, the intellect is decisive about its non-occurrence like other customary things. And it is known to every rational person that God Most High did not create in the millstone all these strange and wonderful things to establish your school. The second form: a compass with legs...

His saying: (the intellect is decisive etc.) The proponents of indivisible parts do not deny decisiveness about its non-occurrence; rather they say it is customarily far-fetched, and when the demonstration leads to the composition of the body from parts, the necessitation of far-fetched things does not harm it. As the philosophers said: the demonstration pertains to the continuity of the body in itself, so the necessitation of the disappearance of the sea by a mosquito's bite and the existence of the two last seas, which is far-fetched, does not harm it. Similarly, the establishment of a rest between every two motions necessitates the stopping of a mountain in the air due to the resistance of a mustard seed, which is far-fetched, and there is no harm in that.
His saying: (disintegration and splitting of the millstone) Here there is a rebuttal based on their statement that the axis of the sphere was not moving when that sphere moves on that axis, yet no disintegration followed at all. So on this basis, why is it not permissible that the millstone moves and its pole does not move at all, and its polar circle moves sometimes and stops other times, so you see its motion slower than the motion of the circumferential circle? And that occurs by means of different preparations and conditions, and yet no disintegration of the millstone follows at all. This is not more far-fetched than the claim of the continuation of the rolling motion for a time on a single point, as has passed. And similarly the discussion applies to the rest of the six forms, so reflect.
And his saying: 'even if the millstone' — this is a clause connected to his saying: 'disintegration would follow'. And his saying: 'then its adhesion' is nominative, coordinated with the mentioned disintegration.
His saying: 'all these strange and wonderful things' — among the strange things is what is given to each of the parts of the millstone...
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage provides the first of six examples of inseparable motions. The author describes a millstone with two concentric circles: the outer (circumferential) circle and the inner (polar) circle. Because they are physically attached, if one moves and the other does not, the millstone would disintegrate. To prove this, he considers lines drawn from the center to the outer rim. These lines are composed of indivisible atoms, and the rings are formed from the parts of these lines. If a ring moves but its neighbor does not, the connection breaks, leading to disintegration—even if the millstone were iron. While God could create such a phenomenon, reason judges it improbable in the ordinary course of things. The commentary then addresses objections: proponents of indivisible parts argue that such far-fetched consequences do not invalidate a proof, just as philosophers accept other implausible implications. Another objection suggests the millstone might move while its pole (center) remains still, but the author counters that this is not more far-fetched than other accepted possibilities, and thus the example stands. The commentary ends with grammatical notes on the text.

Key Terms
الدائرة الطوقية
الدائرة القطبية
الرحى
التفكك
التلازم
أجزاء لا تتجزأ
العاديات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Different speeds in physically attached objects can occur without any breakage.
Correction: If the objects are physically attached and one moves faster, the slower must also move; otherwise, the attachment would break, as shown by the millstone's rings.

Checkpoint
What does the millstone example aim to prove?
A) That two motions can differ in speed without separation.
B) That two motions are inseparable, so if one moves faster the structure must disintegrate unless the slower also moves.
C) That the center of a millstone does not move.
D) That circular motion is slower than linear motion.

Key Takeaway
The millstone example demonstrates that two inseparable motions must be tied together; otherwise, the structure disintegrates, supporting the mutakallimun's argument for atomism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You walk through your day assuming that objects remain intact, that your car won't fall apart as you drive, that your body parts move together. But what if you truly believed that God could cause the millstone of your routines to disintegrate at any moment—not with effort, but with a single will? Would you still cling to your sense of control? What would you change if you knew that every customary pattern is held together by God's ongoing choice, not by necessity?
?
Al-Iji says that even if disintegration were possible in God's power, reason judges it improbable in the ordinary course. But do you live as if the 'ordinary course' is absolute? When you plan your future, work for your goals, or rely on your health, are you banking on probabilities—or subtly forgetting that the One who sustains them could stop them without warning? Let that thought sit in your heart until it shifts something.
?
The millstone's rings are tied together; if one moves without the other, the whole breaks. What are the 'rings' in your life—your faith, your family, your character, your daily practices—that are inseparable? If you let one ring stop or move out of sync, which other parts of your soul would disintegrate? And are you guarding those connections with the same care that God guards the order of His creation?

Responding to Objections on the Millstone Example
Al-Iji addresses objections to the millstone example from proponents of indivisible parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised by proponents of indivisible parts against the millstone example and Al-Iji's response that far-fetched consequences do not invalidate a sound demonstration.

Core Idea
Far-fetched consequences do not undermine a valid proof.

After presenting the millstone example, Al-Iji addresses an objection from those who believe in indivisible parts. They argue that the disintegration scenario is customarily far-fetched, but they do not deny that it is logically possible. Al-Iji responds by citing philosophical precedents where valid demonstrations lead to improbable conclusions, such as the continuity of motion or rest between motions. This lesson examines that objection and the defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the passage where the objection is stated and Al-Iji responds. Note the structure: first, the objection that the conclusion is far-fetched; then, the response that far-fetchedness does not harm the proof.
The objection comes from proponents of indivisible parts (أصحاب الجزء).
2
Understand the analogy with philosophical examples
Al-Iji compares the situation to examples from the philosophers: the disappearance of the sea from a mosquito's bite, or a mountain suspended in air by a mustard seed. These are far-fetched but follow from valid premises.
This reinforces that implausibility does not invalidate logic.
3
Consider the alternative explanation
Another objection suggests that the millstone's pole might not move while the rings move, yet no disintegration occurs. Al-Iji counters that this is no more far-fetched than the claim that a rolling ball stays on one point for a time.
This shows the example is robust against alternative interpretations.

?

?

?
Al-Iji uses examples like a mosquito causing the sea to vanish—do you rely on 'common sense' more than logical evidence when making decisions about your faith? What would it take for you to hold your convictions based on demonstration rather than custom?

Ibn Sina's Refutation of the Atomistic Objection to Prime Matter
Ibn Sina refutes an objection to prime matter using homogeneous parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the atomistic objection to prime matter and Ibn Sina's refutation based on the homogeneity of parts.

Core Idea
If all parts are identical, each must accept both union and separation.

This passage presents a key challenge to the proof of prime matter: if there are no atoms, why not simply compose bodies from tiny homogeneous parts? Ibn Sina shows that this very homogeneity forces the need for a substrate that receives change.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the opponent's claim: rejecting atoms leaves bodies as compositions of tiny homogeneous parts, making prime matter unnecessary.
The objection is based on the denial of indivisible parts (atoms).
2
Identify Ibn Sina's key premises
He assumes the parts are identical in essence. Then he argues that if they are identical, they must share the same possibilities: each must be capable of both connection and separation.
Otherwise the same essence would have contradictory properties.
3
Trace the conclusion
Since connection and separation are possible, a substrate (matter) that undergoes these changes is required. The only escape is an external obstacle, but that obstacle cannot be essential, so it can be removed.
Thus prime matter is established.

المرصد الأول - المقصد السابع : الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة
انتفاء الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ وما في حكمه يستلزم أن الجسم إما أن يكون متصلا في نفسه فيكون جسماً مفرداً، أو يكون في تركيبه منتهياً إلى أجسام مفردة، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الجسم البسيط الذي نحن بصدده مركباً من أجسام مفردة قابلة للانقسام الوهمي دون الفكي فلا تثبت الهيولى بالبرهان المذكور لابتنائه على أن الجسم المتصل في نفسه يرد عليه الانفصال الخارجي بل ولا يثبت أيضاً الجسم التعليمي، لأن تلك الأجسام المفردة لا تتغير أشكالها ومقاديرها، ( وأبطله ) أي قول هذا القائل ( ابن سينا بما حاصله أن كل جزء منها أي من تلك الأجزاء القابلة للانقسام الوهمي ( تحدث فيه القسمة الوهمية اثنينية يكون طباع كل منهما طباع الآخر) وطباع الجملة وهو ظاهر ( و) طباع الجزء الآخر، (الخارج الموافق لها في الماهية ) بناء على ما ذهب إليه ذلك القائل من أن تلك الأجسام المفردة الصغار متوافقة في الماهية النوعية ( فيجوز) حينئذ ( على ) الجزأين ( المتصلين) المفروضين في جزء واحد (ما يجوز على ( الجزأين ( المنفصلين ) أعني الجزء الذي قسم والجزء الآخر ( من الانفصال ) الرافع للاتحاد والاتصال، (و) يجوز أيضاً ( على المنفصلين ما يجوز على المتصلين من الاتصال الرافع للاثنينية والانفكاكية، وذلك لأن هذه الأربعة متوافقة في الماهية فتكون متشاركة إما في الامتناع عن قبول الانفصال والاتصال أو في جواز قبولهما . والأول باطل قطعا فتعين الثاني، فكل واحد من الأجسام الصغار قابل للاتصال والانفصال ( اللهم إلا لمانع خارج عنه ) وذلك المانع لا يكون لازماً لماهيتي الجسم، وإلا انحصر نوعه في شخصه وإذا لم يكن لازماً، فيمكن مفارقة المانع عن

First Observatory - Seventh Objective: The body is composed of matter and form. The negation of the indivisible part and what is in its ruling entails that the body is either connected in itself and thus a single body, or its composition ends at individual bodies. So why is it not permissible that the simple body we are discussing is composed of individual bodies that are susceptible to imaginary division without the composite? Thus matter is not proven by the mentioned proof because it is based on the fact that the body connected in itself is subject to external disconnection; nor is the mathematical body proven either, because those individual bodies do not have their shapes and sizes changed. (And Ibn Sina refuted it) i.e., the statement of this proponent—(Ibn Sina, with the gist that every part of them, i.e., of those parts susceptible to imaginary division, (the imaginary division produces a duality in it, the nature of each of them being the nature of the other) and the nature of the whole, and that is apparent, (and) the nature of the other part, (the external part corresponding to it in essence), based on what that proponent held that those small individual bodies are in agreement in specific essence. (Then it becomes possible) (upon the two connected parts) assumed in one part (what is possible upon the two separate parts), namely the part that was divided and the other part, (of separation) which removes unity and connection, (and) it is also possible (upon the two separate parts what is possible upon the two connected parts of connection which removes duality and separation). And that is because these four are in agreement in essence, so they share either in the impossibility of accepting separation and connection or in the possibility of accepting them. The first is obviously false, so the second is established. Thus each of the small bodies is susceptible to connection and separation (unless there is an external obstacle preventing it). And that obstacle cannot be necessary for the essence of the body, otherwise its species would be confined to its individual; and when it is not necessary, it is possible to separate the obstacle from it. It is not necessary for its essence, otherwise its species would be confined to its individual; and when it is not necessary, (it is possible to separate it, and when its removal is assumed, it becomes actually susceptible to separation and connection) (and the desired is achieved), which is the proof of matter.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a complete argument: it presents an objection to the proof of prime matter (هيولى) and then gives Ibn Sina's refutation. The objection is that if we deny the indivisible part (atom), then the body could simply be composed of tiny individual bodies that are homogeneous in nature, and thus the proof of matter is unnecessary. Ibn Sina responds by arguing that if these parts are all identical in essence, then each part must be capable of both connection and separation, because otherwise the same nature would necessitate contradictory properties. Since the parts can both connect and separate, a substrate (matter) that receives these changes is required. The only escape is if an external obstacle prevents connection or separation, but that obstacle cannot be essential, otherwise the species would be limited to one individual. Therefore, the obstacle can be removed, and the part would then have the capacity, proving the existence of matter.

Correction: Ibn Sina shows that the very homogeneity of parts forces them to be capable of change, requiring a substrate (prime matter).

According to Ibn Sina's argument, what follows from the assumption that all small parts of a homogeneous body are identical in essence?
A) They must be indivisible.
B) They must be incapable of connection.
C) Each part must be capable of both connection and separation.
D) Matter is unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
The homogeneity of parts does not obviate the need for prime matter; rather, it demands a substrate that can undergo connection and separation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that change (connection/separation) is possible for every part of your being—unless an external obstacle prevents it. What external obstacles are you allowing to block the spiritual transformation your soul was created for? Are you certain those obstacles are truly outside your control, or have you attached yourself to them as if they were essential?
?
Ibn Sina argues that the obstacle to change is not necessary to your nature—it can be removed. What is one thing in your life you have accepted as a permanent barrier to drawing closer to Allah? Will you assume it can be removed and take one concrete step to remove it, trusting that your essence is not bound by it?
?
If your soul, like prime matter, is a pure potential that receives form, then every day you are giving it a form through your choices. What form is your soul taking right now—is it the form of a servant of Allah or the form of a slave to your desires? If you find it distorted, know that the obstacle is not in your essence—so why do you keep it?

Objection on Connection as Part and Rebuttal
Objection and rebuttal on connection/ disconnection as parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that connection and disconnection are accidents, not parts of the body, and the response that the proof of matter only requires a receptive substrate.

Core Idea
Connection need not be a part; a substrate that receives it suffices for proof.

In this passage, Sanusi presents and rebuts an objection to the proof of matter (hyle). The objector argues that connection (unity) and disconnection (multiplicity) are accidents of the body, not constituent parts. The response explains that the proof does not require connection to be a part; it only needs a persistent substrate that can receive both states. We will walk through the objection, its reasoning, and the effective rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Read the first part of the passage where the objector claims that connection and disconnection are accidents. Note the demand: 'So it is upon you to clarify that connection is a part of the body'.
The objector thinks the proof of matter fails if connection is not a part.
2
Identify the objector's reasoning
Understand why the objector says connection and disconnection are accidents: they are unity and multiplicity, which are clearly not substances. Therefore they cannot be parts of the body.
3
Examine the response
The response denies that connection must be a part. Instead, the proof establishes a substrate (matter) that accepts connection and disconnection. The dispute then becomes whether the body is that substrate alone or composite.
The substrate is necessary regardless of connection being accidental.
4
Consider the implications on individuation
The final gloss addresses individuation acting as an obstacle or condition, but this does not undermine the proof. The substrate remains, allowing disjointed division.
This is a subtle point, but the main argument holds.

(And perhaps) one objects to the proof of matter and says: Connection is unity, and disconnection is multiplicity, and they are accidents of the body, external to it. So it is upon you to clarify that connection is a part of the body so that it is proven that it is composed of connection and the receptive thing. His statement: (And it is said: connection etc.) means that the necessary consequence of the proof is the existence of a persistent thing that accepts connection and disconnection. The connection of the body is its non-division into parts in actuality, which is unity, and disconnection is its division into them, which is multiplicity, and they are accidents of the body without doubt and cannot be two parts of it. So you must explain what is meant by connection and then prove that it is a part of the body so that the approach is complete and it is proven that the body is composed of that receptive thing and connection. His statement: (i.e., we deny that it is a part of it) means that the mentioned meaning is an accident, or in another sense the body does not accept it, let alone it being a part of it. His statement: (Perhaps the individuation of one of them is an obstacle) which is the individuation of the singular part at which division ends, and his statement: or the individuation of the other is a condition, which is the individuation of the non-singular part which has not ended its division with a number.

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its rebuttal. The objector argues that connection (unity) and disconnection (multiplicity) are accidents of the body, not parts of it; therefore the proof of matter is invalid unless it is shown that connection is an actual part. The rebuttal, explained in the accompanying glosses, clarifies that the proof does not require connection to be a part; rather, the proof shows the necessity of a persistent substrate that can receive both connection and disconnection. The glosses further explain that even if connection and disconnection are accidents, the substrate remains necessary. The last gloss addresses the possibility that individuation could act as an obstacle or condition, but this does not undermine the proof because the obstacle itself is not essential.

Potentiality, Actuality, and the Refutation of the Hyle Argument
Presents and refutes the argument for hyle from potentiality and actuality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument for hyle based on potentiality and actuality is refuted by noting that a single thing can have both attributes relative to different aspects.

Core Idea
A single substance can be both potential and actual in different respects.

This lesson examines a brief but important objection-and-answer in the debate over hyle (prime matter). The argument claims that a body's combination of potentiality and actuality proves it must be composed of matter and form. Sanusi presents the argument and then refutes it by clarifying that potentiality and actuality can coexist in a simple substance as long as they refer to different things.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument for hyle
Read the first part of the Arabic passage: the claim that body has both potentiality and actuality, and that a simple thing cannot have both. Understand the reasoning behind the claim.
The argument relies on the idea that a simple substance cannot be both potential and actual in the same respect.
2
Study the refutation
Read the refutation: the author argues that it is possible for one thing to be qualified by both potentiality and actuality relative to different things. The impossibility is only relative to the same thing.
The example of hyle itself (which is actual but receptive to forms) illustrates the point.
3
Apply the distinction
Consider the example given: hyle exists in actuality but is in potentiality with respect to forms. This shows that the original argument fails to force composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه ) ( وربما قالوا : ( في إثبات الهيولى (الجسم له قوة وفعل وذلك لأن كل جسم فهو من حيث جسميته موجودة بالفعل ومن حيث أنه مستعد لأعراض كثيرة متصف بالقوة، ( والبسيط لا يكون كذلك) لأن الواحد من حيث هو واحد لا يقتضي قوة وفعلا لامتناع اجتماعهما فيه وهو مردود لجواز أن يتصف الواحد بهما بالنسبة إلى شيئين إنما الممتنع اجتماعهما بالنسبة إلى شيء واحد، ألا ترى أن الهيولى موجودة بالفعل، وقابلة للصور المتعددة، فهي بالقوة في بعضها قطعاً
Notice. And perhaps they say: (in proving hyle) the body has potentiality and actuality, and that is because every body, in terms of its corporeality, exists in actuality, and in terms of its being prepared for many accidents, it is qualified by potentiality. (And the simple is not like that) because the one inasmuch as it is one does not entail potentiality and actuality due to the impossibility of their combination in it. And it is refuted because it is possible for the one to be qualified by both relative to two things; the impossible is only their combination relative to one thing. Do you not see that hyle exists in actuality and is receptive to multiple forms, so it is in potentiality with respect to some of them definitely?
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a brief objection-and-answer (تنبيه) presenting and refuting an argument for hyle based on potentiality (القوة) and actuality (الفعل). The argument states that every body has both potentiality (as it is prepared for accidents) and actuality (as it exists corporeally), and a simple thing cannot have both, so the body must be composed of a material principle (hyle) and a formal principle. The refutation is that a single thing can be qualified by both potentiality and actuality relative to different things—it is only impossible relative to the same thing. The author gives the example of hyle itself: it exists in actuality but is in potentiality with respect to the forms it receives. Thus, the argument does not force composition.

Misconception: A substance cannot simultaneously possess both potentiality and actuality.
Correction: A single substance can possess both as long as they are in different respects (e.g., hyle is actual in existence but potential in receiving forms).

D) Because the body is not simple.

Key Takeaway
The argument for hyle from potentiality and actuality fails because a single thing can possess both attributes in different respects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The author refutes the argument by showing that attributes can be relative. Where in your own life have you falsely concluded that you cannot have two seemingly opposite qualities (e.g., humility and confidence) because you thought they must combine in the same respect? What would change if you applied the lesson's distinction?

The Objection to Hyle and Its Refutation Using the Example of Water
Refutes an objection about hyle and proves its existence via the water example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of water refutes the objection that the substantial form alone can account for conjunction and disjunction, and thereby establishes the existence of hyle as the enduring substratum.

Core Idea
The water example proves that something (hyle) persists through change of individual identity.

In this lesson, we encounter a key proof for hyle (prime matter) in the Islamic philosophical tradition. The author presents an objection claiming that the substantial form suffices, and then refutes it with a concrete example of water being divided. By following the argument, you will see why a substratum distinct from form and individual identity is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection says that the thing receptive to conjunction and disjunction could be the substantial form itself, so hyle is unnecessary. Note how this challenges the need for a separate substratum.
2
Grasp the clarification
The author clarifies that when we say the body is receptive to conjunction, we do not mean one individual receives multiple conjunctions successively; rather, there is a preserver of corporeal essence that remains while individual identities change.
This sets up the need for a persisting subject.
3
Analyze the water example
Water in a jar is one continuous individual; when poured into cups, its identity changes but something remains. The relation of the cups' water to the original water is different from other unrelated waters. This shows a persisting substratum.
The proof is that the relation is not the same as to entirely new water.
4
Derive the conclusion
The continuous substance (the formed body) does not persist; what persists is hyle, which is itself devoid of continuity and multiplicity, allowing it to receive these states. This is the method of disjunction.
Thus, hyle is established as the underlying principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتصل وزواله جاز أن يقال : ذلك القابل لهما هو الصورة الجوهرية فلا يثبت في الجسم جوهر مغاير لها متصف بهما فلا تثبت الهيولى، فما ذكره ليس جواباً للسؤال. ( وجوابه) الحق ( أن قولنا : الجسم قابل للاتصال ليس معناه أن شخصاً من الجسم باقياً على هويته الشخصية الاتصالية ( يتوارد عليه اتصال ( واحد ) تارة واتصالان) آخران تارة أخرى ) فإنه غير معقول كما ترى وكيف يكون الواحد بالشخص واحداً تارة واثنين أخرى؟ بل مرادنا أن ثمة أمراً يستحفظ الماهية الجسمية) دون الهوية الشخصية ( معلوم البقاء في الأحوال الطارئة على الجسم من الاتصال والانفصال المتعاقبين عليه ) وتتوارد عليه الهويات ( الشخصية فتارةً تكون معه هوية واحدة ،اتصالية وتارة هويتان أو أكثر فذلك المستحفظ هو القابل بالحقيقة للاتصال والانفصال، (و) هو مغاير للهويات التي تتجدد بالاتصال والانفصال، فإنا نعلم بالضرورة ( أن الماء الذي في الجرة على تقدير كونه واحداً متصلاً في نفسه إذا جعل في الكيزان فقد زالت هويته الشخصية الاتصالية التي لم يكن فيها مفصل أصلاً ( حتى صار شخص واحد أشخاصاً متعددة ) أي زال شخص كان متصلاً اتصالاً وحدانياً، وحصلت أشخاص هي متصلات متعددة لم تكن موجودة في تلك الهوية الاتصالية على ذلك التقدير. وثمة أمر باق في الحالين هو معروض تارة لاتصال) واحد ) وتارة لاتصالات متعددة و الدليل على أن ثمة أمراً باقياً هو أنه ليس نسبة هذه الأشخاص) التي في الكيزان ) إلى ذلك الشخص الذي كان في الجرة ( كنسبة سائر الأشخاص من مياه لم تكن في تلك الجرة، ولو كان زوال) تلك (الهوية) الشخصية ) لا بزوال جزء وبقاء جزء آخر، بل بانتفاء الأجزاء بالمرة لما كان ( الأمر ( كذلك ) بل كان نسبة هذه الأشخاص كنسبة سائر المياه ولا شك أن الجوهر المتصل الوحداني ليس باقياً، فالباقي جوهر آخر يجب أن لا يكون في نفسه متصلاً ولا منفصلاً ولا واحداً ولا كثيراً كما مر حتى يمكن اتصافه بهذه الأمور كلها فظهر من ذلك أن الجوهر المتصل لو كان قائماً بذاته لكان التفريق إعداماً له بالكلية، وهذا الذي قرره في إثبات الهيولى هو مسلك الانفصال
The continuous [body] and its removal: it may be said: that which is receptive to them [conjunction and disjunction] is the substantial form, so no substance other than it that is qualified by both is established in the body, so hyle is not established. So what he mentioned is not an answer to the question. (And its answer) the truth (that our saying: the body is receptive to conjunction does not mean that a particular body remains in its individual conjunctive identity (that one conjunction and then two conjunctions successively befall it at another time) for that is incomprehensible as you see — how can something one in individual be one at one time and two at another? Rather, we mean that there is something that preserves the corporeal essence) without the individual identity (known to remain in the states that occur to the body from the successive conjunction and disjunction) and the individual identities (successively befall it; sometimes one conjunctive identity is with it, and sometimes two or more. So that preserver is the receptive thing in reality for conjunction and disjunction, (and) it is other than the identities that are renewed by conjunction and disjunction. For we know necessarily (that water which is in the jar, on the supposition that it is one continuous in itself, when it is placed into cups, its individual conjunctive identity in which there was no joint at all has ceased (so that one individual became multiple individuals) — i.e., an individual that was continuously connected with a unitary connection ceased, and individuals that are multiple continuities came to be that were not existing in that conjunctive identity on that supposition. And there is something remaining in both states; it is a substrate sometimes for (one) conjunction and sometimes for multiple conjunctions. And the proof that there is something remaining is that the relation of these individuals (which are in the cups) to that individual which was in the jar is not like the relation of other individuals of water that were not in that jar. And if the cessation of that (individual identity) were not by the cessation of a part and the remaining of another part, but by the complete nonexistence of parts, then (the matter) would be (so), but the relation of these individuals would be like the relation of other waters. And there is no doubt that the unitary continuous substance is not remaining. Therefore, what remains is another substance that must not in itself be continuous nor discontinuous, nor one nor many, as has passed, so that it can be qualified by all these things. From this it appears that if the continuous substance were self-subsistent, then division would be its complete annihilation. This is what he established in proving hyle — this is the method of disjunction.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit proving the existence of hyle (الهيولى). It begins by presenting the objection: if one says that the thing receptive to conjunction and disjunction is the substantial form (الصورة الجوهرية), then no other substance (جوهر) is needed, so hyle is not established. The author states that this is not a valid answer. The true answer follows: when we say the body is receptive to conjunction, we do not mean that the same individual body remains with its individual identity and receives first one conjunction then two; that would be incoherent. Rather, we mean there is a thing that preserves the corporeal essence (الماهية الجسمية) while individual identities come and go. This preserver is what is truly receptive to conjunction and disjunction. The author then provides a concrete proof using water: water in a jar is one continuous individual; when poured into cups, its individual identity disappears, but there remains something that underlies both states. The proof is that these water individuals in the cups are not related to the original water as other unrelated waters would be; something persists. If the original individual were completely annihilated, then the relation would be the same as to any other water, but it is not. Thus, the continuous substance does not remain, but another substance (the hyle) remains, which in itself is neither continuous nor discontinuous, neither one nor many, so that it can receive all these. The conclusion: if the continuous substance were self-subsistent, division would annihilate it entirely. This is the method of disjunction (مسلك الانفصال) for proving hyle.

Key Terms
الهيولى
الصورة الجوهرية
الاتصال
الانفصال
الماهية الجسمية
الهوية الشخصية
الجوهر المتصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the water example shows the same individual water persists; the author clarifies it is a substratum (the hyle) that persists, not the individual identity.
Correction: The water example actually demonstrates that the individual identity changes (the one continuous water becomes many), but something else — the hyle — remains as the substratum underlying these changes.

Checkpoint

A) Water can change its identity entirely, so nothing remains.
B) Something remains through change in conjunction and disjunction, distinct from individual identity.
C) Water is composed of atoms, so hyle is not needed.
D) The substantial form is the only underlying principle.

Key Takeaway
The water example demonstrates that a substratum (hyle) persists through changes in individual identity, proving its existence distinct from substantial form.

The Proof of Hyle through the Path of Separation
Presentation of the argument for hyle via the path of separation (maslak al-infisal).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the path of separation (maslak al-infisal) proves the existence of hyle (matter) as a substrate that persists through change in physical bodies.

Core Idea
Division of a continuous substance would annihilate it, so a substrate must persist.

This lesson presents the first kalam argument for hyle, the path of separation. The argument demonstrates that when a body is divided, the original continuous substance cannot remain; otherwise division would be annihilation. Therefore, there must be an underlying substrate (hyle) that enables change. This argument is foundational for understanding the composite nature of bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the logical flow: composition → analogy → conclusion.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that body is composed of hyle and form, but not by removal of parts. Instead, division eliminates parts completely.
This is the starting point of the argument.
3
Understand the water analogy
The continuous unitary substance (like water) loses its identity when divided; it does not remain. What remains is a different substance.
This analogy illustrates that a self-subsistent continuous substance cannot survive division.
4
Grasp the logical conclusion
If the continuous substance were self-subsisting, division would be complete annihilation. Thus, there must be a substrate (hyle) that persists through change.
The argument concludes that hyle is necessary for composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد السابع: الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة الشخصية لا بزوال جزء وبقاء جزء آخر، بل بانتفاء الأجزاء بالمرة لما كان الأمر كذلك بل كان نسبة هذه الأشخاص كنسبة سائر المياه ولا شك أن الجوهر المتصل الوحداني ليس باقياً، فالباقي جوهر آخر يجب أن لا يكون في نفسه متصلاً ولا منفصلاً ولا واحداً ولا كثيراً كما مر حتى يمكن اتصافه بهذه الأمور كلها فظهر من ذلك أن الجوهر المتصل لو كان قائماً بذاته لكان التفريق إعداماً له بالكلية، وهذا الذي قرره في إثبات الهيولى هو مسلك الانفصال
The First Observatory - The Seventh Objective: The body is composed of hyle and individual form, not by the removal of a part and the remaining of another part, but by the complete elimination of parts. When the matter is such, and the proportion of these individuals is like the proportion of other waters, and there is no doubt that the continuous unitary substance is not remaining, so that which remains is another substance that must not in itself be continuous nor separate nor one nor many, as has passed, so that it can be described by all these matters. Thus it became clear that if the continuous substance were self-subsisting, its division would be complete annihilation. And this which he established in proving hyle is the path of separation.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the existence of hyle (مادة) as a constituent of body, known as the path of separation (مسلك الانفصال). The argument begins by stating that body is composed of hyle and form, but not by the removal of one part and retention of another; rather, by the complete elimination of parts. It compares individual bodies to water: just as water loses its identity when divided, so a continuous unitary substance cannot persist through division. If the continuous substance were self-subsistent, division would annihilate it completely. Therefore, there must be an underlying substrate (hyle) that persists through change. The author concludes that this is the path of separation he has established.

Key Terms
هيولى
صورة
مسلك الانفصال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims body is simply a composite of parts that can be removed and replaced.
Correction: The argument specifically denies this; it says composition is not by removal of one part and retention of another, but by complete elimination of parts, implying a deeper substrate.

Checkpoint
According to the argument from separation (مسلك الانفصال), what happens to a continuous substance if it is divided?
A) It remains unchanged.
B) It becomes two substances.
C) It is completely annihilated.
D) It becomes hyle and form.

Key Takeaway
The path of separation proves that a continuous substance cannot survive division, necessitating an underlying hyle that persists through change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Proof of Hyle through Potentiality and Actuality
Argument for hyle from potentiality and actuality with refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that bodies must be composite because they possess both potentiality and actuality, and how the objection against this is refuted.

Core Idea
Potentiality and actuality can coexist in one substance with respect to different aspects.

This passage presents a key argument for the existence of hyle (prime matter) based on the observation that every body has both potentiality and actuality. The argument contends that a simple entity cannot have both simultaneously, so body must be composed of a potential substrate and an actual principle. The text then addresses and refutes a potential objection, strengthening the case for hyle.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the complete Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument and refutation.
2
Identify the premises
Note the two premises: every body has actuality (as body) and potentiality (to receive accidents).
3
Understand the objection
The objection claims a simple entity cannot have both potentiality and actuality.
4
Follow the refutation
See how the refutation allows combination with respect to different aspects, using hyle as an example.
The refutation is key to defending the existence of hyle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه: وربما قالوا في إثبات الهيولى: الجسم له قوة وفعل وذلك لأن كل جسم فهو من حيث جسميته موجودة بالفعل ومن حيث أنه مستعد لأعراض كثيرة متصف بالقوة، والبسيط لا يكون كذلك لأن الواحد من حيث هو واحد لا يقتضي قوة وفعلا لامتناع اجتماعهما فيه وهو مردود لجواز أن يتصف الواحد بهما بالنسبة إلى شيئين إنما الممتنع اجتماعهما بالنسبة إلى شيء واحد، ألا ترى أن الهيولى موجودة بالفعل، وقابلة للصور المتعددة، فهي بالقوة في بعضها قطعاً
A note: They sometimes say in proving hyle: The body has potentiality and actuality, because every body, by virtue of its corporeality, exists in actuality, and by virtue of its being disposed to many accidents, it is described with potentiality. The simple is not like that, because the one by virtue of its being one does not imply potentiality and actuality due to the impossibility of their combination in it. This is refutable because it is permissible for the one to be described with both in relation to two things; what is impossible is their combination in relation to one thing. Do you not see that hyle exists in actuality and is receptive to multiple forms, so it is in potentiality with respect to some of them, definitely.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents the argument for hyle from potentiality (قوة) and actuality (فعل). The argument states that every body, as a body, is in actuality by virtue of its corporeality, but also has potentiality because it is disposed to receive various accidents. A simple entity cannot have both potentiality and actuality simultaneously, as the one cannot be both in the same respect. Therefore, body must be composite, consisting of a substrate (hyle) that has potentiality and a form that gives actuality. The text then refutes the objection that the simple cannot combine both, by noting that combination is only impossible with respect to the same thing; hyle itself is actual in existence but potential with respect to specific forms.

Key Terms
قوة
فعل
هيولى
بسيط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Potentiality and actuality cannot coexist in the same substance.
Correction: They can coexist with respect to different aspects, as hyle is actual in existence but potential in relation to forms.

Checkpoint
According to the argument from potentiality and actuality, why must a body be composite?
A) Because a simple thing cannot have both potentiality and actuality.
B) Because body is indivisible.
C) Because hyle is formless.
D) Because all bodies are eternal.

Key Takeaway
Bodies are composite because they possess both potentiality and actuality, which a simple entity cannot have in the same respect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life are you currently in a state of pure potentiality (قوة) rather than actuality (فعل)? What is one concrete step you can take today to move towards actualizing that potential, and how does this lesson remind you that potentiality and actuality can coexist in you without contradiction?
?
Think about a time when you assumed you could not hold two seemingly opposite qualities in yourself. How does the lesson's refutation—that the one can be described with both in relation to two things—challenge that assumption? What would change if you embraced that you can be both weak in one area and strong in another?
?
Like hyle, you are actual in your existence but potential for new forms of character, knowledge, and faith. What is one specific new form—a virtue, a skill, or a belief—that you have been resisting receiving? What would it cost you to receive it, and what would it cost you not to? Commit now to one action that opens you to that form.

The Proof of Hyle from Rarefaction and Condensation
Argument for hyle from rarefaction and condensation and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument for hyle based on rarefaction and condensation and the response that the corporeal form can account for changes in magnitude.

Core Idea
Rarefaction and condensation do not necessitate hyle; the corporeal form suffices.

This passage presents a common kalam argument for the existence of hyle (material substrate) using the phenomena of rarefaction and condensation. It then provides a response that refutes the need for hyle by appealing to the inherent plasticity of the corporeal form.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the main ideas.
2
Identify the argument for hyle
Notice how the argument uses real rarefaction and condensation to posit an unquantified substrate (hyle) that accepts different magnitudes.
The argument is presented as a conditional statement.
3
Examine the refutation
Understand the response: the corporeal form, though always associated with magnitude, is not tied to a specific magnitude, so it can receive different magnitudes without hyle.
This refutation undercuts the need for a separate substrate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما استعانوا في إثبات الهيولى بالتخلخل والتكاثف الحقيقيين، فإنه إذا لم يكن في الجسم أمر غير متقدر بذاته حتى يتصور قبوله للمقادير المختلفة امتنع ازدياد حجمه وانتقاصه من غير انضمار شيء إليه وانفصاله عنه وجوابه أن الصورة الجسمية، وإن كانت مستلزمة في الوجود والتعقل للمقدار إلا أنها لا تستلزم مقداراً مخصوصاً فجاز أن تكون هي قابلة لتلك المقادير المختلفة فلا يثبت وجود أمر آخر
And they sometimes seek help in proving hyle from real rarefaction and condensation. For if there were not in the body a thing not intrinsically quantified so that it could accept different magnitudes, its increase in volume and decrease would be impossible without something being assimilated to it or separated from it. The answer is that the corporeal form, although it is concomitant in existence and conception with magnitude, does not necessitate a particular magnitude; thus it is permissible that it itself be receptive to those different magnitudes, so the existence of another thing is not proven.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents the argument for hyle from the phenomena of rarefaction (التخلخل) and condensation (التكاثف). The argument states that if a body did not have a component that is not intrinsically quantified (غير متقدر بذاته), it could not accept different magnitudes during expansion or contraction without adding or removing matter. The answer (جوابه) is that the corporeal form itself, while necessarily associated with magnitude in existence and thought, does not require a specific magnitude. Therefore, it is possible for the corporeal form to receive different magnitudes, and thus no additional substrate (hyle) is needed. This effectively refutes the argument for hyle.

Key Terms
تخلخل
تكاثف
هيولى
صورة جسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rarefaction and condensation necessarily imply the existence of hyle.
Correction: The answer demonstrates that the corporeal form itself can account for different magnitudes, so hyle is not required.

Checkpoint
What is the response to the argument from rarefaction and condensation?
A) The corporeal form does not require a specific magnitude.
B) Hyle is the only substrate capable of change.
C) Rarefaction and condensation do not occur.
D) The body must have a separate substrate.

Key Takeaway
Rarefaction and condensation do not prove the existence of hyle, because the corporeal form can accommodate changes in magnitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a physical change in your life—like growth, aging, or a change in circumstances—requires some hidden, underlying substance to explain it? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what does it teach you about trusting Allah's creation as sufficient for the changes He ordains? Will you now look at changes in your body and world as signs of Allah's wisdom rather than puzzles needing extra causes?
?
This passage shows that we often overcomplicate explanations by adding unnecessary entities (like hyle) when the simpler truth (corporeal form) suffices. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes multiply worries, justifications, or rituals thinking they are needed, when Allah has already made the basic acts of worship and faith sufficient? Name one area where you can simplify your practice based on the principle that what Allah has created is enough, and commit to it this week.

The Proof of Hyle from Generation and Corruption
Argument for hyle from generation/corruption and its circularity refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument for hyle from generation and corruption and why it is considered circular.

Core Idea
The argument for hyle from change is circular because it already assumes hyle.

This passage tackles whether the observable processes of generation (coming into being) and corruption (perishing) require a permanent substrate called hyle. The author presents the argument and then refutes it by pointing out a circularity (دور). Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: first the argument for hyle, then the refutation.
The refutation contains two parts: an alternative explanation and a charge of circularity.
2
Examine the argument
Identify the reasoning: generation and corruption require a thing that removes one form and puts on another – that thing is hyle. Ask: is this necessary?
3
Understand the refutation
The author gives two points: first, what changes are the specific forms, and corporeal form could be the receiver; second, using these processes to prove hyle is circular because they already depend on hyle.
Circularity (دور) means the proof assumes what it tries to prove.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكون والفساد: أي وربما استعانوا بهما أيضاً إذ لا بد فيهما من أمر يخلع صورة ويلبس أخرى وهو الهيولى وفساده ظاهر لأن المتبدل في الكون والفساد هو الصور النوعية فجاز أن يكون القابل لها خلعاً ولبساً هو الصورة الجسمية على أنا نقول: وجود هذه الأمور التي استعين بها مبني على وجود الهيولى فيلزم الدور.
And generation and corruption: They may also seek help from them, for in them there must be a thing that removes a form and puts on another, which is hyle. Its refutation is clear, because what changes in generation and corruption is the specific forms, so it is permissible that the receiver for them in removal and putting on is the corporeal form. Moreover, we say: the existence of these matters that are used as evidence is based on the existence of hyle, so circularity (دور) follows.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents the argument for hyle from generation (كون) and corruption (فساد). The argument claims that when a substance is generated or corrupted, there must be a substrate that loses one form and acquires another, and that substrate is hyle. The refutation (فساده ظاهر) states that what actually changes are the specific forms, and the corporeal form itself could serve as the receiver, losing and gaining specific forms. Furthermore, the arguer objects that using these phenomena to prove hyle is circular (دور) because the existence of these processes already presupposes hyle.

Key Terms
كون
فساد
هيولى
صورة نوعية
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Generation and corruption necessarily prove the existence of hyle.
Correction: The text shows that these processes can be explained by the corporeal form alone, and the argument is circular because it assumes hyle.

Checkpoint
What is the circularity (دور) mentioned in the argument?
A) The proof of hyle relies on the existence of hyle itself.
B) Generation and corruption are the same.
C) Corporeal form cannot change.
D) The argument assumes what it tries to prove.

Key Takeaway
The argument for hyle from generation and corruption is refuted by pointing out the circularity and the alternative role of corporeal form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an argument that seems to prove something, do you ever pause to check whether the argument already assumes what it is trying to prove? Where in your own reasoning about matters of faith or life might a hidden circularity be lurking?
?
The lesson shows that change can be explained without a separate substrate. Think of a personal change you have experienced or are resisting. What is the 'substrate' you assume must stay constant? Is that assumption justified, or could the change be more fundamental?
?
If the argument for hyle is circular, what does that say about how you accept claims from authority or tradition? Are there beliefs you hold that you have never really examined because the evidence for them already presupposes them? Commit here to examine one such belief today.
