The Theologians' Refutation of Hyle (Matter)
Theologians refute hyle by a dichotomy of space occupancy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theologians' dichotomous argument that hyle reduces to either a body or an accident, thus refuting its existence as a separate principle.

Core Idea
Hyle cannot exist because it would have to be either a body or an accident, both impossible.

The theologians (المتكلمون) developed a crisp logical refutation of the concept of hyle (prime matter). They argued by dividing possibilities: if hyle exists, it must either occupy space or not. Each branch leads to a contradiction, showing that hyle is not a needed principle. This passage presents the first branch of that dichotomy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and its translation once to grasp the structure. Notice that this is a refutation based on a dichotomy.
The text cuts off mid-sentence on the second branch; focus on the first branch.
2
Identify the dichotomy
Find the key division: 'either it has occupancy of space or it does not'. This is the starting point of the argument.
3
Analyze the first branch
If hyle occupies space, the passage further splits into independent or dependent occupancy. Work through each sub-branch and its consequences (body, regress).
Pay attention to the terms 'استقلال' (independence) and 'تسلسل' (infinite regress).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتمد عند المتكلمين في نفي الهيولى أنها على تقدير وجودها إما أن يكون لها حصول الحيز أو لا يكون. فإن كان لها حصول فيه فإما أن يكون ذلك الحصول على سبيل الاستقلال فجسم أي فالهيولى جسم لأن المتحيز بالذات لا بد أن يكون جوهراً ممتداً في الجهات ولا معنى للجسم إلا ذلك. وأيضاً فالصورة الجسمية حينئذ مثل لها، فكيف تحل فيها؟ وأيضاً إن احتاجت الهيولى إلى محل لزم التسلسل، وإلا كانت الجسمية مستغنية عن المحل لأنها مثلها. أو لا يكون ذلك الحصول
And what is relied upon by the theologians in negating hyle is that, on the assumption of its existence, either it has occupancy of space or it does not. If it has occupancy in it, then either that occupancy is independent, so it is a body, i.e. hyle is a body, because that which is intrinsically spatial must be a substance extended in dimensions, and the meaning of body is nothing but that. Moreover, then the corporeal form would be similar to it; so how can it inhere in it? Also, if hyle needs a substrate, circularity (تسلسل) follows; otherwise, corporeality would be independent of a substrate because it is similar to it. Or it does not have that occupancy.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents the theologians' (المتكلمين) refutation of hyle. They argue by dichotomy: if hyle exists, it must either occupy space (حصول الحيز) or not. If it does occupy space, then either it occupies independently – in which case it is a body, since anything that occupies space intrinsically is a substance extended in dimensions, which is the definition of body. If hyle were a body, then the corporeal form would be similar to it (both extended), making inherence impossible. Additionally, if hyle requires a further substrate, an infinite regress (تسلسل) results; if it does not, then corporeality itself would be independent of a substrate. The second branch (if it does not occupy space) is mentioned but not elaborated; it would imply hyle is an accident, which cannot serve as a substrate. Thus hyle is not a separate principle.

Key Terms

هيولى
حيز
استقلال
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The theologians of Islam accepted the existence of hyle (prime matter) as a basic principle.
Correction: The theologians argued that hyle cannot exist because if it did, it would have to be either a body or an accident, both of which are untenable.

Checkpoint
According to the theologians, if hyle occupies space independently, what is it?
A) An accident.
B) A body.
C) A simple substance.
D) A form.

Key Takeaway
The theologians refute hyle by showing that any assumption of its existence leads to contradictions, proving it is not a necessary principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If hyle cannot be, then every body and accident is created. Consider one object in your room: can you now see it as a sign of the Creator's direct action? What would change if you truly lived by that?
?
The theologians refute hyle by pure logic. Are you willing to subject your own beliefs about the material world to the same rigorous questioning? What is one assumption you hold that you have never critically examined?

Proof That Continuous Extension Needs Matter and the Response
Sanusi proves the need for matter from the impossibility of self-subsistence of continuous extension, then responds by denying the unity of bodily nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that continuous extension requires matter, and how the opponent denies the unity of bodily nature to refute the proof.

Core Idea
Continuous extension cannot be self-subsistent—it needs matter; but is bodily nature truly a single reality?

This passage presents a core argument in Islamic atomistic cosmology: the continuous extension (the bodily form) must inhere in matter because it cannot exist independently. After establishing this, the text anticipates an objection that denies the unity of the bodily nature, claiming it is a genus that can vary. Understanding this dialectic sharpens your grasp of how kalam philosophers defend the matter-form relationship.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if it were self-subsistent, then...'
The proof uses a reductio argument: if continuous extension were self-subsistent in any body, it would be independent and cannot inhere.
2
Identify the general principle
Sanusi states a general rule: a single specific reality has uniform necessary concomitants; it cannot be self-subsistent at one time and in a substrate at another.
This principle is key to the proof—it shows that the continuous extension, as a single reality, cannot vary in its relationship to a substrate.
3
Understand the response
The response denies the premise that the bodily nature is a single specific reality. Instead, it is an ambiguous universal like quantity, diversified by differentiae or external matters.
This response undermines the proof by showing the bodily nature can have different requirements in different contexts.
4
Reflect on the dialectic
Consider the strength of each side. Does the response successfully block the proof? What assumptions about reality are at stake?
This exchange illustrates the use of general principles and genus-species distinctions in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاتصال الذي هو الصورة الجسمية ( إلى المادة ) في الأجسام العنصرية لكونه حالاً فيها ( امتنع قيامه بنفسه في شيء من الأجسام ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يمتنع قيامه بنفسه بل قام بذاته في الفلك مثلاً ( كان ) ذلك الاتصال الجوهري (في حد ذاته غنياً عن المحل والغنى عن المحل لا يحل فيه ) أصلاً . ( وبالجملة فالحقيقة الواحدة ) النوعية لا تختلف (لوازمها) ومقتضياتها ( فتكون ( بالنصب على أنه جواب النفي (قائمة بذاتها تارة، وبالغير أخرى كما لا تكون جوهراً مرة وعرضاً أخرى ) أي كما أن انقلاب الحقائق محال كذلك اختلاف لوازم حقيقة واحدة محال لاستلزامه أن لا تكون تلك الحقيقة تلك الحقيقة بل حقيقة أخرى ) والجواب منع اتحاد الاتصال الجسمي ) ، أي لا نسلم أن الطبيعة الجسمية طبيعة واحدة نوعية ) وذلك مما لا سبيل إلى إثباته ) فإن ما ذكرتموه من اختلافها بالأمور الخارجية عنها مسلم لكن انحصار اختلافها فيه ممنوع فإن الطبيعة الجسمية مطلقاً أمر مبهم كالمقدار، فلا يتصور وجودها إلا بأن يتنوع بفصول مقومة لها، أو بعد تنوعها ينضم إليها أمور خارجة عنها فلم قلتم : إنها ليست كذلك ؟
The continuous extension which is the bodily form (to matter) in elemental bodies, because it is an accident in them, (it is impossible for it to be self-subsistent in any body. Otherwise) i.e., if it were not impossible for it to be self-subsistent but rather it subsisted by itself in the celestial sphere, for example, (then) that substantial continuity (would be in itself independent of a substrate, and what is independent of a substrate cannot reside in it.) (In summary, a single specific reality) does not differ in (its necessary concomitants) and requirements, (so it would be) (in the accusative as a response to the negation) (sometimes self-subsistent and sometimes in another, just as it is not a substance at one time and an accident at another.) i.e., just as the transformation of realities is impossible, so too the difference of the concomitants of a single reality is impossible, because it would necessitate that that reality is not that reality but another reality. (The answer is the denial of the unity of the bodily continuity), i.e., we do not concede that the bodily nature is a single specific nature. And that is impossible to prove. For what you mentioned of its difference by external matters is conceded, but the restriction of its difference to them is denied. Indeed, the bodily nature absolutely is an ambiguous thing like quantity; it cannot be conceived to exist except by being diversified by constitutive differentiae, or after its diversification, external matters are joined to it. So why do you say it is not so?
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-response structure. The author begins by stating the impossibility of the continuous extension (the bodily form) being self-subsistent in any body, because if it were, it would be independent of a substrate, and what is independent cannot reside in anything. Then a general rule is given: a single specific reality has uniform necessary concomitants and requirements; it cannot be sometimes self-subsistent and sometimes in another, just as a substance cannot be an accident. The conclusion is that the continuous extension must depend on matter. The response then denies the premise that the bodily nature is a single specific reality. Instead, bodily nature is an ambiguous universal like quantity, which only exists when diversified by differentiae or by external accidents. Therefore, it can have varying relationships with matter, so the proof fails.

اللوازم
الطبيعة الجسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the denial of the unity of bodily nature is a weak evasion, but it is grounded in the view that bodily nature is a genus, not a species.
Correction: The response is philosophically robust: it points out that 'bodily nature' is an ambiguous term that only exists concretely when specified by differentiae, so its relation to matter can vary.

C) It would be both substance and accident simultaneously.
D) It would be identical to the celestial sphere.

Key Takeaway
The proof fails if bodily nature is not a single specific reality but an ambiguous genus that can have different concomitants.

?

No Intermediate Between Need and Self-Sufficiency
Sanusi presents and refutes two objections using the principle of no intermediate between need and self-sufficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle of no intermediate between essential need and self-sufficiency is used to refute objections about bodily substances, and why generic natures can have varying concomitants while specific natures cannot.

Core Idea
Essential need and self-sufficiency leave no middle ground.

Sanusi now addresses two objections that challenge the conclusion that the continuous extension cannot be a substance. The first objection suggests it could be both in matter and self-subsistent at different times. The second objection points to generic natures like animality that have different requirements in different species. Sanusi's responses rely on the logical impossibility of intermediate states and the nature of generic versus specific natures.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the argument's flow come through before interpreting.
The passage contains two separate objections; notice how each is introduced.
2
Identify the two objections and their answers
The first objection is about the continuous extension sometimes needing matter and sometimes not. The second objection is about generic natures having different concomitants. Find how Sanusi refutes each.
The refutation of the first uses the principle of no intermediate; the second uses the distinction between generic and specific natures.
3
Understand the principle of no intermediate
Sanusi states that there is no intermediate between essential need and essential self-sufficiency. Ask yourself why this principle is so powerful in kalam arguments.
This principle eliminates the possibility of a thing being neither needy nor self-sufficient in its essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يقوم بالمادة تارة ويقوم بنفسه أخرى ولا محذور في ذلك وقد ( لا يكون الشيء محتاجاً لذاته ) إلى محل ) ولا غنياً لذاته ) عنه ( بل يعرض كل منهما له عن علة ) فلا يلزم أن يكون الغني بذاته عن شيء حالاً فيه ويمكن أن يدفع هذا بأنه لا واسطة بين الحاجة والغني الذاتيين، فإن الشيء إما أن يكون لذاته محتاجا إلى محل أو لا ، وإذا لم يكن محتاجاً إليه لذاته كان مستغنياً عنه في حد ذاته إذ لا معنى للغنى سوى عدم الحاجة، والمستغني في حد ذاته عن محل يستحيل حلوله فيه . ( وأما النقض بالطبيعة الجنسية ) بأن يقال : الحيوانية مثلا طبيعة واحدة مع أن لوازمها ومقتضاتها مختلفة، فقد تقتضي في الإنسان ما لا تقتضيه في الفرس. فقد عرفت جوابه ) حيث نبهناك على أن الجنس أمر مبهم لا يدخل في الوجود إلا بعد تحصله يفصل بعينه، وهما متحدان بحسب الخارج في الجعل والوجود فالطبيعة الجنسية في الخارج حقيقة مختلفة بحسب فصولها المنوعة فجاز اختلافها في الاقتضاء واللوازم بخلاف الطبيعة النوعية فإنها حقيقة متحصلة لا يتصور اختلاف لوازمها .
That it subsists in matter sometimes and by itself at other times, and there is no harm in that, and (a thing may not be essentially needy) of a substrate nor (essentially self-sufficient) of it, (rather each of them may accrue to it from a cause.) So it does not follow that what is essentially self-sufficient of something is an accident in it. And this can be refuted by saying that there is no intermediate between essential need and essential self-sufficiency. For a thing is either essentially in need of a substrate or not. And if it is not in need of it essentially, then it is self-sufficient of it in itself, for there is no meaning of self-sufficiency other than absence of need. And what is self-sufficient in itself of a substrate cannot be an accident in it. (As for the objection from generic nature) by saying: for example, animality is a single nature while its concomitants and requirements differ, for it may require in a human what it does not require in a horse. (You have known its answer) where we alerted you that the genus is an ambiguous thing that enters existence only after being specified by a differentia, and they are united externally in making and existence. So the generic nature externally is a different reality according to its diversifying differentiae. So its difference in requirement and concomitants is permissible, unlike the specific nature, for it is a determinate reality whose concomitants cannot be conceived to differ.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector says the continuous extension could sometimes be in matter and sometimes self-subsistent, just as a thing can be neither essentially needy nor essentially self-sufficient but acquire either from a cause. The answer asserts that there is no intermediate between essential need and essential self-sufficiency. If something is not essentially needy, it is essentially self-sufficient, and what is essentially self-sufficient cannot reside in anything. Then a second objection is raised: generic natures like animality have different concomitants in different species, so why can't bodily nature differ? The answer is that generic natures are ambiguous and only exist when specified by differentiae; in the external world, they are multiple realities. Specific natures, however, are determinate and cannot have differing concomitants.

Key Terms
الحاجة الذاتية
الغنى الذاتي
الطبيعة الجنسية
الطبيعة النوعية
فصل
جنس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Something can be neither essentially needy nor essentially self-sufficient, but acquire either from a cause.
Correction: Sanusi argues that essential need and self-sufficiency are complementary: if a thing is not essentially needy, it is essentially self-sufficient. There is no middle ground.

Checkpoint
What is the principle used to refute the objection that the continuous extension could be both in matter and self-subsistent?
A) The impossibility of a thing having two different natures.
B) There is no intermediate between essential need and essential self-sufficiency.
C) Generic natures cannot have varying concomitants.
D) The continuous extension is a specific nature.

Key Takeaway
The principle of no intermediate between essential need and self-sufficiency is a powerful logical tool in kalam for determining the nature of substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The author teaches that essential need and self-sufficiency leave no room for compromise. Where in your spiritual life have you tried to carve out a neutral zone—a place where you neither submit to God's command nor completely rebel—assuming the two can coexist?
?
If there is no intermediate between need and self-sufficiency, then every thought, action, and desire either reflects your need for God or a false sense of independence. What would you have to change today to live consistently in the recognition of your total need for Him?

Hyle Never Exists Without Form: First Proof
Sanusi states the second sub-branch and provides the first proof that hyle never exists without form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second sub-branch regarding hyle and the first proof that hyle never exists without form, including the argument that hyle abstracted is an ambiguous genus requiring a differentia.

Core Idea
Hyle never exists without form; it is an ambiguous genus needing differentia.

This lesson delves into the second branched issue of hyle, establishing that hyle cannot be found without form. The first proof argues that hyle considered abstractly is only in the intellect, as a substance receptive of dimensions requiring a differentia. We'll also examine objections regarding self-sufficiency and indwelling, and clarify the nature of hyle's dependence on form.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: a claim, a proof, an objection, and clarifications.
The passage is dense; focus on the logical flow.
2
Identify the core claim
The second sub-branch states that hyle is never free from form. Understand what 'absolutely' means: neither before nor after corporeality.
3
Analyze the first proof
The proof: hyle abstracted by assumption from form is only in the intellect; it is a substance receptive of three dimensions, an ambiguous genus needing a differentia (bodily form). This shows hyle cannot exist without form.
4
Examine the objection and response
The objection concerns a self-sufficient thing residing in a substrate. The response clarifies that impossibility is with respect to essence, and discusses the intermediate between essential need and self-sufficiency.
This part is subtle; reflect on the definitions of need and self-sufficiency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ثانيها : ( أي ثاني تفريعات الهيولى ) أن الهيولى لا تخلو عن الصورة ) أي لا توجد خالية عن الصورة الجسمية مطلقاً وذلك لوجوه : الأول : الهيولى المجردة بالفرض عن الصورة (إما في العقل . أعني الجوهر القابل للأبعاد الثلاثة جنس مبهم يحتاج إلى انضمام فصل ينوعه، لكن الصورة الجسمية التي كالمادة لا التي كما نص عليه في الشفاء. قوله: (يستحيل حلوله فيه أي بالنظر إلى ذاته فلا يرد أنه في حد ذاته يجوز أن يحل لعارض إنما المستحيل حلول الأمر الذي يقتضي ذاته ،الفناء وما قيل إنه إذا كان في حد ذاته ذاته إذ الفرض أنه مستغن في حد ذاته ففيه أن الاستغناء من وهي مستغنياً، فلا بد لاستغنائه علة لكونه عدمياً يكفيه عدم علة الاحتياج. أيضاً. قوله : ( إن الهيولى) أي هيولى الأجسام نص عليه في الشفاء وسيجيء في كلام الشارح قوله : ( مطلقاً) أي لا قبل حلول الجسمية ولا بعدها فإن قيل: بعد ما ثبت أن الهيولى في نفسها لا واحدة ولا كثيرة ولا متصلة ولا منفصلة كل ذلك بواسطة الجسمية ظهر امتناع قوله : ( بأنه لا واسطة بين الحاجة والغنى (الذاتيين ولعل المصنف أراد بكون الشيء محتاجاً لذاته إلى المحل أن يكون ذلك الشيء مقتضياً لذلك المحل وأراد بكون الشيء غنياً لذاته عن المحل أن يكون هو لذاته مقتضياً لعدم الحلول في ذلك المحل، فحينئذ يتصور أن يكون بين الاحتياج والغنى واسطة فقوله : والمستغني في حد ذاته عن محل يستحيل حلوله فيه ممنوع أيضاً في الواسطة التي لم تكن مقتضية لذاتها الحلول ولا عدم الحلول في المحل، وإلى القول بأن الحلول يقتضي الاحتياج الذاتي فممنوع أيضاً، تدبر.
(The second one) i.e., the second subdivision of hyle, is that hyle is never free from form, i.e., it does not exist free from bodily form absolutely. And that is through proofs. The first: hyle, abstracted by assumption from form, (is either in the intellect. I mean the substance receptive of three dimensions is an ambiguous genus needing the addition of a differentia to specify it, but the bodily form which is like matter, not the one as stated in the Shifa.) His statement: (it is impossible for it to reside in it) i.e., with respect to its essence, so it is not countered that in itself it may be possible to reside for an accident. What is impossible is the residence of that which essentially necessitates its essence ... annihilation ... And what is said that if it is in itself its essence, since it is supposed to be self-sufficient in essence, then there is in it that self-sufficiency from ... and it is self-sufficient, so its self-sufficiency needs a cause because it is privative, and the absence of the cause of need suffices. Also. His statement: (Indeed hyle) i.e., hyle of bodies stated in al-Shifa and will come in the commentator's speech. His statement: (absolutely) i.e., neither before the indwelling of corporeality nor after it. So if it is said: after it is established that hyle in itself is neither one nor many, nor continuous nor discrete, all that by means of corporeality, then the impossibility appears of his statement: (that there is no intermediate between essential need and self-sufficiency). Perhaps the author meant by 'a thing being essentially needy of a substrate' that the thing necessitates that substrate, and by 'a thing being essentially self-sufficient of a substrate' that it is in itself necessitating lack of indwelling in that substrate. Then it is conceivable that there is an intermediate between need and self-sufficiency. So his statement: 'what is self-sufficient in itself of a substrate cannot be an accident in it' is also denied in the case of the intermediate which does not necessitate in itself indwelling nor non-indwelling in the substrate. And if it is said that indwelling necessitates essential need, it is also denied. Reflect.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage introduces the second sub-branch regarding hyle: that it never exists without form. The first proof states that hyle considered abstractly from form is only in the intellect; it is a substance receptive of three dimensions, an ambiguous genus that requires a differentia. The commentator then discusses an objection about the impossibility of a self-sufficient thing residing in a substrate, clarifying that the impossibility is with respect to the thing's essence. Further comments address the idea that hyle in itself is indeterminate and only takes on properties through corporeality. The text also revisits the earlier issue of the intermediate between need and self-sufficiency, suggesting that if 'need' and 'self-sufficiency' are defined as essential properties, an intermediate is possible where the thing does not essentially necessitate either. However, this is a subtle point.

Correction: The text asserts hyle never exists without form; even its consideration is only in the mind.

Hyle never exists without form; it is only conceptually abstracted but always accompanied by bodily form.

Hyle Is Never Free from Form – First Argument
First rational proof that hyle never lacks bodily form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational proof that prime matter (hyle) always possesses bodily form, based on the impossibility of hyle existing either as an extended substance or as a formless substrate.

Core Idea
Hyle cannot exist without bodily form; any attempt leads to logical contradiction.

This lesson presents the first of several arguments that hyle must always be accompanied by bodily form. The author uses a disjunctive argument: if hyle has sensory indication, it falls into one of three categories, each of which contradicts its being a substrate; if it lacks indication, the occurrence of form in it leads to impossibility. The argument forces the conclusion that hyle is never free from form.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: it presents two major cases based on whether hyle has sensory indication or not.
2
Analyze the case of hyle with indication
Identify the three subcases (body, atom, line/surface) and understand why each leads to a contradiction with hyle being a substrate for form.
The passage argues that if hyle has indication, it must either be a body (itself a form), an indivisible atom (impossible), or an accident inhering in a body—none of which allows it to be a substrate.
3
Analyze the case of hyle without indication
Examine the three possibilities for form occurrence (in all, none, some) and why the first two are impossible and the third lacks a specifier.
The conclusion is that all possibilities are invalid, so the initial assumption that hyle can be without form is false.

تعليمياً أو غيرها لامتناع الجوهر الفرد وما في حكمه، فلا يكون جوهراً فرداً ولا خطاً ولا سطحاً ولا أمراً حالاً في أحدها، وهذا على تقدير الأغمض عن جوهريته، فالواجب الاكتفاء على كونها جسماً، وأما ما ذكره الشارح ففيه اختلال، لأنه إن قيد الإشارة الحسية بالاستقلال لا يصح قوله: لا جوهرياً، بل عرضياً، وإن لم يقيد لم يصح كانت جسماً أي صورة جسيمة لجواز أن يكون جسماً تعليمياً. قوله : (بل (عرضياً الظاهر أنه إضراب عن قوله: جوهرياً أي بل خطاً أو سطحاً عرضياً، وفيه أنه يجوز أن يكون نقطة. فلا بد من التعرض له إلا أن يقال: بل أمراً عرضياً فيشمل النقطة أيضاً بل هاهنا، فإن الإضراب عن باطل إلى باطل لا معنى له. والصواب أن يقال: وما لم ينقسم أصلاً أو انقسمت في جهة أو في جهتين، كانت نقطة أو سطحاً لامتناع الجوهر الفرد وما في حكمه. قوله: (فتكون الهيولى حينئذ أمراً حالاً إلخ) أي صورة جسمية نبه بذلك لأنه اللازم من Second of them: (i.e., second of the divisions of hyle) that hyle is never free from form, i.e., it does not exist devoid of bodily form absolutely, and that is for reasons. First: hyle isolated by assumption from form (is either that which has indication or not; if it has indication, then hyle would then be a body or something inhering in a body, due to the impossibility of the indivisible atom) because if it has position i.e., is capable of sensory indication, then if it divides in directions, it is a body, i.e., a bodily form because it is the body in initial view as previously; and if it does not divide at all, it is an indivisible atom; and if it divides in one direction or two only, it is a line or a surface not substantial because they are in the ruling of the indivisible atom as you know, rather accidental; then hyle would be something inhering in the body not a substrate for the bodily form—this is a contradiction. (Otherwise) i.e., if there is no indication to it such that it is not extended primarily nor secondarily, and there is no doubt that it is receptive of bodily form since the discussion is about hyle of bodies, (then when the bodily form occurs in it, it either) occurs with it (in all places and manifestations, or does not occur in any of them, or occurs in some) not in others, and the (three) divisions are invalid; the first two are invalid (necessarily) because hyle joined to bodyiness inheres in it, its existence without form is impossible because it is impossible for a thing to exist that is neither one nor many. I said: you have known that what is negated from it before form is the continuous unity and the separable multiplicity; as for its unity in itself, it is established for it in all states. His saying: (and that etc.) The most apparent and briefest is to say: because if it is pointed to independently, it is a body, i.e., a substance having volume; and if it is pointed to dependently, it is something inhering in the body, whether it be a point, a line, a surface, or a mathematical body or other, due to the impossibility of the indivisible atom and what is in its ruling, so it is not an indivisible atom, nor a line, nor a surface, nor something inhering in one of them; and this is on the assumption of disregarding its substantially, so it is necessary to suffice with its being a body. As for what the commentator mentioned, there is disorder in it, because if he restricted sensory indication to independence, his saying 'not substantial rather accidental' is not correct, and if he did not restrict, his saying 'it is a body, i.e., a bodily form' is not correct because it could be a mathematical body. His saying: ('rather accidental)' – evidently it is an elision from his saying 'rather'? No, 'rather a line or an accidental surface', and there is in it that it could be a point, so it is necessary to address it unless it is said: rather an accidental thing, then it includes the point as well. But here, eliding from false to false has no meaning. And the correct thing is to say: and what does not divide at all, or divides in one direction or two, is a point or a surface due to the impossibility of the indivisible atom and what is in its ruling. His saying: (then hyle would be something inhering etc.) i.e., a bodily form; he alerted by that because it is necessary from its being divided in three directions, not from its being composed of hyle and form. His saying: (and the manifestations) and they are particularities of types, kinds, and individuals. His saying: (due to absence of specifier) and the objection against it will come later in his saying: and it may be said: it is possible that a form accompanies hyle, i.e., a particular form for example.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the second division of hyle, presenting the first proof that hyle is never free from bodily form. The argument splits into two cases: either hyle is capable of sensory indication or not.

1. **If it has indication**: Since hyle would then have position, it must either divide in three directions (becoming a body, which is a bodily form), not divide at all (becoming an indivisible atom, which is impossible), or divide in one or two directions (becoming a line or surface, which are not substances but accidents, thus inhering in a body). In all these subcases, hyle ends up either being a body itself or inhering in a body, contradicting the assumption that it is a substrate for form.

2. **If it has no indication**: Hyle is not extended; but it is still receptive of form. When form arises in it, three possibilities exist: form is present in all places/manifestations, in none, or in some. The first two are invalid because without form hyle cannot exist (as a thing neither one nor many is impossible). The third is also invalid because it would require a specifier (mukhassis), which is not present. Thus all possibilities fail, proving hyle must always have form.

The commentary refines the argument, discussing whether 'indication' means independent or dependent pointing, and correcting the commentator's earlier expression. The unity of hyle in itself is affirmed even before form, but the relevant unity here is continuous unity, not the unity of the essence.

Correction: The argument proves that hyle is always accompanied by bodily form; otherwise, it would either be a body itself or lead to logical contradictions.

Hyle must always have bodily form; otherwise, logical contradictions arise from the impossibility of hyle existing as an extended substance or as a formless substrate.

?
The argument eliminates all possibilities except that hyle always has form. It leaves no room for 'maybe' or 'sometimes'. Are there beliefs or commitments in your own life where you have left room for exceptions or 'maybe' regarding obedience to Allah? How would it change your daily actions if you insisted—like this proof insists—that there is no alternative to submission in every aspect of your life?
?

Your own soul, in this life, is either oriented toward tawheed (one direction) or scattered (many distractions). Are you aware of times when you are 'neither one nor many'—neither fully committed to Allah nor fully distracted—just floating in indecision? Iji's Answer and Refutation of Two Arguments for Hyle
Iji gives the answer that the free agent determines place, then presents and refutes two arguments for hyle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Iji refutes two key arguments for the necessity of hyle by appealing to the free agent and the relativity of potentiality and act.

Core Idea
The free agent makes hyle's specification possible; potentiality and act can coexist in one thing under different respects.

This passage from Iji's work presents two key arguments for the existence of hyle (matter) and their refutation. First, the claim that only form determines place is answered by pointing to the free agent. Then, the argument from act and potentiality is refuted by noting that a single thing can have both relative to different things. Finally, the argument from division is refuted by clarifying that increase and decrease are attributes of the composite, not of hyle itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full translation. Let the structure emerge naturally.
Notice the three main parts: answer, two arguments, and their refutation.
2
Understand the answer regarding the free agent
Iji responds to the objection that only form determines place by asserting that the free agent's will specifies the place for corporeality indwelling in hyle.
This refutes the assumption that no free agent exists.
3
Analyze the second argument (act and potentiality)
The argument claims that if hyle were isolated, it would have both existence in act and preparedness to receive form, which is impossible for a unitary thing. Thus hyle must be united with form.
Iji later refutes this by showing that a single thing can have both relative to different things.
4
Analyze the third argument (division)
The argument says that if hyle could exist without form, then after division, the matter of the part and whole would lead to absurdities about equality or increase, implying corporeal form.
Iji refutes this by noting that hyle itself is not described with increase/decrease.
5
Examine the refutation
Iji rejects both arguments: the second because potentiality and act can coexist in one thing under different respects; the third because hyle's attributes (increase/decrease) only appear with the corporeal form.
This shows the arguments do not prove hyle's necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( والجواب ) عن هذا الوجه من الاستدلال أنه فرع عدم القادر المختار وأنه لا مخصص بالحيز المعين إلا الصورة وما يتبعها من الأوضاع. لكنا نقول : إن الجسمية إذا حلت في الهيولى تخصصت بحيز معين لإرادة الفاعل المختار الذي أوجد الجسمية فيها باختياره. الوجه الثاني : أنه يلزم له أي للمجرد الذي هو الهيولى فعل وقبول، يعني أن الهيولى لو تجردت عن الصورة لكان لها حال تجردها وجود بالفعل واستعداد لقبول الصورة، وقد تبين أن الشيء الأحدي أن يتصف بالقوة والفعل معاً، فوجب أن تكون المادة المجردة مجتمعة يمتنع مع الصورة هذا خلف. الوجه الثالث : لو جاز تجرد هيولى جسم عن صورته لجاز تجردها بعد انقسامه إلى جزأين مثلاً، وحينئذ نقول : مادة الجزء ومادة الكل إن تجردتا معاً فإن كانتا واحدة بأن لا تزيد مادة الكل على مادة الجزء فالشيء مع غيره كالعدم معه وذلك محال، وإلا أي وإن لم تكونا واحدة كان المحمول المركب من مادتي الجزأين أعني مادة الكل زائداً على مادة الجزء، فثم مقدار باعتباره صارت المادة متصفة بالزيادة والنقصان وصورة جسمية لأن الجوهر الممتد في الجهات هو الجسمية كما مر، فلا تكون الهيولى مجردة. وقد عرفت ما فيهما أي هذين الوجهين من الفساد أما في الثاني فلجواز اتصاف الواحدة بالقوة والفعل بالنسبة إلى شيئين، وأما في الثالث فلأن الهيولى في نفسها لا توصف بمساواة ولا بزيادة ونقصان إنما تتصف بهذه الأوصاف حال اقترانها بالصورة الجسمية، فلا نكررهما.
(And the answer) regarding this aspect of the argument is that it is based on the absence of the free agent and that there is no specifier for the specific place except the form and what follows it of positions. But we say: When the corporeality indwells in the hyle, it becomes specified to a specific place by the will of the free agent who brought the corporeality into existence in it by His choice. The second aspect: That it necessitates for it—i.e., for the isolated which is the hyle—act and reception, meaning that if the hyle were stripped of form, it would have, in its state of isolation, existence in act and preparedness to receive form, and it has been shown that a unitary thing cannot be described with potentiality and act together, so it is necessary that the isolated matter be united [with form]—this is contradictory. The third aspect: If it were allowed for the hyle of a body to be isolated from its form, then it would be allowed for it to be isolated after its division into two parts, for example, and then we say: The matter of the part and the matter of the whole, if they are both isolated, then if they are one—i.e., that the matter of the whole does not exceed the matter of the part—then a thing together with another thing is like non-existence with it, and that is impossible; otherwise (if they are not one) then the composite of the two matters of the parts (i.e., the matter of the whole) would be additional to the matter of the part, so there would be a quantity by virtue of which the matter becomes described with increase and decrease, and a corporeal form because the substance extended in directions is the corporeality as previously mentioned, so the hyle would not be isolated. And you have known what is in both of them—i.e., these two aspects—of corruption: As for the second, because it is possible for a single thing to be described with potentiality and act with respect to two things; and as for the third, because the hyle in itself is not described with equality nor with increase and decrease—it is only described with these attributes when it is associated with the corporeal form. So we do not repeat them.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage provides the answer to the objection that assumes no free agent; the reply asserts that the body becomes specified to a specific place by the will of the free agent. Then it presents two arguments for the necessity of hyle: the second argument from act and potentiality (that a single thing cannot have both simultaneously, so hyle must be united with form), and the third argument from division (that if hyle were separable, absurdities follow regarding the matter of parts and whole). The refutation of both arguments follows: the second is invalid because one thing can have potentiality and act relative to different things; the third is invalid because hyle itself is not described with increase or decrease—these only appear with the corporeal form.

Correction: Actually, the refutation demonstrates that a single thing can be described with potentiality and act relative to two different things, so the argument fails.

Checkpoint

A) It is impossible for one thing to have both.
B) A single thing can have both relative to different things.
C) Hyle cannot have potentiality.
D) The free agent negates potentiality.

Key Takeaway
The free agent resolves the specification of place, and potentiality and act can coexist in one thing under different respects, undermining the arguments for necessary hyle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you truly believed that Allah's will determines the specifics of your existence—your place, your circumstances—would you trust Him more? Or do you still think it is up to chance or your own planning? Examine your heart: do you rely on the free agent or on yourself?

Proof of Prime Matter from Shape and Size
Al-Jurjani presents a proof for prime matter from the shape of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani argues for the existence of prime matter in bodies by analyzing the source of shape in finite things.

Core Idea
Shape requires matter; form alone cannot account for variation in shape.

Al-Jurjani now turns to a proof for prime matter based on a fundamental property of bodies: shape. Since every finite body has a particular shape, the question arises whether that shape comes from corporeality itself or from another cause. The answer leads to the necessity of prime matter. This lesson unpacks the dichotomy and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Start by reading the Arabic text and its English translation. Follow the logical flow from the finitude of dimensions to the necessity of prime matter.
Pay attention to the phrase 'إما لنفس الجسمية' which sets up the dichotomy.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The argument presents two options: either shape is due to corporeality alone, or it is due to another cause. Notice how each option is handled.
The first option leads to an impossibility; the second leads to the need for prime matter.
3
Understand the refutation of the first option
If shape came from corporeality alone, all bodies would share the same shape and size, making the whole equal to the part—an impossibility.
This absurdity forces us to accept the second option.
4
Grasp the conclusion about prime matter
The second option implies that form can accept different shapes, which requires separation and conjunction—processes that need prime matter. Thus prime matter exists.
The argument relies on the earlier proof that what accepts separation and conjunction must be associated with matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في جميع الجهات لتناهي الأبعاد ( و) كان أيضاً ( مشكلاً) بشكل مخصوص لأن الشكل كما عرفت هيئة شيء تحيط به نهاية واحدة أو أكثر من جهة إحاطتها به فكل شيء متناه يلزمه أن يكون ذا شكل فذلك الشكل الثابت للصورة المجردة (إما لنفس الجسمية ) ولوازمها فكل جسم يجب أن يكون له ذلك الشكل ) العارض لمقدار مخصوص لاشتراك الأجسام كلها في الجسمية المقتضية له فيتساوى) حينئذ (الكل والجزء) في الشكل والمقدار المخصوصين وهو محال ) أو (لا) لنفس الجسمية بل لسبب آخر ( فتكون ( الصورة المجردة ( قابلة لغيره ) أي لغير ذلك الشكل من الأشكال المخالفة له وما هو ( أي ليس قبول شكل آخر ) إلا بالفصل والوصل فالصورة بدون الهيولى قابلة للفصل والوصل وقد أبطلناه ) بما مر من أن القابل لهما لا بد أن يكون مقارناً للهيولى
In all directions due to the finitude of dimensions, and it also has a particular shape, because shape, as you know, is the configuration of a thing surrounded by one or more boundaries from the aspect of their surrounding it. So every finite thing necessarily has a shape. So that shape belonging to the abstracted form (الصورة المجردة) is either due to corporeality (الجسمية) itself and its concomitants, so every body must have that shape which is an accident of a particular magnitude, because all bodies share in corporeality that necessitates it; then the whole and the part would be equal in that particular shape and magnitude, which is impossible. Or it is not due to corporeality itself but due to another cause; then the abstracted form would be capable of something other than that shape, i.e., of shapes different from it, and that (capability of accepting another shape) is only through separation and conjunction. So the form without prime matter (الهيولى) is capable of separation and conjunction, and we have already refuted this by the earlier point that what accepts both must be associated with prime matter.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of prime matter (الهيولى) in bodies. The argument proceeds by dichotomy. First, the author establishes that any finite thing has a shape. The abstracted form (الصورة المجردة) in a body is finite, so it has a particular shape. The question is: does that shape come from corporeality (الجسمية) alone, or from another cause? If from corporeality alone, then since all bodies share in corporeality, they would all have the same shape and size, making the whole and the part equal in these respects—which is absurd. If from another cause, then the abstracted form must be capable of accepting different shapes, and that capability requires separation and conjunction. But the form alone, without prime matter, cannot undergo separation and conjunction; that requires matter, as previously established. Therefore, prime matter must exist in bodies.

Key Terms
الهيولى
الصورة المجردة
الجسمية
الشكل
المقدار
الفصل والوصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Shape is a necessary attribute of form alone and does not require matter.
Correction: The argument shows that if shape were solely from form, all bodies would be identical in shape. Variation in shape requires matter as a substrate for change.

Checkpoint
What is the first possibility considered in the dichotomy regarding the shape of the abstracted form?
A) The shape is due to prime matter alone.
B) The shape is due to corporeality itself and its concomitants.
C) The shape is due to the specific form of the body.
D) The shape is due to external causes.

Key Takeaway
Prime matter is necessary because form alone cannot account for the diversity of shapes; shape requires a substrate capable of change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If even your physical form depends on something deeper, how does this affect your attachment to your appearance? Will you invest more in what is permanent—your soul—than in what changes?
?
The argument shows that form alone cannot account for diversity; matter is necessary. In your life, do you often focus only on external forms (status, looks, wealth) while neglecting the inner substance? How can you remind yourself daily that true value lies not in shape but in the reality behind it?
?
This proof uses logical necessity to affirm a created reality (prime matter). If your mind can grasp such deep truths about the physical world, what does that tell you about the Creator who gave you that intellect? How will you use your intellect today to draw closer to Him?

Proof of Prime Matter from Non-Pointability and Immateriality
Al-Jurjani presents a second proof for prime matter from non-pointability and immateriality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Jurjani's second proof for prime matter based on the impossibility of a purely abstract form being bodily.

Core Idea
An unpointable form cannot be bodily; a purely intellectual form cannot join matter.

Al-Jurjani provides a second argument for the necessity of prime matter. He shows that if a form is not pointable, it cannot be a bodily form, and if it is purely intellectual, it cannot be associated with material bodies. This lesson unpacks the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the argument. Note the key terms: 'abstracted form' (الصورة المجردة), 'pointable' (مشار إليها), 'space' (حيز).
The passage contains a main proof and a simplified version.
2
Trace the conditional arguments
Identify the two conditional statements: (1) If the form is not pointable, then it is not bodily. (2) If the form is purely intellectual, then it cannot join matter. Follow the reasoning for each.
The first uses the definition of bodily form as substantial extension.
3
Connect to the necessity of prime matter
Understand how these arguments show that a purely abstract form cannot account for the spatial properties of a body, thus implying the need for a material substrate (prime matter).
The simplified version uses finitude and shape to reach the same conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كانت ( الصورة المجردة (غير مشار إليها فليست صورة جسمية لأن الصورة الجسمية ليست عبارة إلا عن هذا الامتداد الجوهري الممتد الجهات الملزوم للامتداد العرضي ذهناً وخارجاً ) ويمتنع أن يتصور ) هذا الامتداد بلا حيز ولا إشارة وأيضاً فتكون الصورة المجردة على تقدير كونها غير قابلة للإشارة ( أمراً عقلياً محضاً ) لا تعلق له بحيز أصلاً ( فيمتنع مقارنته للمادة ) المتحيزة ولو تبعاً كسائر المجردات واعلم أن هذا الاستدلال يتم بأن يقال : لو تجردت الصورة . لكانت متناهية ومتشكلة فذلك الشكل إما للجسمية وحدها أو لسبب آخر فلا حاجة إلى التعرض لكونها قابلة للإشارة أو غير قابلة لها بل هذا الترديد مما جعل في الملخص دليلاً مستقلاً، هكذا الصورة المفارقة إن قبلت الإشارة فهي لا محالة في جهة ومختصة بمادة، وإن لم تقبل فهي غير الصورة التي تشير إليها حال كونها مادية
And if the abstracted form is not pointable, then it is not a bodily form, because bodily form is nothing but this substantial extension extended in the directions, which is the necessary condition for accidental extension mentally and externally. And it is impossible to conceive this extension without space and indication. Also, the abstracted form, on the assumption that it is not capable of being pointed to, is a purely intellectual thing having no relation to space at all, so its conjunction with matter that occupies space is impossible, even by way of subordination, like other abstract entities. And know that this proof is completed by saying: If the form were abstracted, it would be finite and shaped, so that shape is either due to corporeality alone or due to another cause. There is no need to address whether it is pointable or not, but this dichotomy is made in the summary as an independent proof. Similarly, the separate form: if it accepts indication, then it is inevitably in a direction and specific to matter, and if it does not accept, then it is not the form that you point to when it is material.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof for prime matter, followed by a simplified restatement. The proof has two parts. First, if the abstracted form (الصورة المجردة) is not pointable (غير مشار إليها), then it cannot be a bodily form because bodily form is defined as substantial extension that is extended in directions and is the necessary condition for accidental extension. Such extension cannot be conceived without space (حيز) and indication (إشارة). Therefore, an abstract form that is not pointable cannot be a bodily form. Second, if the form is purely intellectual (أمر عقلي محض), it has no relation to space, so it cannot be joined to matter which occupies space, even subordinately. Hence, prime matter is necessary for a body. The author then notes that this proof can be simplified: the abstracted form must be finite and shaped, leading to the same dichotomy as in the first proof—the shape is either from corporeality or another cause—thus showing that the form cannot be purely abstract. This simplification avoids the need to discuss pointability.

Key Terms
الصورة المجردة
الإشارة
الحيز
الامتداد الجوهري
الامتداد العرضي
المادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A purely intellectual form can be associated with matter.
Correction: Al-Jurjani argues that a purely intellectual form has no spatial relation and therefore cannot be conjoined with matter, which is extended and pointable.

Checkpoint

A) A bodily form
B) A purely intellectual thing
C) Indivisible
D) Necessarily shaped

Key Takeaway
Prime matter is necessary because a form that is abstract cannot account for the spatial properties of bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Celestial Spheres Objection: Shape, Part, and Whole
Al-Jurjani addresses the objection that celestial spheres contradict the first proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the celestial spheres do not refute the argument that common corporeality necessitates equal shape and magnitude, and how the concept of an 'obstacle' (مانع) resolves the apparent contradiction.

Core Idea
Specific forms, not common corporeality, explain celestial shapes.

The proof based on common corporeality seemed to imply that all bodies must have the same shape and size. But what about the celestial spheres? Their parts differ in size, even though they all share spherical shape. This objection seems powerful. Al-Jurjani presents it and then answers it by invoking a hidden obstacle. Let's see how the defense works.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection passage to understand what the skeptic claims about celestial spheres.
Notice the objector distinguishes between shape from specific form and shape from common corporeality.
2
Identify the objector's reasoning
The objector says the sphere's shape comes from its specific form, not common corporeality, and the part shares the form yet differs in size.
3
Read Al-Jurjani's answer
See how he responds by saying an obstacle prevents equality, but the principle still holds.
The answer hinges on the presence of a مانع (obstacle).
4
Understand the role of the specific form as obstacle
The specific form acts as the obstacle that prevents the part from being identical to the whole, preserving the proof.
5
Reflect on the broader implications
Consider how this defense maintains the necessity of matter while accounting for observed diversity.
The proof's core remains intact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : (هذا الذي ذكرتموه من أن الجسمية المشتركة إذا اقتضت وحدها شكلاً مخصوصاً على مقدار معين وجب تساوي الأجسام حتى الجزء والكل في ذلك الشكل على ذلك المقدار ) ينتقض بالفلك إذ شكله مقتضى ذاته التي هي صورته النوعية ( وجزؤه ككله في تلك الصورة النوعية ) ولا يلزم تساويهما في المقدار والشكل المخصوصين معا بل بل لا يجوز ذلك فإن الأفلاك الخارجة والتداوير أجزاء للأفلاك الكلية مع امتناع التساوي في المقدار وإن كانت مساوية لها في الشكل الكري ) لأنا نقول : لولا مانع اقترن بجزء الفلك لكان شكل جزئه ) ومقداره ( ككله ) بسبب الاشتراك فيما يقتضيهما (لكن ثمة مانع يمنع من التساوي في الشكل
It should not be said: 'What you have mentioned—that common corporeality, if it alone necessitated a particular shape upon a specific magnitude, would necessitate the equality of bodies, even the part and the whole in that shape upon that magnitude—is refuted by the celestial sphere, for its shape is necessitated by its essence, which is its specific form, and its part is like the whole in that specific form, yet equality in magnitude and particular shape does not follow together; rather, it is not even permissible, for the outer spheres and epicycles are parts of the universal spheres while equality in magnitude is impossible, even though they are equal in spherical shape.' For we say: If there were no obstacle associated with the part of the sphere, then the shape and magnitude of its part would be like that of the whole, due to sharing what necessitates them, but there is an obstacle preventing equality in shape.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair concerning the earlier proof that common corporeality would force all bodies to be equal in shape and size. The objector brings up the celestial spheres: the sphere's shape comes from its specific form (صورة نوعية), not from common corporeality alone. Moreover, the part of a sphere (e.g., an outer sphere or epicycle) shares the same specific form as the whole sphere, yet the part is not equal to the whole in magnitude or shape—they have the same spherical shape but different sizes. This seems to contradict the original argument. The answer is that if there were no obstacle (مانع) associated with the part of the sphere, then the part would indeed be equal to the whole in shape and magnitude due to sharing the common corporeality that necessitates these. However, there is an obstacle—presumably the specific form or some other factor—that prevents equality in shape (and size?). Thus, the objection does not refute the original proof, because the proof assumed no obstacle from corporeality alone. The presence of a specific form (the obstacle) explains the discrepancy.

Key Terms

الفلك
الصورة النوعية
الشكل الكري
المقدار
المانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The celestial spheres disprove the necessity of matter because their parts have different sizes despite sharing a shape.
Correction: The specific form acts as an obstacle (مانع) that prevents the part from being identical to the whole, but matter (common corporeality) is still necessary; the proof only assumes no obstacle.

Checkpoint
What does the objector say about the celestial sphere's shape?
A) It is due to common corporeality.
B) It is due to its specific form.
C) It is due to prime matter.
D) It is due to the vacuum.

Key Takeaway
The celestial spheres do not refute the proof because the specific form acts as an obstacle, preventing the part from matching the whole, while common corporeality remains necessary.

Objection from the Celestial Sphere
Objection from celestial sphere's shape to the necessity of common corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the celestial sphere objection challenges the claim that common corporeality necessarily determines shape and magnitude, and how the objector uses the distinction between common corporeality and specific form.

Core Idea
Shape and magnitude are not solely determined by common corporeality; specific form can override.

The argument that common corporeality (al-jismiyya al-mushtaraka) alone determines a specific shape and measure seems plausible, but the objector raises a powerful counterexample: the celestial sphere. In this passage, you'll see an objection that uses the celestial sphere to show that shape can come from a body's specific form (al-sura al-naw'iyya), not just its generic corporeality. Understanding this objection is crucial for grasping how theologians defend the role of divine attributes in shaping matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the objector's own words come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: the objector first quotes the target argument, then presents the counterexample.
2
Identify the objection and its target
The objector begins with 'la yuqalu' (it is not said) and quotes an argument. Then he says 'yuntaqadu bil-falak' (is refuted by the celestial sphere). Identify what claim is being refuted.
The target claim is that common corporeality alone (wahdaha) necessitates a specific shape and measure.
3
Grasp the distinction between common corporeality and specific form
The objector asserts that the celestial sphere's shape is necessitated by its essence which is its specific form (sura naw'iyya). Understand that common corporeality is the generic property of being a body, while specific form is what makes a body the kind of body it is (e.g., celestial vs. terrestrial).
4
See how the celestial sphere and its parts illustrate the point
The objector notes that parts of the sphere (outer spheres and epicycles) share the same specific form as the whole, but they are not equal in magnitude. This shows that specific form can result in different magnitudes, disproving the claim that common corporeality alone would force equality.
5
Reflect on the implication for the original argument
Ask yourself: if shape can be determined by specific form rather than common corporeality, what does that mean for the argument that bodies must be equal in shape and magnitude? How does this relate to the broader discussion of divine attributes?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال: (هذا الذي ذكرتموه من أن الجسمية المشتركة إذا اقتضت وحدها شكلاً مخصوصاً على مقدار معين وجب تساوي الأجسام حتى الجزء والكل في ذلك الشكل على ذلك المقدار) ينتقض بالفلك إذ شكله مقتضى ذاته التي هي صورته النوعية (وجزؤه ككله في تلك الصورة النوعية) ولا يلزم تساويهما في المقدار والشكل المخصوصين معاً بل بل لا يجوز ذلك فإن الأفلاك الخارجة والتداوير أجزاء للأفلاك الكلية مع امتناع التساوي في المقدار وإن كانت مساوية لها في الشكل الكري.
It is not said: 'What you have mentioned, that common corporeality alone necessitates a specific shape in a certain measure, so bodies must be equal, even the part and the whole, in that shape and that measure,' is refuted by the celestial sphere, for its shape is necessitated by its essence which is its specific form, and its part is like its whole in that specific form, yet equality between them in measure and specific shape does not follow—rather it is not even permissible, for the outer spheres and the epicycles are parts of the total spheres while equality in measure is impossible, although they are equal to them in spherical shape.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the argument that common corporeality (al-jismiyya al-mushtaraka) alone determines shape and magnitude. The objector points to the celestial sphere (al-falak) as a counterexample: its shape is due to its specific form (sura naw'iyya), not common corporeality. The parts of the celestial sphere (e.g., outer spheres and epicycles) share the same specific form as the whole but do not have the same shape or magnitude—for example, they are equal in spherical shape but not in size. Thus, the claim that common corporeality alone would force equality between parts and whole is false, because the celestial sphere's shape comes from its specific form, and material conditions cause differences.

A) That the celestial sphere has a material substrate.
B) That common corporeality alone dictates shape and magnitude.
C) That specific form can determine shape.

The Obstacle that Prevents Part-Whole Identity
The author replies that material presence prevents the part from having the whole's shape and magnitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a part of a celestial sphere cannot have the same shape and magnitude as the whole due to a material obstacle.

Core Idea
A part cannot be identical to the whole because matter imposes a limit.

The previous objection assumed that if the part shares the same specific form as the whole, it should have the same shape and magnitude. Sanusi replies by introducing the concept of a material obstacle (manii') that prevents this equality. This passage reveals a key principle in kalam physics: the whole's properties arise from the specific form in conjunction with the matter, and a part, being a portion of that matter, cannot fully replicate that combination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the conditional structure and the introduction of the obstacle.
The passage begins with 'lā' (for we say) and uses 'lawlā' (were it not for).
2
Identify the two key elements
Find the specific form (al-sūra al-naw'iyya) and the material obstacle (māni'). Understand how they interact.
The obstacle is that the whole's shape comes from the specific form residing in the celestial matter.
3
Understand the reasoning
Trace the argument: if no obstacle, part would equal whole due to common specific form. But the whole's shape/magnitude results from that form plus the entire matter. The part, being a portion, cannot have that same combination.
The conclusion is that the part cannot have the same shape/magnitude, otherwise it would be the whole.
4
Apply to other simple bodies
The last sentence extends the reasoning to other simple bodies with actual parts. Consider how this principle applies universally.
This shows the argument is not limited to celestial spheres.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنا نقول: لولا مانع اقترن بجزء الفلك لكان شكل جزئه) ومقداره (ككله) بسبب الاشتراك فيما يقتضيهما (لكن ثمة مانع يمنع من التساوي في الشكل والمقدار جميعاً ( وهو أن الكل حصل له ذلك الشكل مع المقدار المخصوص بأن حلت الصورة الجسمية في المادة الفلكية فاقتضى لها صورته النوعية الحالة معها في تلك المادة مقداراً وشكلاً مخصوصين ( فامتنع أن يكون للجزء) من الفلك ) ذلك الشكل ) والمقدار ( وإلا لم يكن (جزءاً وكذا الكلام في سائر الأجسام البسيطة إذا كان لها أجزاء موجودة بالفعل.
For we say: Were it not for an obstacle associated with the part of the sphere, the shape of its part and its magnitude would be like its whole, due to the commonality in what necessitates them. But there is an obstacle that prevents equality in shape and magnitude together, namely that the whole obtained that shape with that specific magnitude because the corporeal form resides in the celestial matter, and its specific form, which coexists with it in that matter, necessitated a specific magnitude and shape. Therefore it is impossible for the part of the sphere to have that shape and magnitude, otherwise it would not be a part. And the same applies to other simple bodies when they have actually existing parts.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage provides the answer to the previous objection about the celestial sphere. The author explains that if there were no obstacle, the part would have the same shape and magnitude as the whole because they share the same necessitating factor (i.e., the specific form). However, there is an obstacle: the whole obtains its specific shape and magnitude because the corporeal form (al-sura al-jismiyya) inhering in the celestial matter (al-mada al-falakiyya) gives rise to the specific form (al-sura al-naw'iyya) which, together with the matter, necessitates a particular magnitude and shape. The part, being a portion of that matter, cannot have the same shape and magnitude because that would make it identical to the whole, contradicting its being a part. The same reasoning applies to other simple bodies (al-ajsam al-basita) that have actual parts.

Key Terms
مانع
مادة فلكية
صورة جسمية
صورة نوعية
بسيط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the answer denies that the part shares the specific form.
Correction: In fact, the answer affirms that the part shares the specific form but posits a material obstacle that prevents identity in shape and magnitude.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does a part of the celestial sphere not have the same shape and magnitude as the whole?
A) Because the part lacks the specific form entirely.
B) Because an obstacle prevents it, namely that the whole's shape comes from the specific form together with the matter in a way not replicated in the part.
C) Because common corporeality does not apply to parts.
D) Because the celestial sphere is not composed of parts.

Key Takeaway
The part of a celestial sphere cannot have the same shape and magnitude as the whole because the specific form together with the matter yields a unique whole that is not replicated in the part due to a material obstacle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author says the part cannot have the whole's shape 'otherwise it would not be a part.' Are there areas where you try to possess attributes that belong only to the whole (e.g., complete control, perfection)? What would change if you truly accepted your part-hood in this area starting today?
?
When you fail to achieve something you thought you should, do you blame the obstacle or ignore it? This lesson teaches that the obstacle is real and necessary. What if your limitation is not a flaw but a defining part of your created nature? How might embracing it bring you closer to the whole's purpose?

Objection from Imagined Parts and Imam al-Razi's Interpretation
Objection from assumed parts and Imam's interpretation of al-Isharat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an objection using imagined parts of simple bodies challenges the claim that corporeality determines shape, and how Imam al-Razi interprets the passage from al-Isharat to counter it.

Core Idea
Imagined parts reveal that shape is not solely determined by corporeality.

In this passage, an objector raises a subtle problem: if we assume parts of simple bodies like the celestial sphere, they can be imagined polygonal rather than round. This calls into question the idea that corporeality alone dictates shape. The author cites al-Isharat and presents two interpretations; here we focus on Imam al-Razi's reading.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its basis
Read the lines about the objection from assumed parts (الأجزاء المفروضة) and how simple bodies can be assumed polygonal. Note the claim that the obstacle is the part's arising from matter.
This sets up the challenge to the theory that corporeality determines shape.
2
Examine Imam al-Razi's interpretation
Read how al-Imam interprets the passage from al-Isharat. Focus on his reasoning that if corporeality alone necessitated shape, then bodies would be equal in extension and shape.
Imam's interpretation uses the idea that bodies share a common nature of extension.
3
See how the objection holds for actual celestial parts
Notice that Imam applies the objection to actually existing parts like eccentrics and epicycles, which share the specific form but differ in shape and measure. This refutes the claim that corporeality alone determines shape.
The objection is strengthened by reference to actual celestial parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من وجه النقض بالأجزاء المفروضة في الفلك وغيره من البسائط فإنها قد تفرض مضلعة لا مستديرة وزعم أن المانع حصول الجزء عن المادة. وفي الإشارات ولو لزمه منفرداً بنفسه عن نفسه تشابهت الأجسام في مقادير الامتدادات وهيأة التناهي والشكل فكان الجزء المفروض من مقدار ما يلزمه الكلية. وفسره الإمام بما حاصله: أنه لو لزم لامتداد الشكل المخصوص حال كونه منفرداً عن نفسه لزم استواء الأجسام في مقادير الامتدادات، وهي هيئة النامي ضرورة أن الأجسام مشتركة في طبيعة الامتداد الجسماني، فلو كان المقتضى للشكل المخصوص نفس الجسمية يوجب من استوائها في طبيعة الامتداد استواؤها في مقادير الامتداد والشكل، وأما قوله: لو كان الجزء المفروض من مقدار ما يلزمه ما يلزم فمعناه أن جزء الجسم البسيط مساو لكل في الماهية، فلو كان المقتضى للشكل الجسمية لكان الجزء مساوياً للكل في الشكل فعلى يرد النقض بالأجزاء الموجودة في الفلك كالخارج والتدوير فإنها مساوية لكله في الصورة النوعية المقتضية لشكله المخصوص مع عدم استواء الأجزاء في الشكل والمقدار المخصوص. وفسره المحقق الطوسي بما حاصله: إنه لو كانت الجسمية بنفسها مقتضية للشكل المخصوص لزم تشابه الأجسام أي الصورة الجسمية أي اتحادها في المقدار والشكل ويلزم منه تساوي الشكل المفروض منها للكل لا بمعنى أنه يكون فرضهما ممكناً من حيث الفرض ويلزم المحال من جهة تشابه أصولهما بعد الفرض بل بمعنى امتناع فرضي الكلية والجزئية في الأصل، بأن وصفهما بالفرض يستلزم رفعهما . فعلى هذا التقدير نقض بالأجزاء المفروضة في الفلك، فإنها مساوية للكل في الصورة النوعية المقتضية للشكل المخصوص مع عدم امتناع فرض الكلية والجزئية . والجواب على التقديرين الفرق بين الصورتين بأنه في صورة النقض المادة موجودة، فالصورة النوعية المقتضية وإن كانت متحدة في الكل والجزء لكن اختلاف القابل مانع عن حصول الشكل الكلي للجزء ومن امتناع فرض الكلية والجزئية، وفيما نحن فيه الصورة الجسمية مجردة عن المادة المستقلة في اقتضاء الكل ممن تشابهما يلزم المحال المذكورة.
And among them is one who objects with the assumed parts (al-ajza' al-mafruda) in the celestial sphere and other simple bodies, for they may be assumed polygonal, not round, claiming that the obstacle is that the part arises from matter. And in al-Isharat: 'And if it were necessitated by itself alone, apart from itself, bodies would be similar in the measures of extensions, the configuration of finitude, and shape; thus the assumed part of a measure would necessitate what the whole necessitates.' Al-Imam interpreted it in summary: that if the specific shape were necessitated by the extension while being separate from itself, bodies would be equal in the measures of extensions and the configuration of finitude, because bodies share the nature of corporeal extension. So if the necessitator of the specific shape were corporeality itself, then from their equality in the nature of extension would follow equality in measures of extension and shape. As for his statement: 'if the assumed part of a measure would necessitate what the whole necessitates,' its meaning is that the part of a simple body is equal to the whole in essence; so if the necessitator of shape were corporeality, the part would be equal to the whole in shape. Thus the objection applies using the actually existing parts of the celestial sphere like the eccentrics and epicycles, for they are equal to the whole in the specific form that necessitates its specific shape, despite the parts not being equal in shape and specific measure. Al-Muhaqqiq al-Tusi interpreted it in summary: that if corporeality by itself were necessitator of the specific shape, bodies would be similar, i.e., the corporeal form would be unified in measure and shape, and from this would follow the equality of the assumed shape of them to the whole—not meaning that their assumption is possible in terms of assumption and that impossibility follows from similarity of their principles after assumption, but rather meaning the impossibility of assuming wholeness and partiality in origin, because describing them by assumption entails their removal. Thus on this assumption, the objection is made with assumed parts in the celestial sphere, for they are equal to the whole in the specific form that necessitates its specific shape, while there is no impossibility in assuming wholeness and partiality. And the answer according to both interpretations is the distinction between the two forms: that in the case of the objection, matter is present, so the necessitating specific form, although it is unified in the whole and the part, the difference of the receiver prevents the part from attaining the whole's shape and prevents the assumption of wholeness and partiality; whereas in our case, the corporeal form is abstracted from matter and independent in necessitating the whole, so if they were similar, the mentioned impossibility would follow.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents a further objection from imagined parts (al-ajza' al-mafruda) of simple bodies like the celestial sphere. The objector argues that one can assume a part of a simple body to be polygonal rather than round, thereby casting doubt on the claim that corporeality alone determines shape. The author then cites al-Isharat and provides two interpretations by al-Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) and al-Muhaqqiq al-Tusi. Al-Imam's interpretation focuses on the equality of bodies in extensions and shape if corporeality were the sole cause, and points out that actual parts like eccentrics and epicycles in the celestial sphere share the specific form but differ in shape, which refutes the claim. Al-Tusi's interpretation emphasizes that the impossibility of assuming a part and whole arises only when corporeality is abstracted from matter. The answer common to both is that in the objection (where matter is present), the specific form is unified but the receiver (the material substrate) differs, preventing the part from having the whole's shape and preventing the very assumption of wholeness and partiality. In contrast, in the original argument where corporeality is abstracted from matter, if the part and whole were similar, the absurdity would follow.

Key Terms
أجزاء مفروضة
بسائط
إشارات
الإمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that imagined parts are purely hypothetical and have no bearing on actual bodies.
Correction: Imam shows that the objection applies even to actual existing parts of the celestial sphere, like eccentrics and epicycles, making it a real challenge.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why might an assumed part of a simple body be polygonal?
A) Because matter forces the part to be polygonal.
B) Because the part is not subject to the same laws as the whole.
C) Because the part arises from matter, which allows different shapes.
D) Because the part is imaginary and can be any shape.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi shows that even if parts share the specific form, differences in shape disprove that corporeality alone determines shape.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed a simple explanation for why something is the way it is, without testing it against the complex reality? When you saw a counterexample (like the parts of a sphere not being spherical), did you revise your assumption, or did you ignore the evidence so comfortable? What part of the truth are you settling for because it's easier than investigating deeper?
?
In your own life, do you treat certain beliefs as if they are self-evident (like 'corporeality alone determines shape') — without examining the hidden assumptions beneath them? What would it look like to honestly examine one such belief this week, even if it threatens your comfort?
?
When you encounter an argument that uses hypothetical examples (like imagined parts), do you dismiss it as abstract and irrelevant? Or do you allow it to challenge your understanding of reality? This lesson forces you to see that even imagined things can reveal truth. When will you stop hiding behind the concrete and allow the intellectual tools God gave you to pierce through to deeper reality?
