Al-Tusi's Interpretation and the Shared Answer
Al-Tusi's interpretation and the common answer about matter's role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Tusi's interpretation of al-Isharat and the common answer that distinguishes the presence of matter as the key difference.

Core Idea
The presence of matter changes the rule: where matter exists, the part does not necessitate the whole's shape.

Al-Tusi offers a different interpretation of the same passage from al-Isharat, focusing on the impossibility of assuming wholeness and partiality. The answer then unifies both interpretations by pointing to the role of matter in the objection's scenario versus the abstracted corporeal form in the original argument.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Tusi's interpretation
Read how al-Muhaqqiq al-Tusi interprets the passage. Note his focus on the impossibility of assuming wholeness and partiality in the original argument.
Tusi's interpretation shifts the focus to the conceptual impossibility rather than equality of shape.
2
Understand the objection under Tusi's reading
See how the objection is valid on Tusi's interpretation: assumed parts in the celestial sphere are equal in specific form but not in shape, yet assuming wholeness and partiality is possible.
This shows a different angle of the objection.
3
Grasp the shared answer
The answer distinguishes the two scenarios: in the objection, matter is present, so the specific form is unified but the receiver differs, preventing the part from having the whole's shape. In the original argument, corporeal form is abstracted from matter, so similarity would lead to absurdity.
This resolves the objection by highlighting the role of matter.

The Mutual Need of Matter and Form
Proof of bilateral dependence without circularity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why matter (hyle) and form cannot exist independently and how their mutual dependence avoids logical circularity.

Core Idea
Matter and form need each other in different respects, so no vicious cycle.

Al-Iji now tackles a key question in the metaphysics of composite substances: how are matter and form related? They are inseparable, but does that mean they cause each other? In this passage, he argues that each depends on the other in a distinct way, avoiding the pitfall of circular reasoning. The analogy of supports holding up a ceiling will help you grasp the subtlety.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: a premise, then the mutual need, then two specific arguments.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the premise about efficient cause
The opening sentences state that something one in person requires an efficient cause that is also one in person. This sets up why neither matter nor form can be the sole cause of the other.
This premise is used to rule out one-sided causation.
3
Analyze the analogy of supports
Al-Iji compares the succession of forms to supports holding a ceiling. When one support is removed, another takes its place, so the ceiling stays. This shows how matter needs form for its preservation, not for its initial existence.
Focus on the concept of 'preservation' (baqa') vs. origination.
4
Examine form's need for matter
Form requires matter for individuation (tashakhkhus) and the accidents that accompany it, like shape and size. Matter provides the principle of multiplicity and finitude.
This is the other side of the mutual need.
5
Reflect on the avoidance of circularity
The needs are in different respects (preservation vs. individuation), so there is no logical circle. This is the key insight of the passage.
Circularity would occur if each needed the other in the same respect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصورة المطلقة قلنا : الواحد بالشخص لا بد أن تكون علته الفاعلية واحدة بالشخص والصورة المطلقة ليست كذلك إذا تمهد هذا فنقول : التلازم وامتناع الانفكاك بينهما دل على الاحتياج من الجانبين ( فحاجة الهيولى إلى الصورة في بقائها لأن الصورة تستحفظها بتواردها عليها ( إذ لو فرضنا زوال صورة ) عنها ( وعدم اقتران ) صورة ( أخرى ) بها ( عدمت المادة ( لما مر من امتناع بقائها خالية عن الصور كلها ( فهي ) أي تلك الصور المتواردة عليها ) كالدعائم تزال واحدة منها عن ( واحدة ) منها عن السقف ) وتقام مقامها) دعامة (أخرى) فيكون السقف باقياً على حاله بتعاقب تلك الدعائم. ( وحاجة الصورة ) إلى الهيولى ( في التشخص والعوارض اللازمة لتشخصها (إذ قد علمت أن تشخصها وتعددها (بالمادة وما يكتنفها من الأعراض ) وعلمت أيضاً أن تناهيها وتشكلها لأجل المادة فقد ثبت الاحتياج من الطرفين على وجه لم يلزم منه الدور.
The absolute form: we say: the one in person must have its efficient cause one in person, and the absolute form is not like that. When this is established, we say: the mutual entailment and the impossibility of separation between them indicate the need from both sides. So the need of hyle for form in its preservation because the form preserves it by succeeding upon it; for if we suppose the removal of a form from it and the non-attachment of another form to it, the matter would cease to exist, due to what has passed of the impossibility of its remaining devoid of all forms. So it (i.e., those successive forms upon it) is like the supports: one is removed from the ceiling and another support is put in its place, so the ceiling remains in its state by the succession of those supports. And the need of form for hyle in individuation and the accidents necessary for its individuation, since you have known that its individuation and multiplicity are through matter and the accidents that surround it, and you have also known that its finitude and formation are due to matter. So the need from both sides is established in a way that does not entail circularity.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the mutual dependence between hyle (matter) and form in composite substances. The author begins by establishing a premise: something that is one in person must have its efficient cause also one in person. The absolute form (i.e., form in general) is not one in person, so neither matter nor form can be the efficient cause of the other. They are co-dependent. The mutual entailment and impossibility of separation between them shows that each needs the other. Specifically, matter needs form for its preservation (not for its original existence), because form preserves matter by succeeding upon it—like supports that replace one another to keep a ceiling standing. Form, on the other hand, needs matter for its individuation and accompanying accidents (like finite shape and size), which come from matter. The author concludes that the need is mutual without circularity because the need is in different respects.

Key Terms
هيولى hyle
صورة form
تلازم mutual entailment
امتناع انفكاك impossibility of separation
احتياج need
بقاء preservation
تشخص individuation
دور circularity

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things depend on each other, it leads to circular reasoning (dawr).
Correction: Circularity is avoided because the dependence is in different respects: matter needs form for preservation, while form needs matter for individuation. They are not each other's cause in the same way.

Checkpoint
According to al-Iji, what is the relationship between matter (hyle) and form?
A) They are independent and do not need each other.
B) They are mutually dependent but in different respects, avoiding circularity.
C) Form depends entirely on matter for its existence.
D) Matter depends entirely on form for its existence.

Key Takeaway
Matter and form depend on each other in different aspects—preservation and individuation—so their mutual need does not entail logical circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If matter cannot preserve itself without a succession of forms, what does that say about your own existence? What 'supports' are holding you in existence right now, and would you know it if one were removed?
?
You consider yourself a unique individual, but this lesson says your individuation comes from your matter and its accidents. How does that change the way you see your identity? Is there anything about 'you' that is not dependent on something else?
?
The author uses the analogy of supports holding a ceiling: one is removed, another takes its place. What are the 'forms' in your life that you rely on for preservation but never notice until they start to shift? What would it take for you to recognize the true Preserver?

The Fifth Point: Bodies Have Specific Forms
Al-Iji argues that every body has a specific form based on differing properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why every body must have a specific form distinct from its common corporeality and matter, and how differences in properties prove this.

Core Idea
Differences in bodies' properties prove they have distinct specific forms.

This passage is the fifth of several points establishing the existence of hyle and form. Al-Iji now argues that just as matter always has the corporeal form, it also always has a specific form that determines the body's type. The proof comes from observable differences in how bodies accept division and aggregation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once without analysis, then the translation. Notice how the argument progresses from a claim to evidence and refutation.
The passage is dense but logical.
2
Identify the main claim
The claim is that every body has a specific form (sura naw'iyya) that makes it the type of body it is.
3
Examine the evidence
The evidence is that bodies differ in their necessary properties (lawazim), specifically acceptance of separative division and aggregation. Three categories are given with examples.
Look for the three categories: easy, difficult, non-acceptance.
4
Understand the refutation
Al-Iji refutes two alternative explanations: common corporeality (cannot cause different effects) and hyle alone. This forces the conclusion that specific form is the cause.
This is a classic kalam argument from difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خامسها : كما أن الهيولى لا تخلو عن الصورة الجسمية كذلك لا تخلو عن صورة أخرى بل لكل جسم من الأجسام صورة نوعية بحسبها يتنوع الجسم أنواعاً كثيرة من البسائط والمركبات وذلك لأنها مختلفة في اللوازم كقبول الانقسام الانفكاكي وقبول الالتئام والتشكل التابع لهما (بسهولة) كما في العنصريات الرطبة مثل الماء والهواء ) أو عسر ) كما في العنصريات اليابسة مثل الحجر والحديد ( أو عدمه ) أي عدم قبول ذلك الانقسام والالتئام والتشكل كما في الفلكيات. وليس ذلك الاختلاف في تلك اللوازم (للجسمية المشتركة) بين الأجسام لأن الأمور المختلفة لا يجوز أن تكون معللة بأمر مشترك ولا للهيولى
The fifth: just as hyle is never devoid of corporeal form, similarly it is never devoid of another form; rather, every body has a specific form according to which the body is diversified into many types, simple and composite. That is because they differ in the necessary properties, such as acceptance of separative division and acceptance of aggregation and formation consequent upon them, with ease, as in moist elemental bodies like water and air, or with difficulty, as in dry elemental bodies like stone and iron, or non-acceptance, as in celestial bodies. And that difference in those properties is not due to the common corporeality shared among bodies, because different things cannot be caused by a common thing, nor is it due to hyle.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is the fifth point in the proof of hyle. It argues that matter is never devoid not only of corporeal form but also of a specific form (sura naw'iyya). Every body has a specific form that determines its type. The evidence is that bodies differ in their necessary properties (lawazim), such as how easily they accept division, aggregation, and shaping. Some are easy (water, air), some difficult (stone, iron), and some do not accept them at all (celestial bodies). This difference cannot be due to the common corporeality shared by all bodies, because a common cause cannot produce different effects. Nor can it be due to hyle alone. Therefore, there must be a specific form in each body that causes the differences.

Key Terms
صورة نوعية specific form
لوازم necessary properties
جسمية مشتركة common corporeality
هيولى hyle
انقسام انفكاكي separative division
الالتئام والتشكل aggregation and formation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the difference in bodies' properties is due to the common corporeality or to matter alone.
Correction: The passage refutes both: a common cause cannot produce different effects, and hyle is common to all bodies.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth point, what explains the differences in bodies' properties?
A) The common corporeality shared by all bodies.
B) Hyle (matter) alone.
C) The specific form of each body.
D) Accidental qualities.

Key Takeaway
Every body has a specific form that determines its distinct properties, proven by the variation in how bodies accept division and aggregation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate the diversity of creation—from the fluid water to the solid stone to the distant stars—do you see them merely as different arrangements of the same matter, or do you recognize the distinct forms Allah has given each? How does this awareness change your appreciation of His wisdom and power? What specific creation will you meditate on today with this insight?
?
The passage argues that the differences in bodies cannot come from what is common; each has a specific form. In your own life, do you sometimes attribute the unique qualities of others to mere chance or material circumstances, rather than to the distinct forms Allah has placed in them? How might acknowledging the specific form of every person transform your interactions with them?
?
Celestial bodies do not accept division or aggregation, yet they have their own form. This implies that even the unchanging heavens have a purpose and order from Allah. Is there an area of your life where you seek constant change and division, resisting the form Allah has prescribed for you? What would it look like to embrace the 'form' (i.e., the divine pattern) He has set for your life, even if it feels static or challenging?

Refuting Natural Place: The Attraction of Like to Like
Thought experiment refuting natural place by showing motion is due to homogeneity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the motion of earthly matter is explained by attraction to similar mass (homogeneity) rather than an inherent tendency to a specific location.

Core Idea
Motion is caused by the part seeking its whole, not by a place.

The author challenges the ancient notion that each element has a natural place towards which it moves. Through a thought experiment, he shows that the concept of center and periphery is relative. He then presents Thabit ibn Qurra's view and several scenarios to demonstrate that motion is driven by the attraction of like to like, not by a place's inherent property.

Guided Path
1
Imagine an empty universe with only earth
Read the first part of the passage where the author sets up the scenario: all regions empty, then earth created alone. Notice that without a center or periphery, all regions are equal.
This undermines the idea of a natural place.
2
Understand Thabit ibn Qurra's explanation
The author quotes Thabit's view that no place has a special property to attract a body. Instead, Thabit explains motion as part seeking whole by homogeneity.
The author uses Thabit to support his own refutation.
3
Examine the thought experiments
The passage presents three scenarios: earth split into halves, earth raised to sun's sphere with a stone, earth scattered. Each shows that parts move toward each other, not toward a fixed place.
These illustrate the principle of homogeneity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرضت الأحياز كلها خالية عن الأجسام ثم فرض أنه خلق الأرض وحدها كان نسبتها إلى الأحياز كلها سواء إذ ليس ثمة مركز ولا محيط وإذا جعلت الأرض بأسرها في أي حيز اتفق وجب أن تقف فيه ولا تنتقل منه إلى غيره، لاستحالة الترجيح بلا مرجح يتوهم من أن الأرض طالبة للمكان الذي هي فيه باطل كما قال به ثابت بن قرة فإنه قال ليس لشيء من الأمكنة حال يخص به دون غيره أن جسماً معيناً طالب له بطبعه دون ما عداه وإذا رمينا مدرة إلى فوق فإنما تعود المدرة إلى مركز الأرض لا لأن الطبيعة الأرضية طالبة له كما توهم، بل لأن الجزء مائل إلى كله الذي يجذبه بعلة الجنسية ولو جعل الأرض نصفين وجعل كل نصف في جانب آخر لكان طلب كل منهما مساوياً لطلب صاحبه حتى يلتقيا في وسط المسافة التي بينهما ولو فرض أن الأرض كلها رفعت إلى فلك فيه الآن الشمس، ثم أطلق من المكان الذي هي حجر لارتفع ذلك الحجر إليها لطلبه للأمر العظيم الذي هو شبيهه، ولو فرض أنها تقطعت وتفرقت في جوانب العالم ثم أطلقت أجزاؤها، لكان يتوجه بعضها إلى بعض ويقف حيث يتهيأ تلاقيها
If all regions were supposed empty of bodies, then it was supposed that the earth alone was created, its relation to all regions would be equal, since there is neither center nor periphery. And if the whole earth is placed in any region whatsoever, it must stand there and not move from it, because preference without a preponderating factor is impossible. The imagination that the earth seeks the place it is in is false, as stated by Thabit ibn Qurra, for he said: No place has a state peculiar to it such that a particular body seeks it by nature to the exclusion of others. And if we throw a stone upward, it returns to the center of the earth, not because the earthly nature seeks it as imagined, but because the part inclines to its whole, which attracts it by reason of homogeneity. And if the earth were made into two halves, each half placed on opposite sides, the demand of each would be equal to the demand of the other until they meet midway. And if it were supposed that the whole earth were raised to a sphere where the sun now is, then a stone released from its position would rise to it seeking the great thing that is its like. And if it were supposed that the earth broke up and scattered in the regions of the world, then its parts were released, they would move toward each other and stop where their meeting becomes possible.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a proof by counterexample and an appeal to authority. The author constructs a thought experiment: if all regions are empty and only the earth is created, the earth has no center or periphery, so its relation to all regions is equal. If placed anywhere, it would necessarily stay there; there is no natural tendency. The author then invokes Thabit ibn Qurra's statement that no place has a special property to attract a body. The motion of a stone to the earth's center is explained not by a natural place but by the part's inclination to its whole due to homogeneity. Several hypothetical scenarios illustrate this: if the earth is split, halves meet midway; if raised to the sun's sphere, a stone rises to it; if scattered, parts converge. Thus, the apparent natural motion is explained by attraction to similar mass, not by an inherent tendency to a specific place.

مركز (center)
محيط (periphery)
ترجيح (preference)
مرجح (preponderating factor)
طبيعة (nature)
جزء (part)
كل (whole)
جنسية (homogeneity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bodies move because they have a natural place to go to (e.g., earth seeks the center).
Correction: The passage shows that motion is due to the part seeking its whole by homogeneity, not because a place has a property to attract.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what explains the motion of a stone toward the earth's center?
A) The earthly nature seeking its natural place.
B) The part inclining to its whole which attracts it by homogeneity.
C) The free agent's specification.
D) The impossibility of being elsewhere.

Key Takeaway
Motion is not towards a fixed place but towards similar mass due to homogeneity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that motion is not toward a fixed place but toward what is similar. What 'places' in your life have you assumed were naturally good for you, but were actually just familiar? Are you being drawn toward Allah because of a genuine affinity, or just because of habit?
?
The earth's parts seek each other due to homogeneity. Your soul was created with a desire for Allah. Is there any created 'homogeneity'—a person, a status, a possession—that you are pursuing as if it were your ultimate whole? What would it look like to let your heart be drawn only to its true whole?
?
The author argues that without a preponderating factor, a body cannot prefer one place over another. Reflect on a spiritual 'preponderating factor'—what weighs most heavily in your decisions when choosing between obedience and convenience? Are you letting Allah be the preponderator in your life?

The Thought Experiment: Earth Has No Natural Place
Thought experiment showing earth's relation to empty spaces is equal, negating natural place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the thought experiment that shows the impossibility of the earth having a natural place and the initial refutation of Thabit ibn Qurra's view.

Core Idea
Without a preponderator, no place is naturally preferred.

This lesson presents a foundational thought experiment in natural philosophy. Assuming all spaces were empty, would the earth have any reason to be in one particular place? The argument concludes that it would not, challenging the concept of a 'natural place' for bodies. This sets the stage for understanding the alternative explanation based on part-whole inclination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how the argument begins with a hypothetical assumption of empty spaces.
2
Understand the assumption
The argument assumes all spaces empty and then creates the earth alone. Its relation to all spaces is equal because there is no center or periphery.
3
Grasp the conclusion
If placed in any space, the earth would stay there without moving, because there is no preponderator to choose one place over another. This refutes the idea that the earth naturally seeks its place.
4
Recognize Thabit's view
Thabit ibn Qurra claimed that no place has a special property that makes a particular body seek it. The passage presents his view as a counterpoint.
This sets up the refutation in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثامن : إثبات الهيولى في الجسم فرضت الأحياز كلها خالية عن الأجسام ثم فرض أنه خلق الأرض وحدها كان نسبتها إلى الأحياز كلها سواء إذ ليس ثمة مركز ولا محيط وإذا جعلت الأرض بأسرها في أي حيز اتفق وجب أن تقف فيه ولا تنتقل منه إلى غيره، لاستحالة الترجيح بلا يتوهم من أن الأرض طالبة للمكان الذي هي فيه باطل كما قال به ثابت بن قرة فإنه قال : ليس لشيء من الأمكنة حال يخص به دون غيره أن جسماً معيناً طالب له بطبعه دون ما عداه وإذا رمينا مدرة إلى فوق فإنما تعود المدرة إلى مركز الأرض لا لأن الطبيعة الأرضية طالبة له كما توهم، بل لأن الجزء مائل إلى كله الذي يجذبه بعلة الجنسية ولو جعل الأرض نصفين وجعل كل نصف في جانب آخر لكان طلب كل منهما مساوياً لطلب صاحبه حتى يلتقيا في وسط المسافة التي بينهما ولو فرض أن الأرض كلها رفعت إلى فلك فيه الآن الشمس، ثم أطلق من المكان الذي هي حجر لارتفع ذلك الحجر إليها لطلبه للأمر العظيم الذي هو شبيهه ، ولو فرض أنها تقطعت وتفرقت في جوانب العالم ثم أطلقت أجزاؤها، لكان يتوجه بعضها إلى بعض ويقف حيث يتهيأ تلاقيها . قال : حيث أنه مخصص بالحيز أيضاً مفروض خلوه عنه وإن كان مفروضاً معه من حيث أنه موجود . خلاصته ما في الشفاء يمكن توهم الجنس خالياً عن جميع ما لا يكون مقوماً لماهيته ووجوده، ولا يمكن توهم خلوه عن مكان معين، فلا بد من استناده إلى أمر لا يمكن خلوه عنه . قوله : ( لو فرضت الأحياز إلخ) هذا إنما يصح على رأي القائلين بالبعد، وأما عند أصحاب السطح فلا يمكن ذلك إذ عند عدم الإحساس يتعدى الأحياز . قوله : ( الذي يجذبه ) إشارة إلى أن العود معلل بميل الحيز ولذا كان المدرة الكبير أسرع من الصغير ويجذبه الكل، ولذا كانت حركته سريعة عند القرب من الأرض . قوله : (باطل) إذ المفروض أن الأحياز خالية عن الأجسام ثم خلقت الأرض بحيث لم يكن هناك محيط ولا مركز فحينئذ يلزم أن تكون نسبة الأرض إلى الأحياز كلها على السواء، فلم يتصور أن تكون طالبة للمكان الذي هي فيه . قوله : ( لأن الجزء مائل إلى كله ) ظاهره يدل على أن الكل موجود بالفعل، والجزء يميل إليه، وهذا باطل إذ الكل إنما يحصل بعد وصول الجزء إليه . فالمراد أن الأقل يميل إلى الأكثر حتى يصل إليه، فيحصل الكل أو المراد أن الجزء يميل إلى الاجتماع حتى يحصل الكل. قوله : ( ولو فرض أنها تقطعت وتفرقت إلخ) فلو فرض جزء في وسط الأجزاء بحيث يكون ميله إلى سائر الأجزاء على السواء، لزم أن يكون ذلك الجزء واقفا في موضعه حتى يجتمع سائر الأجزاء معه أو بعرض غلبة بعض الأجزاء على البعض الآخر، وأياً ما كان فلم يلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح ولأن كل جزء يطلب جميع الأجزاء طلباً واحداً ومن المحال أن يلقى الجزء الواحد كل جزء لا جرم طلب أن يكون قربه من جميع الأجزاء قرباً متساوياً، وهذا هو طلب الوسط، ثم إن جميع الأجزاء شأنه هذا، فلزم من ذلك استدارة الأرض وكرويتها، وأن يكون كل جزء منها طالباً للمركز. هكذا نقل عنه في المباحث المشرقية
The first observation - the eighth section: Establishing prime matter in the body. If all spaces are assumed empty of bodies, then it is assumed that the earth alone is created, its relation to all spaces would be equal, since there is neither center nor periphery. And if the earth is placed entirely in any space it happens, it must stop there and not move from it to another, because preponderance without a preponderator is impossible. The imagination that the earth seeks the place it is in is false, as Thabit ibn Qurra said: no place has a state that distinguishes it from another such that a particular body seeks it by nature rather than another. And if we throw a clod upward, it returns to the center of the earth, not because the earthly nature seeks it as imagined, but because the part inclines to its whole which attracts it by the cause of homogeneity. And if the earth were divided into two halves and each half placed on opposite sides, each would seek the other equally until they meet at the midpoint of the distance between them. And if it were assumed that the entire earth were raised to a sphere where the sun is now, then a stone released from that place would rise to it seeking the great thing that is similar to it. And if it were assumed that it broke up and scattered in the regions of the world, then its parts were released, they would head toward each other and stop where their meeting is prepared. He said: 'Because it is also specified by a space, assumed empty of it, even if it is assumed with it in terms of its existence.' Its summary from al-Shifa: one can imagine the genus devoid of everything that does not constitute its essence and existence, but one cannot imagine it devoid of a specific place; so it must rely on something that cannot be devoid of it. His statement: (If spaces are assumed etc.) This is only valid according to those who hold dimensions, but according to the proponents of surfaces it is not possible, because in the absence of sensation spaces are exceeded. His statement: (which attracts it) indicates that return is caused by the inclination of space, hence a large clod is faster than a small one, and the whole attracts it, so its motion is faster near the earth. His statement: (false) because it is assumed that spaces are empty of bodies, then the earth was created such that there was neither periphery nor center; then the earth's relation to all spaces is equal, so it is not imagined to seek the place it is in. His statement: (because the part inclines to its whole) Its apparent meaning indicates that the whole exists actually and the part inclines to it, but this is false because the whole only comes about after the part reaches it. So the intended meaning is that the lesser inclines to the greater until it reaches it, so the whole is obtained; or the intended meaning is that the part inclines to assembly until the whole is obtained. His statement: (And if it were assumed that it broke up and scattered etc.) If a part is assumed in the middle of the parts such that its inclination to all other parts is equal, then that part must remain stationary in its place until the other parts gather with it, or it may be subject to the dominance of some parts over others. In any case, preponderance without a preponderator does not follow. And because each part seeks all parts with a single seeking, and it is impossible for the single part to meet every part, it necessarily seeks that its proximity to all parts be equal, and this is seeking the center. Then, since all parts are like this, it follows that the earth is spherical and round, and that every part of it seeks the center. Thus it is transmitted from him in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of a natural place for the earth and refutes Thabit ibn Qurra's view. The argument begins by assuming all spaces empty and the earth created alone; then the earth's relation to all spaces is equal, so it would have no reason to choose one particular place. If it were placed anywhere, it would stop there, because preponderance without a preponderator is impossible. The idea that the earth naturally seeks its place is false—this is the view of Thabit ibn Qurra, who claimed that no place has a special property that makes a particular body seek it. The passage then gives examples: a clod thrown upward returns to the center because its part inclines to the whole (the earth) by homogeneity, not because the center is naturally sought. If the earth were split into two halves, each half would seek the other equally until they meet midway. If the earth were raised to a sphere and a stone released, it would rise to the earth seeking its similar. If the earth broke apart, parts would move toward each other until they meet. These show that parts tend to aggregate. The commentary explains that the original argument from al-Mawaqif is about the necessity of a specific place. The commentator then clarifies each statement: the argument about spaces assumes dimensions (not surfaces); the return of a clod is due to inclination to the whole; the notion that the earth seeks its place is false because with equal relation there is no preference; the inclination of a part to the whole does not require the whole to already exist actually—rather, the part seeks assembly. Finally, the passage derives the spherical shape of the earth from the equal seeking of all parts for the center. The whole argument is attributed to earlier philosophical works.

Key Terms
حيز
مرجح
مكان طبيعي
ثابت بن قرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The earth naturally seeks its center because the center is a special place.
Correction: The thought experiment shows that without a preponderator, no place is special; the earth would not move to any particular location.

Checkpoint
What is the key assumption in the thought experiment?
A) The earth is moving in a straight line.
B) The spaces are empty and the earth is alone.
C) There is a center and periphery.
D) The earth seeks its natural place.

Key Takeaway
The thought experiment demonstrates that the earth does not have a natural place; it would stay wherever it is placed due to the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the thought experiment, the earth has no natural place and stays wherever it is placed. What belief do you hold about yourself—perhaps about your 'natural' role or destiny—that you assume is fixed, but is actually sustained only by Allah's will? How would you respond if that support were withdrawn?
?
The argument shows that preponderance without a preponderator is impossible. Consider an area of your life where you feel a strong preference (e.g., for a certain outcome). Can you honestly say that preference is based on a real preponderator from Allah, or is it just your own assumption? What would it mean to submit that preference to His decree?
?
Thabit ibn Qurra believed the earth seeks its place by nature. Are there 'natural' explanations you rely on to explain why things happen, rather than seeing Allah's constant action behind every event? Identify one such explanation and ask yourself: does it truly remove the need for a Preponderator, or does it merely disguise His hand?

The Part-Whole Inclination and the Spherical Earth
Examples and commentary demonstrating part-whole inclination leading to spherical earth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative explanation for the earth's shape and the return of objects to the earth, based on part-whole inclination and homogeneity, leading to the spherical shape.

Core Idea
Parts incline to their whole by homogeneity, shaping the earth as a sphere.

The previous lesson showed that the earth does not have a natural place. This lesson explores an alternative: parts of the earth incline toward each other by homogeneity, causing aggregation. Through examples like a clod thrown upward and a divided earth, the argument demonstrates that all parts seek the center, resulting in a spherical shape. This also refines Thabit ibn Qurra's view.

Guided Path
1
Read the examples
The passage presents three examples: a clod returning to earth, a divided earth seeking reunion, and a stone rising to the earth if lifted to the sun's sphere. Each illustrates the part-whole inclination.
2
Understand the cause
The cause is 'the cause of homogeneity' (علة الجنسية) — the part inclines to its whole because they share the same nature.
3
Analyze the commentary
The commentary clarifies that the whole does not need to exist actually; rather, the part seeks assembly. It also addresses the spherical conclusion: all parts end up seeking the center equally, leading to a sphere.
4
Derive the spherical shape
Since all parts seek equal proximity to each other, they naturally form a sphere. This is the proof for the earth's sphericity from natural philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثامن : إثبات الهيولى في الجسم فرضت الأحياز كلها خالية عن الأجسام ثم فرض أنه خلق الأرض وحدها كان نسبتها إلى الأحياز كلها سواء إذ ليس ثمة مركز ولا محيط وإذا جعلت الأرض بأسرها في أي حيز اتفق وجب أن تقف فيه ولا تنتقل منه إلى غيره، لاستحالة الترجيح بلا يتوهم من أن الأرض طالبة للمكان الذي هي فيه باطل كما قال به ثابت بن قرة فإنه قال : ليس لشيء من الأمكنة حال يخص به دون غيره أن جسماً معيناً طالب له بطبعه دون ما عداه وإذا رمينا مدرة إلى فوق فإنما تعود المدرة إلى مركز الأرض لا لأن الطبيعة الأرضية طالبة له كما توهم، بل لأن الجزء مائل إلى كله الذي يجذبه بعلة الجنسية ولو جعل الأرض نصفين وجعل كل نصف في جانب آخر لكان طلب كل منهما مساوياً لطلب صاحبه حتى يلتقيا في وسط المسافة التي بينهما ولو فرض أن الأرض كلها رفعت إلى فلك فيه الآن الشمس، ثم أطلق من المكان الذي هي حجر لارتفع ذلك الحجر إليها لطلبه للأمر العظيم الذي هو شبيهه ، ولو فرض أنها تقطعت وتفرقت في جوانب العالم ثم أطلقت أجزاؤها، لكان يتوجه بعضها إلى بعض ويقف حيث يتهيأ تلاقيها . قال : حيث أنه مخصص بالحيز أيضاً مفروض خلوه عنه وإن كان مفروضاً معه من حيث أنه موجود . خلاصته ما في الشفاء يمكن توهم الجنس خالياً عن جميع ما لا يكون مقوماً لماهيته ووجوده، ولا يمكن توهم خلوه عن مكان معين، فلا بد من استناده إلى أمر لا يمكن خلوه عنه . قوله : ( لو فرضت الأحياز إلخ) هذا إنما يصح على رأي القائلين بالبعد، وأما عند أصحاب السطح فلا يمكن ذلك إذ عند عدم الإحساس يتعدى الأحياز . قوله : ( الذي يجذبه ) إشارة إلى أن العود معلل بميل الحيز ولذا كان المدرة الكبير أسرع من الصغير ويجذبه الكل، ولذا كانت حركته سريعة عند القرب من الأرض . قوله : (باطل) إذ المفروض أن الأحياز خالية عن الأجسام ثم خلقت الأرض بحيث لم يكن هناك محيط ولا مركز فحينئذ يلزم أن تكون نسبة الأرض إلى الأحياز كلها على السواء، فلم يتصور أن تكون طالبة للمكان الذي هي فيه . قوله : ( لأن الجزء مائل إلى كله ) ظاهره يدل على أن الكل موجود بالفعل، والجزء يميل إليه، وهذا باطل إذ الكل إنما يحصل بعد وصول الجزء إليه . فالمراد أن الأقل يميل إلى الأكثر حتى يصل إليه، فيحصل الكل أو المراد أن الجزء يميل إلى الاجتماع حتى يحصل الكل. قوله : ( ولو فرض أنها تقطعت وتفرقت إلخ) فلو فرض جزء في وسط الأجزاء بحيث يكون ميله إلى سائر الأجزاء على السواء، لزم أن يكون ذلك الجزء واقفا في موضعه حتى يجتمع سائر الأجزاء معه أو بعرض غلبة بعض الأجزاء على البعض الآخر، وأياً ما كان فلم يلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح ولأن كل جزء يطلب جميع الأجزاء طلباً واحداً ومن المحال أن يلقى الجزء الواحد كل جزء لا جرم طلب أن يكون قربه من جميع الأجزاء قرباً متساوياً، وهذا هو طلب الوسط، ثم إن جميع الأجزاء شأنه هذا، فلزم من ذلك استدارة الأرض وكرويتها، وأن يكون كل جزء منها طالباً للمركز. هكذا نقل عنه في المباحث المشرقية
The first observation - the eighth section: Establishing prime matter in the body. If all spaces are assumed empty of bodies, then it is assumed that the earth alone is created, its relation to all spaces would be equal, since there is neither center nor periphery. And if the earth is placed entirely in any space it happens, it must stop there and not move from it to another, because preponderance without a preponderator is impossible. The imagination that the earth seeks the place it is in is false, as Thabit ibn Qurra said: no place has a state that distinguishes it from another such that a particular body seeks it by nature rather than another. And if we throw a clod upward, it returns to the center of the earth, not because the earthly nature seeks it as imagined, but because the part inclines to its whole which attracts it by the cause of homogeneity. And if the earth were divided into two halves and each half placed on opposite sides, each would seek the other equally until they meet at the midpoint of the distance between them. And if it were assumed that the entire earth were raised to a sphere where the sun is now, then a stone released from that place would rise to it seeking the great thing that is similar to it. And if it were assumed that it broke up and scattered in the regions of the world, then its parts were released, they would head toward each other and stop where their meeting is prepared. He said: 'Because it is also specified by a space, assumed empty of it, even if it is assumed with it in terms of its existence.' Its summary from al-Shifa: one can imagine the genus devoid of everything that does not constitute its essence and existence, but one cannot imagine it devoid of a specific place; so it must rely on something that cannot be devoid of it. His statement: (If spaces are assumed etc.) This is only valid according to those who hold dimensions, but according to the proponents of surfaces it is not possible, because in the absence of sensation spaces are exceeded. His statement: (which attracts it) indicates that return is caused by the inclination of space, hence a large clod is faster than a small one, and the whole attracts it, so its motion is faster near the earth. His statement: (false) because it is assumed that spaces are empty of bodies, then the earth was created such that there was neither periphery nor center; then the earth's relation to all spaces is equal, so it is not imagined to seek the place it is in. His statement: (because the part inclines to its whole) Its apparent meaning indicates that the whole exists actually and the part inclines to it, but this is false because the whole only comes about after the part reaches it. So the intended meaning is that the lesser inclines to the greater until it reaches it, so the whole is obtained; or the intended meaning is that the part inclines to assembly until the whole is obtained. His statement: (And if it were assumed that it broke up and scattered etc.) If a part is assumed in the middle of the parts such that its inclination to all other parts is equal, then that part must remain stationary in its place until the other parts gather with it, or it may be subject to the dominance of some parts over others. In any case, preponderance without a preponderator does not follow. And because each part seeks all parts with a single seeking, and it is impossible for the single part to meet every part, it necessarily seeks that its proximity to all parts be equal, and this is seeking the center. Then, since all parts are like this, it follows that the earth is spherical and round, and that every part of it seeks the center. Thus it is transmitted from him in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of a natural place for the earth and refutes Thabit ibn Qurra's view. The argument begins by assuming all spaces empty and the earth created alone; then the earth's relation to all spaces is equal, so it would have no reason to choose one particular place. If it were placed anywhere, it would stop there, because preponderance without a preponderator is impossible. The idea that the earth naturally seeks its place is false—this is the view of Thabit ibn Qurra, who claimed that no place has a special property that makes a particular body seek it. The passage then gives examples: a clod thrown upward returns to the center because its part inclines to the whole (the earth) by homogeneity, not because the center is naturally sought. If the earth were split into two halves, each half would seek the other equally until they meet midway. If the earth were raised to a sphere and a stone released, it would rise to the earth seeking its similar. If the earth broke apart, parts would move toward each other until they meet. These show that parts tend to aggregate. The commentary explains that the original argument from al-Mawaqif is about the necessity of a specific place. The commentator then clarifies each statement: the argument about spaces assumes dimensions (not surfaces); the return of a clod is due to inclination to the whole; the notion that the earth seeks its place is false because with equal relation there is no preference; the inclination of a part to the whole does not require the whole to already exist actually—rather, the part seeks assembly. Finally, the passage derives the spherical shape of the earth from the equal seeking of all parts for the center. The whole argument is attributed to earlier philosophical works.

Key Terms
جزء
كل
جنسية
مركز
استدارة
كروية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The earth's center is a special place that naturally attracts objects.
Correction: The attraction is due to homogeneity between part and whole, not a property of the center itself.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why does a large clod fall faster than a small one?
A) It has more weight.
B) It is more attracted by the whole.
C) It has a stronger inclination to the center.
D) It resists air resistance more.

Key Takeaway
The part-whole inclination based on homogeneity explains why objects return to the earth and why the earth is spherical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the part-whole inclination that drives every piece of earth to seek its source, ask yourself: What is the ultimate 'whole' that your soul is inclining toward? Are you allowing the natural pull of your fitrah to lead you to your Creator, or are you distracted by lesser inclinations? What one change can you make today to realign your desires with your true purpose?
?
The passage argues that parts do not need the whole to exist actually in order to incline toward it; rather, they seek assembly. In your own spiritual journey, are you waiting for the 'whole'—the completed vision of faith—to appear before you commit fully, or are you actively assembling your life piece by piece through obedience? Which part of your faith are you neglecting to bring to the gathering?
?
The earth's parts seek equal proximity to all others, resulting in a perfect sphere. Examine your relationships: Are you seeking balanced proximity with all of Allah's creation, or are you partial and imbalanced? How does this principle of equality challenge your attachments?

Objection and Answer: Do All Bodies Have Indeterminate Place?
Objection and answer regarding natural place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that all bodies might naturally require an indeterminate space like parts of the earth, and the answer that this analogy fails because parts cannot exist as independent entities demanding a place.

Core Idea
A part of the earth is not analogous to an isolated body.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection to the theory of natural place: if parts of the earth seek an indeterminate space within the earth's whole, could all bodies behave similarly? The answer offered by the commentator is subtle, and the discussion reveals important assumptions about independence and external specification.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Note how the objector uses the example of a part of the earth to argue that every body might naturally require an indeterminate space, with external factors specifying a specific place.
The objector's key move is to draw an analogy between any body and a part of the earth.
2
Identify the answer's key claim
The answer says the discussion is about a body stripped of all external matters. It then argues that a part of the earth, left to its nature, does not remain an independent existent demanding a place because it does not complete itself as a whole.
The answer denies the analogy by asserting that parts are not self-subsistent.
3
Understand the commentary's critique
The commentary points out that the answer only works if the objection's basis is taken in a certain way. It also questions whether a body can truly be free of all external factors in reality.
The critique shows that the answer is not as strong as it seems.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون كل جسم بحيث لو خلي وطبعه لكان يقتضي حيزاً مبهماً ككل جزء من الأرض فإنه يطلب حيزاً مبهماً من أجزاء حيز الأرض ويكون المخصص لذلك الجسم بحيز معين أمرا من خارج كما أن مخصص جزء الأرض بحيز معين أمر خارج عنه ؟ وقد يجاب بأن الكلام فيما إذا خلي الجسم وطبعه وجرد عن جميع الأمور الخارجة عنه، وأما جزء الأرض فإنه لو خلي وطبعه، لا تصل بكله فلم يبق موجوداً منفرداً مقتضياً للمكان وما دام موجوداً على حده فإنه لا يخلو عن قاسر قوله : ( وبالجملة إلخ ) لفظ الجملة ليس في موقعه لأنه منع الملازمة المذكورة بسند أنه بسبب وجد مكاناً آخر، وهو أن يكون حال كل جسم كحال جزء الأرض. قوله : ( وقد يجاب إلخ ) هذا الجواب إنما يفيد إذا جعل قوله: لكل جزء من الأرض إجمالياً مذكوراً بعد إيراد المنع، وأما إذا جعل سنداً للمنع كما هو الظاهر فلا يفيد إذ هو أعني هذا الجواب يكون حينئذ كلاماً على السند مع أنه يمكن أن يقال : لا نسلم أن فرض خلو الجسم عن جميع الأمور الخارجية عنه يقتضي خلوه في نفس الأمر.
And in summary, why is it not permissible that every body, when left to its nature, necessitates an indeterminate space like every part of the earth? For it seeks an indeterminate space of the parts of the earth's space, and the specifier of that body to a specific space is an external matter, just as the specifier of a part of the earth to a specific space is external to it? And it may be answered that the discussion is about when the body is left to its nature and stripped of all external matters. As for a part of the earth, if it were left to its nature, it does not [complete?] by its whole, so it does not remain an isolated existent demanding a place, and as long as it exists on its own, it is not free from a coercer. His statement: (And in summary etc.) The phrase 'in summary' is not in its proper place because it is a denial of the aforementioned implication with the basis that it [the body] finds another place because it is that the state of every body is like the state of a part of the earth. His statement: (And it may be answered etc.) This answer only benefits if his statement 'for every part of the earth' is taken as a general statement mentioned after the objection. But if it is taken as a basis for the objection, as is apparent, then it does not benefit, because this answer then becomes speech about the basis, and it can be said: we do not grant that assuming the body free of all external matters necessitates its freedom in actual fact.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding natural place. The objector says: why can't every body, when left to its nature, require an indeterminate space like a part of the earth? The part of the earth seeks an indeterminate part of the earth's overall space, and the specifier that assigns it a specific place is an external factor. So similarly, the specifier for any body could be external. The answer: the discussion is about a body stripped of all external factors. But a part of the earth, if left to its nature, does not exist as a complete whole by itself; it is not an independent existent that demands a place. As long as it exists separately, it is subject to a coercer (e.g., the whole earth's attraction). The commentary then clarifies: the objector's 'in summary' is misplaced because it denies an implication by citing a basis (the part's state). The answer is useful only if the statement about the part is taken as a general point after the objection; but if it is the basis itself, the answer is circular. Additionally, one could object that assuming a body free of all external factors does not guarantee it is actually free in reality.

مخصص

خلي وطبعه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the answer completely resolves the objection.
Correction: The commentary shows the answer only works under a specific reading of the objection, and even then, it can be further challenged.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the objection, why does a part of the earth not serve as a valid analogy for an isolated body?
A) Because the part is not left to its nature.
B) Because the part cannot exist as an independent whole demanding a place.
C) Because the part is smaller than the whole.
D) Because the part is subject to external specification only.

Key Takeaway
The objection that all bodies might have indeterminate place like earth's parts is met with an answer that highlights the unique status of parts, but the commentary shows the answer is not fully decisive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used an analogy to defend a belief without examining whether the two cases are truly comparable? Will you now return to that belief and test its foundation?
?
The commentary shows that even a well-intentioned answer can have logical weaknesses if not carefully framed. Is there a teaching or doctrine you accept uncritically because the answer 'sounds right'? Are you willing to scrutinize its logical structure, even if it makes you uncomfortable?
?
When you assume something is 'left to its nature,' do you actually consider whether that thing can ever exist without external factors? How does ignoring reality affect your understanding of yourself and your dependence on Allah?

Argument for Earth's Sphericity and Objection on Indeterminate Place
Argument for sphericity with objection and response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that the earth is spherical because its parts naturally seek the center, and how an objection about bodies seeking indeterminate places is answered.

Core Idea
The earth's sphericity follows from the natural inclination of its parts toward a common center.

This passage presents a fascinating argument from classical kalam for the spherical shape of the earth. It begins by deducing sphericity from the behavior of parts seeking the center, then anticipates an objection: why shouldn't every body, like a part of earth, seek an indeterminate place when left to nature? The response clarifies the unique status of a part of earth, which cannot exist independently. Let's explore the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then its English translation. Notice the logical flow: argument, objection, response.
The passage uses technical terms like 'طبيعي' (natural) and 'قسر' (compulsion).
2
Identify the core argument for sphericity
The argument claims that each part of a body seeks all other parts equally, but since one part cannot meet all parts, it seeks a position equidistant from all—the center. This leads to all parts seeking the center, resulting in a spherical shape.
This is a teleological argument based on natural inclination.
3
Examine the objection
The objection asks: why can't every body, like a part of earth, seek an indeterminate place (i.e., not a specific location) when left to nature, with a specific place determined by an external factor?
This challenges the necessity of a specific natural place for each body.
4
Analyze the response
The response states that the discussion assumes the body is isolated from external factors. A part of the earth, if isolated, would not exist as an independent whole; it is always under compulsion (qasr). Therefore, it cannot serve as a counterexample.
The key point: a part of earth is not a simple body by itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأن كل جزء يطلب جميع الأجزاء طلباً واحداً ومن المحال أن يلقى الجزء الواحد كل جزء لا جرم طلب أن يكون قربه من جميع الأجزاء قرباً متساوياً، وهذا هو طلب الوسط، ثم إن جميع الأجزاء شأنه هذا، فلزم من ذلك استدارة الأرض وكرويتها، وأن يكون كل جزء منها طالباً للمركز. هكذا نقل عنه في المباحث المشرقية، ( وبالجملة، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون كل جسم) بحيث ( لو خُلّي وطبعه لكان يقتضي حيزاً مبهماً ككل جزء من الأرض) فإنه يطلب حيزاً مبهماً من أجزاء حيز الأرض ( ويكون المخصص) لذلك الجسم بحيز معين ( أمرا من خارج) كما أن مخصص جزء الأرض بحيز معين أمر خارج عنه ؟ وقد يجاب بأن الكلام فيما إذا خلي الجسم وطبعه وجرد عن جميع الأمور الخارجة عنه، وأما جزء الأرض فإنه لو خلي وطبعه، لا تصل بكله فلم يبق موجوداً منفرداً مقتضياً للمكان وما دام موجوداً على حده فإنه لا يخلو عن قاسر
And because every part seeks all parts with a single seeking, and it is impossible for one part to meet every part, inevitably it seeks to have its nearness to all parts equal, and this is the seeking of the middle. Then all parts have this same property, so from that follows the roundness and sphericity of the earth, and that every part of it seeks the center. Thus it is transmitted from him in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyyah. (In sum, why is it not permissible that every body) be such that (if left to its nature it would demand an indeterminate site like every part of the earth) for it seeks an indeterminate site from the parts of the earth's site, and (the determiner) of that body to a specific site (is something external) just as the determiner of a part of the earth to a specific site is something external? And it may be answered that the discussion is when the body is left to its nature and stripped of all external matters. As for a part of the earth, if it were left to its nature, it would not [؟] in its entirety, so it would not remain an existing individual demanding place, and as long as it exists on its own, it is not free from a compeller.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is an argument followed by an objection and a response. The argument begins by stating that each part of a body seeks all other parts with a single inclination, but since it cannot meet all parts simultaneously, it must seek a position equidistant from all—the center. This leads to the earth being spherical. A quote from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyyah is cited. Then an objection is raised: why cannot every body be like a part of earth, seeking an indeterminate site when left to nature, with a specific place determined by an external factor? The response distinguishes that the discussion assumes the body is isolated from external factors. A part of earth, when left to nature, cannot exist independently as a whole; it is always subject to a compeller, so it does not serve as a counterexample.

Key Terms

قسر
حيز
مخصص
كروية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a part of earth has a natural place even when isolated, because it seems to seek a specific location within the earth.
Correction: The response shows that a part of earth, if left to nature alone, would not exist as a separate entity; it is always subject to external compulsion, so its situation does not apply to a simple body considered in isolation.

Checkpoint
How does the response refute the objection that every body could seek an indeterminate place like a part of the earth?
A) It argues that a part of the earth, if left to its nature, would not exist independently and remains subject to a compeller.
B) It claims that the earth's spherical shape is the only possible natural shape.
C) It states that all bodies must have a specific determiner from outside.
D) It denies that a part of the earth seeks the center.

Key Takeaway
The sphericity of the earth is deduced from the natural inclination of its parts toward the center, and the objection about indeterminate place fails because a part of earth is not an independent body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the natural inclination of the earth's parts toward the center, ask yourself: What is the 'center' your heart is naturally inclined toward when you are stripped of all external influences? Is it Allah, or something else? Commit to removing one external distraction this week that pulls you away from that center.
?
The objection suggests that a part left to nature demands an indeterminate place. Reflect on your own life: if you were completely alone without society, possessions, or comforts, would your soul still be directed toward Allah, or would it become lost and indeterminate? Write down one area where you rely on external things to feel oriented, and make a concrete plan to depend more on Allah.
?
In this passage, the determiner (mukhassis) is external to the body. What external determinants are shaping your spiritual direction right now—fear of people, desire for status, or the command of Allah? Answer honestly, then recite a dua asking Allah to make your natural inclination solely toward Him.

Proof That a Single Body Cannot Have Two Natural Places
Proof that a single body cannot have two natural places by examining three possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single body cannot have two natural places through a proof by cases that eliminates all possibilities.

Core Idea
A body's nature pulls it to only one place; two would cause contradiction.

This lesson examines a key proof in natural philosophy: that a simple body has only one natural place. The argument considers all possibilities when a body is in one of two supposed natural places or forced outside both, showing that only one can be natural.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the overall argument. Notice the structured case analysis.
The passage uses a three-case proof structure.
2
Identify the three cases
Find the three main cases: (1) body in one place and seeks the other, (2) body in one place and does not seek the other, (3) body outside both by force and then freed. In each case, note the conclusion.
3
Understand why each leads to contradiction
For each case, see how the assumption of two natural places is contradicted. The third case has three subcases, all impossible.
Focus on the principle that nature seeks its place when left free.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
After all cases are impossible, the only logical conclusion is that a body has exactly one natural place. This is a foundational point in physics and kalam.
This proof supports the broader theory of natural motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( فرعان ) على أن لكل جسم مكاناً طبيعياً . ( الأول : لا يكون لجسم) واحد ( حيزان طبيعيان فإنه إذا كان في أحدهما ، فإن طلب الآخر فهذا المكان الذي هو فيه الآن ليس طبيعياً له ) لأنه هارب عنه طالب لغيره ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يطلب الآخر حال كونه في أحدهما ( فالآخر ليس طبيعياً له ) لأنه ليس طالباً له حين ما خلي وطبعه (و) أيضاً إذا كان الجسم ( خارجاً عنهما ) بالقسر ثم خلي وطبعه ( فإما أن يتوجه إليهما معاً (وهو محال ظاهر فيما إذا لم يكونا من المكان القسري في جهة واحدة ( أو لا ) يتوجه إلى واحد منهما فليس شيء منهما طبيعياً أو) يتوجه (إلى أحدهما) فقط ( فالآخر ليس طبيعياً له والكل محال، فالمكان الطبيعي واحد.
(Two branches) on the basis that every body has a natural place. (The first: It is not for a single body to have two natural places; for if it is in one of them, and it seeks the other, then this place in which it is now is not natural for it) because it flees from it and seeks another. (Otherwise) i.e., if it does not seek the other while it is in one of them, (then the other is not natural for it) because it does not seek it when left to its nature. (And) also, if the body is (outside both) by compulsion and then left to its nature, (then either it directs itself to both together—which is clearly impossible if they are not from a compulsory place in one direction—or it does not direct itself to either, so neither is natural; or it directs itself to one only, so the other is not natural for it. All are impossible, so the natural place is one.)
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a formal proof that a single body cannot have two natural places. It presents three cases. First, if the body is in one of the two supposed natural places and seeks the other, then the first is not its natural place because it flees it. Second, if it does not seek the other while in the first, then the other is not natural since nature would necessitate seeking it when free. Third, if the body is forcibly placed outside both and then left to nature, three sub-cases occur: it seeks both simultaneously (impossible unless they are in the same direction and compulsory), it seeks neither (so neither is natural), or it seeks only one (so the other is not natural). All possibilities lead to contradiction, so the assumption of two natural places is false. Thus, a body has only one natural place.

Key Terms
حيزان طبيعيان
مكان طبيعي
طلب
خلي وطبعه
قسر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a body could have two natural places if they are in opposite directions, but the third case shows this leads to absurd simultaneous seeking.
Correction: The proof shows that if a body is forced outside both and then freed, it cannot seek both unless they are essentially the same place, so two distinct natural places are impossible.

Checkpoint
In the third case where the body is forced outside both supposed natural places and then left to nature, which scenario does the author consider clearly impossible?
A) The body seeks both places simultaneously when they are not from a compulsory place in one direction.
B) The body seeks neither place.
C) The body seeks only one place.
D) The body remains stationary without seeking.

Key Takeaway
A simple body has exactly one natural place; assuming two leads to logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Just as a simple body has only one natural place, your heart was created with a single ultimate orientation—towards your Creator. Yet you feel pulled in many directions: towards approval, comfort, success. In this very moment, can you name one 'false natural place' that you have been seeking as if it were your ultimate end? What would it cost you to abandon that pursuit and return to your true home?
?
The proof shows that assuming two natural places leads to absurdity in every scenario. Consider your life: where are you trying to serve two masters or hold two contradictory ultimate goals? Be specific—what is the contradiction? Is your soul in flight because you are in the wrong place? What step can you take today to end that contradiction and rest in your single true purpose?
?
If a body outside its natural place by compulsion is restless until it returns, are you sensing a similar restlessness in your life? What external compulsion (fear, habit, desire) is keeping you from aligning with your nature—your fitrah—which was created to know and worship Allah alone? What does it mean to 'release yourself to your nature' right now?

Composite Body's Natural Place: The Rule of the Dominant Element
Sanusi states the general rule and criterion for composite's natural place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the natural place of a composite body is determined by the dominant simple element, and that when elements are equal in force, the composite has no natural place.

Core Idea
A composite's natural place is where its most powerful component belongs.

This lesson establishes the foundational rule for the natural place of composite bodies. Building on the concept of simple bodies having natural places, we now consider mixtures. The author explains that the composite follows the dominant element, but when elements are equal, the situation changes. We will also learn that the relevant measure of dominance is force, not volume.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general rule
Read the opening statement that the composite's place is the place of the dominant simple. Note the phrase 'يقهَر ما عداه'.
2
Consider the exception of equal simples
The passage then considers when all simples are equal. What happens then? The author notes an objection: if removed, it wouldn't return, so it's not a natural place.
3
Learn the criterion for equality
Two simples equal in size may differ in force. The conclusion is that equality in force is what matters, not size.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثاني ) من الفرعين : الجسم البسيط له مكان طبيعي كما عرفت و مكان المركب) أي مكانه الطبيعي ) مكان البسيط الغالب فيه ( فإنه يقهر ما عداه ويجذبه إلى حيزه، فيكون الكل إذا خلي وطبعه طالباً لذلك الحيز (وإن تساوت البسائط) كلها (فيه، فالمكان الطبيعي له ( هو الذي اتفق وجوده فيه لعدم أولوية الغير. وفيه نظر لأنه لو أخرج المركب المتساوي البسائط ( عنه ) أي عن ذلك المكان الذي اتفق وجوده فيه (لم يعد إليه طبعاً) بل سكن أينما أخرج لعدم المرجح) فلا يكون ذلك المكان طبيعياً (و) البسيطان (المتساويان في ( الحجم و ( المقدار قد يختلفان في القوة ) فإنه إذا أخذ مقداران متساويان من الأرض والنار فربما كان اقتضاء الأرضية للميل السافل أقوى من اقتضاء النارية للميل الصاعد أو بالعكس، بل ربما كان الناقص في المقدار أقوى في القوة ) فالمعتبر) من التساوي في بسائط المركب ( هو التساوي في القوة دون الحجم والمقدار، وقد يفصل هاهنا ويقال : المركب إن تركب من بسيطين، فإن كان أحدهما غالباً في القوة، وكان هناك ما يحفظ الامتزاج، فالمركب ينجذب بالطبع إلى مكان الغالب، وإن تساويا فإما أن يكون كل منهما ممانعاً للآخر في حركته أولا، فإن لم يتمانعا افترقا ولم يجتمعا إلا بقاسر، وإن تمانعا مثل أن تكون النار من تحت والأرض من فوق، فإما أن يكون بعد كل منهما عن حيزه مساوياً لبعد الآخر أو لا. فعلى الأول يتقاومان فيحتبس المركب في ذلك المكان لا سيما إذا كان في الحد المشترك بين حيزيهما. وعلى الثاني ينجذب المركب إلى حيز ما هو أقرب إلى حيزه لأن الحركات الطبيعية تشتد عند القرب من أحيازها وتفتر عند البعد وإن تركب من ثلاثة فإن غلب أحدها حصل المركب بطبعه في حيز الغالب كما مر وإن تساوت فإن كانت الثلاثة متجاورة كالأرض والماء والهواء، حصل المركب في حيز العنصر الوسط كالماء وإن كانت متباينة كالأرض والماء والنار، حصل المركب في الوسط أيضاً لتساوي الجذب من الجانبين ولأن الأرض والماء، وإن اختلفا في الماهية لكنهما يشتركان في الميل إلى أسفل، فهما يغلبان النار بهذا الاعتبار، وإن تركب من أربعة فإن كانت متساوية حصل المركب في الوسط، وإلا ففي حيز الغالب هذا كله بالنظر إلى ما يقتضيه التركيب إذا خلا عن مقتض آخر يمنع العناصر عن أفعالها فإنه يجوز أن يحصل للمركب صورة نوعية تعين له مكان البسيط المغلوب والله أعلم . قوله : ( وقد يفصل إلخ ) منقول من المباحث المشرقية . قوله : ( وكان هناك إلخ) وإن لم يكن المزاج قوياً بطل التركيب، فإن كل جزء له مكان قوله : ( وإن تساويا ) أي في القوة . قوله : (افترقا ولم يجتمعا إلخ) أي لا يحصل التركيب إلا بقاسر يقسر سيما على الاجتماع فعند الاجتماع له مكان قسري، وإذا خلي وطبعه لا يبقى المركـ قوله : ( في حيز العنصر (الوسط) أي في وسط حيز العنصر الوسط.
(The second) of the two branches: the simple body has a natural place as you know, and the place of the composite (i.e. its natural place) is the place of the simple element dominant in it (for it overpowers what other than it and draws it to its region, so the whole, when released and left to its nature, seeks that region. And if the simples are all equal in it, its natural place is the one in which it happens to exist due to no priority of another. And there is a consideration in it: because if the composite of equal simples is taken out from it, i.e. from that place in which it happens to exist, it would not return to it by nature, but would settle wherever it is taken out due to lack of a preferrer, so that place would not be natural. And two simples equal in size and magnitude may differ in force, for if two equal amounts of earth and fire are taken, perhaps the earth's requirement for downward inclination is stronger than the fire's requirement for upward inclination or vice versa, and perhaps the deficient in magnitude is stronger in force. So the criterion for equality in the simples of the composite is equality in force, not in size and magnitude. And here a detailed classification may be given, and it is said: The composite, if composed of two simples, then if one of them is dominant in force, and there is something that preserves the mixture, the composite is attracted by nature to the place of the dominant. If they are equal, then either each of them opposes the other in its movement or not. If they do not oppose each other, they separate and do not come together except by a coercer. If they do oppose each other, such as fire being below and earth above, then either the distance of each from its region is equal to the distance of the other or not. In the first case, they counterbalance each other, and the composite is detained in that place, especially if it is on the common boundary between the two regions. In the second case, the composite is attracted to the region closer to its region because natural motions become stronger near their regions and weaker when far. And if composed of three, if one dominates, the composite obtains by nature in the region of the dominant as before. If they are equal, then if the three are contiguous, like earth, water, and air, the composite obtains in the region of the middle element like water. If they are disparate, like earth, water, and fire, the composite obtains in the middle as well due to equal attraction from the two sides, and because earth and water, though different in essence, share the inclination downward, so they overcome fire in this respect. And if composed of four, if they are equal, the composite obtains in the middle; otherwise, in the region of the dominant. All this is considered from what the composition requires when free from another requirement that prevents the elements from their actions, for it is possible that a specific form occurs for the composite that determines for it the place of the subdued simple. And God knows best. His saying: (And a detailed classification may be given, etc.) is transmitted from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya. His saying: (And if there is something, etc.) and if the mixture is not strong, the composition is invalid, for every part has a place. His saying: (And if they are equal) i.e. in force. His saying: (They separate and do not come together, etc.) i.e. composition does not occur except by a coercer that forces it especially to come together, so upon coming together it has a forced place, and if released and left to its nature, the composite does not remain. His saying: (In the region of the middle element) i.e. in the middle of the region of the middle element.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the natural place of composite bodies. It first states the general rule: the composite's natural place is the place of the dominant simple element. If the elements are equal in force, the composite has no natural place but only a place of occurrence. An objection notes that if removed, it would not return, so that place is not natural. The criterion for equality is force, not size. The passage then provides a detailed classification: for a composite of two elements, if one is dominant, it goes to its place; if equal, they either oppose or not, leading to separation or counterbalancing. For three elements, the middle element's region or the midpoint is determined. For four, the middle or dominant region. This is all assuming no other requirement like a specific form. The commentary notes the source and clarifies that mixture preservation is necessary, and that without it, composition fails. The key is that natural place depends on force, not volume.

البسيط الغالب
القوة
الحجم
المقدار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming equality in size implies equality in force.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that two equal-sized elements may differ in force, and force is the criterion.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what determines the natural place of a composite when elements are not equal?
A) The average of their places.
B) The place of the dominant element.
C) The place of the element with greatest volume.
D) The place of the element with least force.

Key Takeaway
The natural place of a composite body is that of the dominant element in force, not necessarily in size.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the dominant element in the composite of your own life—what one desire, goal, or habit overpowers all others and draws you to its 'region'? Are you conscious of it, or does it act without your reflection?
?
If your life's 'simples' were equal in force—your ambitions, duties, and relationships each pulling with equal strength—would you have a natural direction? Or would you simply settle wherever you happen to be, with no innate urge to return to any particular state? What does that suggest about the need for a dominant purpose?
?
Sanusi says equality is in force, not size or magnitude. What 'small' element in your life—perhaps a seemingly insignificant practice or relationship—might actually possess great force, shaping your direction more than larger concerns? Will you let it be your dominant element?

Detailed Classification: Composite of Two Elements
Detailed classification of two-element composite's natural place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed classification for a composite of two elements: dominance, equal with opposition (counterbalancing or attraction to closer region), and equal without opposition (separation).

Core Idea
When two elements are equal in force, they either oppose and counterbalance, or separate unless forced.

Building on the general rule, the author now provides a detailed classification for composites of two simples. Depending on dominance and mutual opposition, the composite behaves differently. We will explore each case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases
The passage breaks down the two-element composite into: one dominant, equal with opposition, equal without opposition.
2
Understand the dominant case
If one element is dominant and mixture is preserved, the composite goes to its place.
3
Understand equal with opposition
If equal and opposed, they either counterbalance if distances equal, or the composite goes to the closer region.
4
Understand equal without opposition
If equal and not opposed, they separate and only come together by coercion.

Source Text

(The second) of the two branches: the simple body has a natural place as you know, and the place of the composite (i.e. its natural place) is the place of the simple element dominant in it (for it overpowers what other than it and draws it to its region, so the whole, when released and left to its nature, seeks that region. Commentary

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that equal elements always produce a stable composite.

Checkpoint
What happens when two equal elements oppose each other and distances are equal?
A) They separate.

D) The composite moves to the closer region.

Key Takeaway
For a two-element composite, if elements are equal and opposed, the composite detains or moves to the closer region; if not opposed, they separate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of two conflicting tendencies in your own soul — one pulling you toward worldly distractions and the other toward spiritual growth. Which force is dominant in you right now? If they are equal, are you being 'detained' at a standstill (counterbalance) or are you separating into inconsistency? What external force ( قاسر ) might be needed to bring coherence?
?
The passage says natural motions become stronger near their regions. Consider your own journey toward closeness to Allah. Are you feeling your spiritual 'motion' strengthening as you approach your goal, or are you far away and moving weakly? What one concrete action can you take today to reduce the 'distance' to your Lord?
?
Imagine your heart as a composite of good and evil inclinations. According to this lesson, if one is dominant and 'the mixture is preserved', you are naturally attracted to that element. What is preserving your current mixture? Is it faith, habit, or fear? Are you satisfied with the direction you are being pulled in?

Composite of Three and Four Elements: Middle and Dominance
Classification for three and four element composites and the role of specific form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the natural place of composites with three or four elements is determined, including the role of contiguity and the possibility of a specific form overriding the elemental tendencies.

Core Idea
For three or four equal elements, the composite tends to the middle, but a specific form can override.

The detailed classification continues to composites of three and four elements. The author considers contiguity and the shared inclinations of elements. Finally, he notes that all this applies when no specific form intervenes, which can assign a different natural place.

Guided Path
1
Case of three elements
If one dominates, standard rule applies. If equal, consider contiguity: contiguous -> middle element's region; disparate -> middle point.
2
Case of four elements
If equal, middle; otherwise, dominant's region.
3
Important qualification
All above is when no specific form intervenes; a specific form can assign a different place.

ذلك المكان لا سيما إذا كان في الحد المشترك بين حيزيهما. وعلى الثاني ينجذب المركب إلى حيز ما هو أقرب إلى حيزه لأن الحركات الطبيعية تشتد عند القرب من أحيازها وتفتر عند البعد وإن تركب من ثلاثة فإن غلب منقول من المباحث المشرقية . قوله : ( وكان هناك إلخ) وإن لم يكن المزاج قوياً بطل التركيب، فإن كل جزء له مكان قوله : ( وإن تساويا ) أي في القوة . قوله : (افترقا ولم يجتمعا إلخ) أي لا يحصل التركيب إلا
