(The second) of the two branches: the simple body has a natural place as you know, and the place of the composite (i.e. its natural place) is the place of the simple element dominant in it (for it overpowers what other than it and draws it to its region, so the whole, when released and left to its nature, seeks that region. And if the simples are all equal in it, its natural place is the one in which it happens to exist due to no priority of another. And there is a consideration in it: because if the composite of equal simples is taken out from it, i.e. from that place in which it happens to exist, it would not return to it by nature, but would settle wherever it is taken out due to lack of a preferrer, so that place would not be natural. And two simples equal in size and magnitude may differ in force, for if two equal amounts of earth and fire are taken, perhaps the earth's requirement for downward inclination is stronger than the fire's requirement for upward inclination or vice versa, and perhaps the deficient in magnitude is stronger in force. So the criterion for equality in the simples of the composite is equality in force, not in size and magnitude. And here a detailed classification may be given, and it is said: The composite, if composed of two simples, then if one of them is dominant in force, and there is something that preserves the mixture, the composite is attracted by nature to the place of the dominant. If they are equal, then either each of them opposes the other in its movement or not. If they do not oppose each other, they separate and do not come together except by a coercer. If they do oppose each other, such as fire being below and earth above, then either the distance of each from its region is equal to the distance of the other or not. In the first case, they counterbalance each other, and the composite is detained in that place, especially if it is on the common boundary between the two regions. In the second case, the composite is attracted to the region closer to its region because natural motions become stronger near their regions and weaker when far. And if composed of three, if one dominates, the composite obtains by nature in the region of the dominant as before. If they are equal, then if the three are contiguous, like earth, water, and air, the composite obtains in the region of the middle element like water. If they are disparate, like earth, water, and fire, the composite obtains in the middle as well due to equal attraction from the two sides, and because earth and water, though different in essence, share the inclination downward, so they overcome fire in this respect. And if composed of four, if they are equal, the composite obtains in the middle; otherwise, in the region of the dominant. All this is considered from what the composition requires when free from another requirement that prevents the elements from their actions, for it is possible that a specific form occurs for the composite that determines for it the place of the subdued simple. And God knows best. His saying: (And a detailed classification may be given, etc.) is transmitted from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya. His saying: (And if there is something, etc.) and if the mixture is not strong, the composition is invalid, for every part has a place. His saying: (And if they are equal) i.e. in force. His saying: (They separate and do not come together, etc.) i.e. composition does not occur except by a coercer that forces it especially to come together, so upon coming together it has a forced place, and if released and left to its nature, the composite does not remain. His saying: (In the region of the middle element) i.e. in the middle of the region of the middle element.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the natural place of composite bodies. It first states the general rule: the composite's natural place is the place of the dominant simple element. If the elements are equal in force, the composite has no natural place but only a place of occurrence. An objection notes that if removed, it would not return, so that place is not natural. The criterion for equality is force, not size. The passage then provides a detailed classification: for a composite of two elements, if one is dominant, it goes to its place; if equal, they either oppose or not, leading to separation or counterbalancing. For three elements, the middle element's region or the midpoint is determined. For four, the middle or dominant region. This is all assuming no other requirement like a specific form. The commentary notes the source and clarifies that mixture preservation is necessary, and that without it, composition fails. The key is that natural place depends on force, not volume.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming the composite's natural place is always determined solely by elemental tendencies.
Correction: The passage explicitly allows a specific form to override elemental tendencies, assigning a different natural place.

For three or four equal elements, the composite tends to the middle, but a specific form can assign a different place.

The Principle of Equality in Force and Two-Simple Composites
Explains the general principle and two-simple composite behavior.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why in composite bodies, equality is measured by force rather than size, and how a composite of two elements behaves when one is predominant or when they are equal with or without opposition.

Core Idea
In mixing elements, force matters, not size.

This passage from al-Mawaqif delves into the physical principles governing composite bodies. It begins by clarifying a crucial point: when comparing the simples that make up a composite, we must consider their force, not their size. The text then systematically analyzes the case of a composite made of two elements, exploring scenarios of dominance and equality.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle of equality in force
Read the first sentence: 'The deficient in quantity is stronger in force. So what is taken into account for equality in the simples of a composite is equality in force, not size and quantity.' Understand that force is the deciding factor.
This is a fundamental principle for all cases that follow.
2
Examine the case of one predominant element
When one of the two simples is predominant in force and something preserves the mixture, the composite naturally moves to the place of the predominant element.
This is straightforward: the stronger element dictates the composite's location.
3
Study equal elements without opposition
If the two equal elements do not oppose each other's motion, they separate and only come together under external coercion (a coercer).
Without opposition, they cannot stay mixed naturally.
4
Study equal elements with opposition
If they oppose each other (e.g., fire below, earth above), then if distances to their natural places are equal, they resist and the composite stays at the common boundary. If distances are unequal, it moves to the closer place because natural motions intensify near their destinations.
This shows how balance can occur through opposition and distance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الناقص في المقدار أقوى في القوة ) فالمعتبر) من التساوي في بسائط المركب ( هو التساوي في القوة دون الحجم والمقدار، وقد يفصل هاهنا ويقال : المركب إن تركب من بسيطين، فإن كان أحدهما غالباً في القوة، وكان هناك ما يحفظ الامتزاج، فالمركب ينجذب بالطبع إلى مكان الغالب، وإن تساويا فإما أن يكون كل منهما ممانعاً للآخر في حركته أولا، فإن لم يتمانعا افترقا ولم يجتمعا إلا بقاسر، وإن تمانعا مثل أن تكون النار من تحت والأرض من فوق، فإما أن يكون بعد كل منهما عن حيزه مساوياً لبعد الآخر أو لا. فعلى الأول يتقاومان فيحتبس المركب في ذلك المكان لا سيما إذا كان في الحد المشترك بين حيزيهما. وعلى الثاني ينجذب المركب إلى حيز ما هو أقرب إلى حيزه لأن الحركات الطبيعية تشتد عند القرب من أحيازها وتفتر عند البعد وإن تركب من ثلاثة فإن غلب أحدها حصل المركب بطبعه في حيز الغالب كما مر وإن تساوت فإن كانت الثلاثة متجاورة كالأرض والماء والهواء، حصل المركب في حيز العنصر الوسط كالماء وإن كانت متباينة كالأرض والماء والنار، حصل المركب في الوسط أيضاً لتساوي الجذب من الجانبين ولأن الأرض والماء، وإن اختلفا في الماهية لكنهما يشتركان في الميل إلى أسفل، فهما يغلبان النار بهذا الاعتبار، وإن تركب من أربعة فإن كانت متساوية حصل المركب في الوسط، وإلا ففي حيز الغالب هذا كله بالنظر إلى ما يقتضيه التركيب إذا خلا عن مقتض آخر يمنع العناصر عن أفعالها فإنه يجوز أن يحصل للمركب صورة نوعية تعين له مكان البسيط المغلوب والله أعلم .
The deficient in quantity is stronger in force. So what is taken into account for equality in the simples of a composite is equality in force, not size and quantity. And it may be elaborated here and said: A composite, if it is composed of two simples, then if one of them is predominant in force and there is something that preserves the mixture, the composite is naturally attracted to the place of the predominant. If they are equal, then either each of them opposes the other in its motion or not. If they do not oppose each other, they separate and do not come together except by a coercer. If they oppose each other, such as fire being below and earth above, then either the distance of each from its natural place is equal to the distance of the other or not. In the first case, they resist each other and the composite is confined in that place, especially if it is in the common boundary between their places. In the second case, the composite is attracted to the place that is closer to its place because natural motions intensify when near their places and weaken when far. If it is composed of three, then if one of them is predominant, the composite naturally occurs in the place of the predominant as before. If they are equal, then if the three are contiguous like earth, water, and air, the composite occurs in the place of the middle element like water. If they are disparate like earth, water, and fire, the composite also occurs in the middle due to equal attraction from both sides and because earth and water, although different in essence, share in the inclination downward, so they overcome fire by this consideration. If it is composed of four, then if they are equal, the composite occurs in the middle; otherwise, in the place of the predominant. All this is with respect to what composition requires if it is free from another requirement that prevents the elements from their effects, for it is possible that a specific form may occur for the composite that assigns to it the place of the overcome simple. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about the behavior of composite bodies when mixing elements of different strengths. It begins by stating that in comparing simples of a composite, equality is measured by force (قوة), not size. The author then elaborates on different cases of composition. For two simples: if one is stronger and there is something preserving mixture (يحفظ الامتزاج), the composite moves naturally to the stronger's place. If equal: if they don't oppose, they separate unless forced; if they oppose (e.g., fire below, earth above), and distances to natural places are equal, they resist and the composite stays on the common boundary; if distances unequal, it moves to the nearer place because natural motions are stronger near their destination. For three simples: if one is dominant, it goes to that place. If equal: if the elements are contiguous, the composite goes to the middle element (e.g., water between earth and air); if disparate (earth, water, fire), it also goes to the middle because earth and water both tend downward, outweighing fire. For four equal simples, it goes to the middle; otherwise to the dominant. The conclusion notes that this applies when no other factor prevents the elements' actions, and a specific form might assign a different place. The argument uses natural physics common in classical thought.

مركب
قوة
حيز
امتزاج
قاسر

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Equality in composite is based on size or quantity.
Correction: The text explicitly states that equality is based on force, not size and quantity.

Checkpoint
In the case of two opposing elements with equal distance from their natural places, what happens to the composite?
A) It moves to the place of the heavier element.
B) It separates into its components.
C) It is confined in the common boundary between their places.
D) It moves upward.

Key Takeaway
The composite's natural place is determined by force, and when equal forces oppose with equal distances, the composite stabilizes at the common boundary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What is the dominant force in your life right now? Is it your desire for worldly gain or your longing for the Hereafter? Based on this lesson, where is your composite self being pulled—are you moving toward what dominates your heart?
?
When the forces within you are in conflict (e.g., duty vs. desire), do you find yourself in a state of resistance, like the composite at the common boundary, or do you separate internally? What external coercers are keeping you together, and are they healthy?
?
The lesson says natural motions intensify near their place. Think of your soul's yearning—what 'place' are you moving toward? Is your spiritual motion strong because you are close, or weak because you are far? What one step can you take today to draw nearer?

Composites of Three and Four Elements
Explains three and four-element composite behavior.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how composites of three or four equal elements behave based on the contiguity or disparity of the elements and the direction of their natural motions.

Core Idea
Equal elements contiguously arranged place the composite in the middle element.

Building on the principles from the previous lesson, the text now extends the analysis to composites of three and four elements. It examines cases where one element dominates and where all are equal, considering whether the elements are contiguous or disparate in their natural order. The lesson concludes with a note that specific forms can override these general rules.

Guided Path
1
Read the case of three simples: one dominant
If one of three elements is predominant, the composite goes to its place, same as before.
This is a direct extension of the two-simple case.
2
Study three equal contiguous elements
If the three are equal and contiguous (e.g., earth, water, air), the composite ends up in the place of the middle element (water).
The middle element serves as a balance point.
3
Study three equal disparate elements
If they are disparate (e.g., earth, water, fire), the composite also goes to the middle because earth and water both tend downward, overcoming fire.
Shared inclination outweighs individual differences.
4
Study four elements and the concluding remark
For four equal elements, the composite goes to the middle; otherwise to the dominant. The author notes that a specific form (صورة نوعية) may assign a different place, overriding the natural tendency.
This shows the limits of the physical analysis.

This passage is a detailed argument about the behavior of composite bodies when mixing elements of different strengths. It begins by stating that in comparing simples of a composite, equality is measured by force (قوة), not size. The author then elaborates on different cases of composition. For two simples: if one is stronger and there is something preserving mixture (يحفظ الامتزاج), the composite moves naturally to the stronger's place. If equal: if they don't oppose, they separate unless forced; if they oppose (e.g., fire below, earth above), and distances to natural places are equal, they resist and the composite stays on the common boundary; if distances unequal, it moves to the nearer place because natural motions are stronger near their destination. For three simples: if one is dominant, it goes to that place. If equal: if the elements are contiguous, the composite goes to the middle element (e.g., water between earth and air); if disparate (earth, water, fire), it also goes to the middle because earth and water both tend downward, outweighing fire. For four equal simples, it goes to the middle; otherwise to the dominant. The conclusion notes that this applies when no other factor prevents the elements' actions, and a specific form might assign a different place. The argument uses natural physics common in classical thought.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A composite of three equal disparate elements will not have a stable place because they all pull in different directions.
Correction: The text says it still goes to the middle because earth and water together overcome fire.

Key Takeaway
For equal elements in a composite, their contiguity and shared inclinations determine the composite's place, with the middle often being the equilibrium point.

The Objection of Different Star Positions in Celestial Spheres
The author addresses the objection that different star positions imply differing actions of a single celestial nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that different star positions challenge the uniformity of celestial nature, and the answer that multiple forms (such as stars, epicycles, eccentrics) can coexist in a single celestial sphere without violating its essential nature.

Core Idea
Different star positions arise from multiple forms, not a single nature acting differently.

The study of celestial spheres raises a puzzle: if the heavens are made of a single, uniform nature, how can different stars occupy different positions? In this passage, the author presents this objection and begins an answer by introducing the concept of multiple forms (such as stars, epicycles, and eccentrics) that can attach to a celestial sphere, producing visible differences without contradicting the sphere's essential nature. As you read, notice how the argument defends the possibility of a simple body hosting multiple forms.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the first sentence of the Arabic passage and its translation. It states that a single nature would produce different actions in different locations, which is problematic.
The objection assumes that a single nature must act uniformly.
2
Identify the answer given
The answer denies that the difference stems from a single nature. Instead, it attributes the difference to multiple forms that attach to the celestial sphere, such as a star, an epicycle, or an eccentric.
These forms are not part of the sphere's essential nature but are additional.
3
Understand the consequence for the sphere
The passage explains that when an additional form attaches, it separates another sphere from the first, leaving a 'notch' or 'complement' in the original sphere. This explains how different positions arise.
The first sphere retains its spherical form, but with a modification.
4
Consider the rebuttal and its incompleteness
A counter-objection states that multiple forms require different materials or dispositions, which is impossible in a simple celestial sphere. The author begins to rebut by denying the exclusivity, but the passage cuts off. Reflect on how the argument might continue.
This shows the author's strategy: to reject the premise that only material differences can account for multiple forms.

The Objection and Answer: A Single Nature and Multiple Forms
Presents an objection, answer, and counter-objection about a single nature's action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection against a single nature producing different motions is answered by appealing to multiple forms, and how the counter-objection about forms in a simple body is rebutted.

Core Idea
Difference in action arises from multiple forms, not from a single nature.

In this passage, the author presents a key objection against the view that a single nature (the celestial sphere) acts uniformly. The objection points out that the sphere moves differently in different places. The answer introduces multiple forms (epicycles, eccentrics) that inhere in the sphere, explaining the variation. A further objection questions how multiple forms can coexist in a simple body, and the answer denies the principle that such forms require different matters or dispositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the opening sentence: 'In a place of the sphere... the act of one nature differs.' Understand the problem it poses.
2
Examine the answer via multiple forms
Read the answer: the difference is due to multiple forms (epicycles, eccentrics). See how this explains the different motions.
Notice how the forms create a separate sphere causing a dent.
3
Analyze the counter-objection and rebuttal
Consider the objection against multiple forms in a simple body, and the rebuttal that denies the exclusivity principle.
The rebuttal introduces the possibility of causes from the agents.

In a place of the celestial sphere, i.e., a side of it without another, then the act of one nature in one matter would differ. And some answered this by saying that the mentioned difference is not based on one nature but on multiple forms. Indeed, the celestial sphere has acquired a specific form that necessitates the spherical shape, but another form attached to it which separated from it another sphere that is specific to it—it is a planet, an epicycle, or an eccentric. So it follows from that that there remains in the first sphere a dent or a complement conceived by the first form only. It may not be said: 'The indwelling of different forms does not occur except by difference of matters or by difference of dispositions of one matter, and that is not conceivable in the celestial sphere.' For we say to him: He may deny the exclusivity, since it is possible that the difference of forms in some simple bodies is based on causes that revert to the agents.

His saying: 'And some answered etc.' You have known that the mentioned question is a prevention of the premise of the proof, and it cannot be made a contradiction of the premise after the proof has been established to the contrary, for it is a statement of a case in which the ruling of the mentioned premise does not exist, so it is a support for the prevention and not a refutation of the mentioned premise, since no proof was mentioned for it that would be refuted by the withholding of the ruling from it. So the answer can only be by establishing the prevented premise, so its determination is that the mentioned premise is evident upon reflection, and the case that is the support of the prevention and the source of confusion in that premise is not of our concern because the acts here are multiple.

His saying: 'to causes that revert etc.' And those agents cannot be souls, because their attachment to bodies occurs after the indwelling of the specific form in them, and the relation of the intellects to the whole is the same. Prevention of this premise destroys many of the rules that they built upon this premise, as is not hidden from the investigator.

His saying: (specific to it) i.e., this other sphere is specific to that other form, and reversal is permissible in referring the two pronouns mentioned in his saying: 'specific to it'.

His saying: (it is a planet or an epicycle) i.e., this other sphere is a planet or an epicycle etc. And his saying: 'so it follows from that that there remains in the first sphere a dent etc.' It does not follow from the occurrence of this dent in the sphere that it is susceptible to breach, for their meaning is that it does not accept breach after its attainment and completion in itself, and what was mentioned of the dent was considered in its attainment and completion in itself. And his saying: 'conceived by the first form only' i.e., conceived by the form of the universal sphere, meaning that the dent and the complement have no other form than the form of the first sphere such that the difference of the act of the one nature in one matter would follow. Rather, the other form belongs to the planets, or to the epicycle, or to the eccentric, but the impossibility in that is as he mentions.

His saying: (except) for difference of matters—and this is like the difference of hyle in the universal spheres, and like the difference of elemental composite matters. And his saying: 'or for difference of dispositions of one matter'—and this is apparent in elemental things, as is well-known. And his saying: 'and that is not conceivable in the celestial sphere' i.e., in the one sphere only, even if it is conceivable in the estimated spheres as we mentioned.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer regarding the motion of a single nature. The objection argues that if a single nature (a simple substance) acts in a single matter, it cannot produce different effects in different places—here the celestial sphere moves some parts differently. The answer is that the difference is due not to a single nature but to multiple forms that inhere in the sphere, such as the form of the sphere itself and the forms of epicycles, eccentrics, etc. This answer is defended against the objection that multiple forms cannot inhabit a simple celestial body because that would require different matters or dispositions. The response is to deny the exclusivity of that principle, since it is possible that the difference of forms in some simple bodies is due to causes that revert to the agents (the intellects or movers). The commentator then explains that the objection is a 'prevention' (man') of a premise, not a contradiction, and the proper answer is to establish the premise. He also explains that the agents cannot be souls because souls attach after the body is formed, and the intellects act on the whole uniformly. He clarifies various phrases from the original text regarding the dent, the complement, and the distinction between forms.

Misconception: The objection is a direct contradiction of the premise.
Correction: The objection is actually a prevention (man') of the premise, not a refutation (naqd).

Understanding the Objection as Prevention (Man') Rather than Refutation
Commentator explains the methodological nature of the objection as man' and clarifies related points.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator insists that the objection is a prevention (man') of the premise and not a refutation (naqd), and how this affects the proper response.

Core Idea
A prevention (man') challenges a premise without proof, while a refutation (naqd) requires proof.

After presenting the objection and answer, the commentator provides a methodological analysis. He clarifies that the objection is not a direct refutation of the premise but a prevention (man')—it points to a case where the premise fails, but since the premise was not proven, this does not refute it. The proper response is to establish the premise. He also discusses why the agents cannot be souls and explains the dent metaphor.

Guided Path
1
Differentiate man' from naqd
Understand the commentator's key distinction: the objection is a prevention, not a refutation.
A prevention points to a counterexample but does not disprove the premise.
2
Study the additional clarifications
Read the discussion about agents not being souls and the dent and complement.
Note why souls cannot be the agents.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why denying this premise would destroy many rules established by the theologians.

قوله: (تختص بها أي تختص هذه الكرة الأخرى بتلك الصورة الأخرى، ويجوز التعكيس في إرجاع الضميرين المذكورين في قوله : تختص بها .

قوله : ( هي كوكب أو (تدوير) أي هذه الكرة الأخرى هي كوكب أو تدوير إلخ . وقوله : فلزم ذلك أن يبقى في الفلك الأول نقرة إلخ، لا يلزم من حصول هذه النقرة في الفلك أن يكون قابلا للخرق فإن مرادهم من ذلك أن لا يقبل الخرق بعد تحصله وتكمله في نفسه وما ذكر من النقرة، كان معتبراً في تحصله وتكمله في نفسه وقوله : متصور بالصورة الأولى فقط أي متصور بصورة الفلك الكلي، يعني أن لا يكون للنقرة ولا للمتمم صورة أخرى غير صورة الفلك الأول حتى يلزم اختلاف فعل الطبيعة الواحدة في المادة الواحدة، بل كون الصورة الأخرى للكواكب أو للتدوير أو للخارج المركز، لكن الاستحالة في ذلك كما يذكره .

قوله : (إلا) لاختلاف (المواد وهذا كاختلاف الهيولى في الأفلاك الكلية، وكاختلاف المواد العنصريات المركبة. وقوله أو لاختلاف استعدادات مادة واحدة وهذا ظاهر في العنصريات، كما هو المشهور وقوله : ولا يتصور ذلك في الفلك أي في الفلك الواحد فقط وإن كان متصوراً في الأفلاك المقدرة كما ذكرنا .
In a place of the celestial sphere, i.e., a side of it without another, then the act of one nature in one matter would differ. And some answered this by saying that the mentioned difference is not based on one nature but on multiple forms. Indeed, the celestial sphere has acquired a specific form that necessitates the spherical shape, but another form attached to it which separated from it another sphere that is specific to it—it is a planet, an epicycle, or an eccentric. So it follows from that that there remains in the first sphere a dent or a complement conceived by the first form only. It may not be said: 'The indwelling of different forms does not occur except by difference of matters or by difference of dispositions of one matter, and that is not conceivable in the celestial sphere.' For we say to him: He may deny the exclusivity, since it is possible that the difference of forms in some simple bodies is based on causes that revert to the agents.

His saying: 'And some answered etc.' You have known that the mentioned question is a prevention of the premise of the proof, and it cannot be made a contradiction of the premise after the proof has been established to the contrary, for it is a statement of a case in which the ruling of the mentioned premise does not exist, so it is a support for the prevention and not a refutation of the mentioned premise, since no proof was mentioned for it that would be refuted by the withholding of the ruling from it. So the answer can only be by establishing the prevented premise, so its determination is that the mentioned premise is evident upon reflection, and the case that is the support of the prevention and the source of confusion in that premise is not of our concern because the acts here are multiple.

His saying: 'to causes that revert etc.' And those agents cannot be souls, because their attachment to bodies occurs after the indwelling of the specific form in them, and the relation of the intellects to the whole is the same. Prevention of this premise destroys many of the rules that they built upon this premise, as is not hidden from the investigator.

His saying: (specific to it) i.e., this other sphere is specific to that other form, and reversal is permissible in referring the two pronouns mentioned in his saying: 'specific to it'.

His saying: (it is a planet or an epicycle) i.e., this other sphere is a planet or an epicycle etc. And his saying: 'so it follows from that that there remains in the first sphere a dent etc.' It does not follow from the occurrence of this dent in the sphere that it is susceptible to breach, for their meaning is that it does not accept breach after its attainment and completion in itself, and what was mentioned of the dent was considered in its attainment and completion in itself. And his saying: 'conceived by the first form only' i.e., conceived by the form of the universal sphere, meaning that the dent and the complement have no other form than the form of the first sphere such that the difference of the act of the one nature in one matter would follow. Rather, the other form belongs to the planets, or to the epicycle, or to the eccentric, but the impossibility in that is as he mentions.

His saying: (except) for difference of matters—and this is like the difference of hyle in the universal spheres, and like the difference of elemental composite matters. And his saying: 'or for difference of dispositions of one matter'—and this is apparent in elemental things, as is well-known. And his saying: 'and that is not conceivable in the celestial sphere' i.e., in the one sphere only, even if it is conceivable in the estimated spheres as we mentioned.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer regarding the motion of a single nature. The objection argues that if a single nature (a simple substance) acts in a single matter, it cannot produce different effects in different places—here the celestial sphere moves some parts differently. The answer is that the difference is due not to a single nature but to multiple forms that inhere in the sphere, such as the form of the sphere itself and the forms of epicycles, eccentrics, etc. This answer is defended against the objection that multiple forms cannot inhabit a simple celestial body because that would require different matters or dispositions. The response is to deny the exclusivity of that principle, since it is possible that the difference of forms in some simple bodies is due to causes that revert to the agents (the intellects or movers). The commentator then explains that the objection is a 'prevention' (man') of a premise, not a contradiction, and the proper answer is to establish the premise. He also explains that the agents cannot be souls because souls attach after the body is formed, and the intellects act on the whole uniformly. He clarifies various phrases from the original text regarding the dent, the complement, and the distinction between forms.

Key Terms
منع
مقدمة
دليل
نقض
فاعل
نفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection directly disproves the premise.
Correction: The objection only prevents the premise from being accepted without proof; it does not disprove it.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is the objection considered a prevention (man') and not a refutation (naqd)?
A) Because the objection proves the premise false
B) Because no proof was given for the premise in the first place
C) Because the objection is from a different school of thought
D) Because the premise is self-evident

Key Takeaway
An objection that points to a case contrary to a premise without proof is a prevention (man'), not a refutation (naqd); the proper response is to establish the premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have doubted a foundational belief in your faith, did you treat your doubt as a refutation (naqd) when it was actually only a prevention (man')? How can you humbly seek to establish the premise before dismissing it?
?
This lesson shows that a single unestablished premise can unravel many rules built upon it. Are there unexamined premises in your own worldview that, if they turned out to be weak, would collapse your entire structure of belief? What will you do today to test them?
?
The commentator says the premise is 'evident upon reflection.' Have you ever dismissed a truth as 'not evident' when you had not reflected deeply enough? What specific step will you take this week to look again at an Islamic teaching you find hard to accept?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
4 min
The Objection: Impossibility of Two Specific Forms in Celestial Bodies
Presents the objection that two specific forms cannot coexist in a celestial body, leading to composition and undermining simplicity and spherical necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three-part objection against multiple forms in celestial bodies: impossibility of coexisting forms, violation of simplicity, and the consequence for spherical shape.

Core Idea
Two specific forms cannot coexist in one subject without contradiction.

Before presenting the responses, the author states the objection clearly. If spheres contain two forms (sphere and planet), they would be composite, not simple, and the necessity of spherical shape would be undermined. This lesson lays out the objection in its three components.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the three claims. Then read the translation.
The passage is concise; each claim is distinct.
2
Identify the three claims
Find the three objections: (1) impossibility of two forms coexisting, (2) composition of forces and natures, (3) generalization to all simple bodies.
These are listed in sequence.
3
Reflect on the threat to spherical necessity
Consider why the objection is significant: it challenges the uniqueness of celestial bodies and their spherical shape.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن يبقى عليه أنه يلزم اجتماع صورتين نوعيتين في الكواكب والتدوير والخارج المركز، وهو محال وأنه إذا كان في الفلك صورتان كان فيه تركيب قوي وطبائع فلا يكون بسيطاً، وأنه إذا جاز أن يتصل بالفلك صور متعددة وهي مبادئ أفعال مختلفة جاز في سائر البسائط، فلا يلزم أن يكون شكلها مستديراً، وربما يدفع الأول بمنع استحالته، فإنه صور العناصر باقية في المركب وقد حل فيه صورة أخرى نوعية سارية في جميع أجزائه، وهي العناصر فيكون في كل عنصر هناك صورتان نوعيتان . والثاني بأن معنى التركيب القوي أن يكون لجزء من الجسم قوة ولجزء آخر منه قوة أخرى، حتى إذا كان له جزءان قويان كان له قوتان وليس الأمر في الفلك كذلك، إذ الصورة الأولى سارية في

قوله : ( وهو محال ) لما تقرر عندهم من التضاد بين الصورة النوعية . قوله : ( فلا يلزم إلى آخره لأنه إنما يلزم إذا كان الفاعل واحداً لم لا يجوز أن يكون متعدداً كما في الفلك المكوكب

قوله : ( بمنع استحالته إلخ ) فيه أنه فرق بين الصورتين فإن صورة كل واحد من العناصر في الحيز والصورة الأخرى في المجموع فلا اتحاد في المحل بخلاف ما نحن فيه، فإنه قد اجتمع صورة الفلك وصورة الكوكب في محل واحد، فالجواب أنه لما كان صورة الكل سارية كان الحال في الكوكب جزء الصورة النوعية للكل، وجزء الصورة ليست بصورة حتى يلزم اجتماع المتضادين. قوله : ( حتى إذا كان له إلخ أراد به أن كل جزء منه يكون له قوة مغايرة لقوة جزء آخر، فهذا لا يتأتى في شيء من المركبات العنصرية ليوافق الأجزاء الأرضية مثلا في القوة، وإن أراد يكون فيه جزءان متغايران في القوة يرد الإشكال بتلك الثوابت لوجود الكواكب المتعددة المشتملة على القوى المتغايرة. فالجواب أن المراد بتركب القوى والعليا مع أن يكون حصول المركب بتركب الأجسام الحاملة للقوى لا تركيب بعضها مع بعض.

قوله : ( إلى أسباب تعود إلى (الفواعل الفواعل إما أن تكون متغايرة بالذات، فيصور وجودها في الأفلاك والعناصر ويتصور كونها أسباباً للصور المتعددة أيضاً، وإما أن تكون متغايرة بالاعتبار، فيتصور أيضاً وجودها في الأفلاك والعناصر، أما في العناصر فظاهر، وأما الأفلاك فكالعقل التاسع مثلاً، فإن له جهات عقلية واعتبارات مختلفة يسميها قرا وجد هو العقل العاشر والقمر والتدوير وحامله والخارج المركز والجوزهر والفلك الكلي للقمر وكذا قد أوجد الصورة النوعية لهذه الكرة وقد أوجد الهيولى والصورة الجسمية هناك أيضا كما ذكروا . قوله : ( اجتماع صورتين نوعيتين في الكوكب إلخ إحداهما الصورة النوعية لهذا الكوكب ممثلاً. والثانية هي الصورة النوعية لمجموع الفلك الكلي، وهي الحالة في مجموع الحاوية والمحوية وسائر الكرات المرتكزة في ذلك الفلك الكلي .

المتممات
But it remains upon him that it necessitates the conjunction of two specific forms in the planets, the epicycle, and the eccentric, which is impossible, and that if in the sphere there are two forms, then there is in it a composition of forces and natures, so it would not be simple, and that if it is permissible for multiple forms to attach to the sphere while they are principles of different acts, then it is permissible in all simple bodies, so it does not necessarily follow that their shape is round. And perhaps the first [objection] is repelled by denying its impossibility, for indeed the forms of the elements remain in the composite and another specific form has indwelt in it permeating all its parts, which are the elements, so in each element there are two specific forms. And the second [objection] is [answered] by that the meaning of composition of forces is that a part of the body has a force and another part has another force, such that if it has two strong parts, it has two forces, and the matter is not like that in the sphere, for the first form permeates in...

His saying: (and it is impossible) due to what is established with them of the opposition between specific forms. His saying: (so it does not necessarily follow etc.) because it only follows if the agent is one; why is it not permissible for it to be multiple, as in the sphere with planets?

His saying: (by denying its impossibility etc.) In it there is a difference between the two forms: the form of each of the elements is in a locative, and the other form is in the totality, so there is no unity of subject, contrary to our case, for the form of the sphere and the form of the planet have come together in one subject. The answer is that since the form of the whole is permeating, the state in the planet is part of the specific form of the whole, and part of the form is not a form such that the conjunction of opposites would follow. His saying: (such that if it has etc.) He meant by it that each part of it has a force different from the force of another part, so this is not realized in any of the elemental compounds, for the earthly parts, for example, agree in force. And if he meant that there are in it two parts differing in force, the objection is countered by those fixed stars, due to the existence of multiple planets containing different forces. The answer is that what is meant by composition of forces and the highest is that the composite occurs by the composition of the bodies that bear the forces, not the composition of some of them with others.

His saying: (to causes that revert to the agents) The agents are either different in essence, so their existence in the spheres and elements is conceivable and their being causes for the multiple forms is also conceivable, or they are different in consideration, so their existence in the spheres and elements is also conceivable. As for the elements, it is apparent. As for the spheres, it is like the ninth intellect, for example, for it has mental aspects and different considerations that it calls 'qira wajad' — it is the tenth intellect, the moon, the epicycle, its carrier, the eccentric, the head of the dragon, and the universal sphere of the moon. And likewise it has brought into existence the specific form of this sphere and has brought into existence the hyle and the corporeal form there also as they mentioned. His saying: (the conjunction of two specific forms in the planet etc.) One of them is the specific form of this planet, for example. And the second is the specific form of the totality of the universal sphere, which is the state of the totality of the containing and the contained and the rest of the spheres centered in that universal sphere.

The completions.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage presents a new objection: even if we accept that multiple forms can be caused by agents, it is impossible for two specific forms (e.g., sphere form and planet form) to coexist in one subject because they are opposites. Also, the presence of two forms would make the sphere composite, violating its simplicity. Moreover, if this is allowed for spheres, it should be allowed for all simple bodies, undermining the necessity of spherical shape. The response first denies the impossibility, using the analogy of elemental composites where the forms of elements remain in the compound and a new form permeates all parts. The second response redefines 'composition of forces' as requiring different forces in different parts, which does not apply to the sphere where the form is permeating. The commentator also discusses the agents being different in essence or consideration, giving the example of the ninth intellect that, through different aspects, produces multiple forms and spheres. Finally, he clarifies that the conjunction of two specific forms in the planet is not really a conjunction because the planet's specific form is a part of the whole's specific form.

Key Terms
صورة نوعية
تركيب
بسائط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is that two forms would physically occupy the same space, like overlapping objects.
Correction: The objection is about logical impossibility of two specific forms (opposites) inhering in the same subject, not physical overlap.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection mentioned in the passage?
A) Two forms cannot coexist because they are opposites.
B) The sphere would lose its motion.
C) Forms would cancel each other.
D) The sphere would become heavy.

Key Takeaway
The objection argues that multiple forms in a sphere would make it composite and undermine the necessity of its spherical shape.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever try to combine contradictory beliefs about Allah—such as affirming His attributes while denying them—thinking they can coexist in your heart? How does the impossibility of two specific forms in one subject expose the danger of intellectual inconsistency in your faith? Will you examine your beliefs today and remove any contradiction, no matter how comfortable it has become?
?
If a simple thing like a sphere cannot hold two forms without losing its nature, what does that say about your own soul when you try to serve both Allah and your desires? Are you willing to let go of one desire that competes with your devotion, so that your heart becomes simple and pure in its worship?
?
The objection warns that allowing multiple forms in one sphere leads to the collapse of order (spherical shape no longer necessary). Where in your life have you accepted contradictory principles—like trusting in Allah while relying on forbidden means—and how has that eroded the coherence of your Tawhid? What step will you take today to restore consistency?

Responses to the Objection: Analogy and Redefinition
Presents the first two responses to the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main responses: denying impossibility by analogy with elemental compounds, and redefining 'composition of forces' to preserve the sphere's simplicity.

Core Idea
The sphere's permeating form avoids true composition, making it still simple.

The author now offers two clever responses to the objection. The first denies the impossibility by pointing out that in elemental compounds, the forms of elements remain while a new specific form permeates all parts. The second redefines 'composition of forces' so that it requires different forces in different parts, which does not apply to the sphere.

Guided Path
1
Read the responses passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand both responses.
The passage is continuous; note the transition from first to second response.
2
Analyze the analogy with elemental compounds
Understand how the example of elements shows that two forms can coexist without contradiction.
The key is that the elemental forms remain and a new form permeates.
3
Grasp the redefinition of composition of forces
See how 'composition of forces' is defined as requiring different forces in different parts, which is not the case in the sphere.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if he meant that there are in it two parts differing in force, the objection is countered by those fixed stars, due to the existence of multiple planets containing different forces. The answer is that what is meant by composition of forces and the highest is that the composite occurs by the composition of the bodies that bear the forces, not the composition of some of them with others.

His saying: (to causes that revert to the agents) The agents are either different in essence, so their existence in the spheres and elements is conceivable and their being causes for the multiple forms is also conceivable, or they are different in consideration, so their existence in the spheres and elements is also conceivable. As for the elements, it is apparent. As for the spheres, it is like the ninth intellect, for example, for it has mental aspects and different considerations that it calls 'qira wajad' — it is the tenth intellect, the moon, the epicycle, its carrier, the eccentric, the head of the dragon, and the universal sphere of the moon. And likewise it has brought into existence the specific form of this sphere and has brought into existence the hyle and the corporeal form there also as they mentioned. His saying: (the conjunction of two specific forms in the planet etc.) One of them is the specific form of this planet, for example. And the second is the specific form of the totality of the universal sphere, which is the state of the totality of the containing and the contained and the rest of the spheres centered in that universal sphere.

The completions.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage presents a new objection: even if we accept that multiple forms can be caused by agents, it is impossible for two specific forms (e.g., sphere form and planet form) to coexist in one subject because they are opposites. Also, the presence of two forms would make the sphere composite, violating its simplicity. Moreover, if this is allowed for spheres, it should be allowed for all simple bodies, undermining the necessity of spherical shape. The response first denies the impossibility, using the analogy of elemental composites where the forms of elements remain in the compound and a new form permeates all parts. The second response redefines 'composition of forces' as requiring different forces in different parts, which does not apply to the sphere where the form is permeating. The commentator also discusses the agents being different in essence or consideration, giving the example of the ninth intellect that, through different aspects, produces multiple forms and spheres. Finally, he clarifies that the conjunction of two specific forms in the planet is not really a conjunction because the planet's specific form is a part of the whole's specific form.

Key Terms
صورة نوعية
تركيب قوي
سارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy with elements implies that two forms occupy the exact same subject without distinction.
Correction: The elemental forms are in locative parts, while the permeating form is in the whole; they are not in the same subject in the same way.

Checkpoint
How does the second response reinterpret 'composition of forces'?
A) It means the sphere has no forces.
B) It requires different forces in different parts.
C) It means forces are added together.
D) It means the sphere is composite.

Key Takeaway
The sphere's permeating form prevents true composition, preserving its simplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The second response defines true composition as different parts having different forces. In your own life, do you ever act as if different areas (worship, work, family) are governed by conflicting forces? What would it mean for you to allow the single form of Islamic guidance to permeate every aspect?
?
The first response uses a natural example where multiple forms coexist harmoniously. How does seeing unity in Allah's creation strengthen your trust in His oneness? Can you think of a situation where you need to look beyond apparent multiplicity to find the single divine hand at work?
?
If the celestial sphere were composed of conflicting forces, it would be weak. In what areas of your character do you sense inner conflict? How can the principle of a permeating, unifying form help you seek consistency and strength in your faith?

The Agents: Different in Essence or Consideration
Explains how multiple forms can be caused by agents differing in essence or consideration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that multiple forms in spheres can be explained by agents that are either different in essence or different in consideration, with the example of the ninth intellect.

Core Idea
Multiple forms can arise from a single agent with multiple aspects.

The discussion now turns to the agents causing the forms. The author explains that agents can be different in essence (separate beings) or different in consideration (one agent with multiple aspects). He gives the example of the ninth intellect, which, through different mental aspects, produces multiple spheres and forms.

The passage includes a clarification about conjunction of forms.
2

3
Consider the example of the ninth intellect
Reflect on how the ninth intellect, through different mental considerations, brings about multiple spheres and forms.
The text mentions several spheres and the hyle.

But it remains upon him that it necessitates the conjunction of two specific forms in the planets, the epicycle, and the eccentric, which is impossible, and that if in the sphere there are two forms, then there is in it a composition of forces and natures, so it would not be simple, and that if it is permissible for multiple forms to attach to the sphere while they are principles of different acts, then it is permissible in all simple bodies, so it does not necessarily follow that their shape is round. The Third Proof: Single Power, Spherical Shape, and the Refutation
Al-Iji presents the third proof and its refutation regarding simple bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof for the existence of a formative power based on the shapes of animate beings, and its refutation that a single power can produce diverse effects in a composite substrate.

Core Idea
A single power on simple matter yields only a sphere, but on compound matter it yields diversity.

Al-Iji presents a third argument known as the 'argument from shapes,' which claims that the diversity of organic forms cannot arise from a simple power unless there is an additional formative force. He then provides the refutation: when matter is compound, the same power can produce different effects. This lesson walks through the logic of both sides.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from proof to refutation.
The proof is presented in two sentences, then the refutation.
2
Identify the proof's core premise
The proof states that a single power acting on a single simple matter can only necessitate a spherical shape. Understand why this is the foundation.
This is a key assumption in the argument.
3
Examine the example of formative power
The proof then gives the example of the formative power (quwwa musawwira) which shapes limbs but does not give spherical shapes to its materials. Notice how this challenges the initial premise.
The proof is presented as an objection against the idea that a single power always gives sphere.
4
Understand the refutation
The refutation argues that the action of the simple power occurs in a compound matter (the generated animal or plant). In a compound with multiple receptacles, different effects are possible. This undermines the proof.
The refutation preserves the simplicity of the power while explaining diversity.
5
Reflect on the overall significance
Consider why this argument and its refutation are important in kalam: they show that observations of diversity do not necessitate multiple agents or powers.
This connects to larger themes in theology about divine simplicity and causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : بأن كل صورة تفرض في البسيط قوة واحدة تؤثر في مادة واحدة فلا تقتضي إلا شكلاً مستديراً. الوجه ( الثالث : الفاعل) عندهم ( لأشكال الأعضاء في الحيوان والنبات ومقاديرها في العظم والصغر وصفاتها من الملاسة ،والخشونة هي القوة المصورة وهي (قوة) واحدة بسيطة فعلها ألا ترى أنها لم تفد موادها شكل الكرة ، بل أشكالاً مختلفة. ( وقد يجاب) هذا من قبلهم : ( بأن فعلها أي فعل تلك القوة البسيطة ( في مركب ) هو التي يتخلق منها الحيوان أو النبات واختلاف آثار القوة البسيطة في مادة مركبة من قوابل متعددة جائز لا في مادة بسيطة.
The third: that every form assumed in the simple is a single power acting on a single matter, so it necessitates only a spherical shape. The third point: the agent, according to them, for the shapes of limbs in animals and plants and their magnitudes in bigness and smallness and their qualities of smoothness and roughness is the formative power, and it is one simple power; its action—do you not see that it did not give its matters the spherical shape but rather different shapes? And it is answered from their side: that its action—i.e., the action of that simple power—in a compound is that from which the animal or plant is generated, and the difference of effects of the simple power in a compound matter consisting of multiple receptacles is possible, not in a simple matter.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (الوجه الثالث) and its refutation. The proof argues that because a single power in a simple body can only produce a spherical shape, the diversity of shapes in animate beings proves the existence of a formative power. The refutation responds that when the power acts in a compound matter (like a composite substance), different effects can arise due to the multiple receptacles, so the argument does not apply to simples.

Correction: In reality, a simple power acting on a compound matter with multiple receptacles can produce diverse effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Commit to looking at that same thing tomorrow with the conviction that its diversity comes from the One Power acting on a receptive creation.
?
The refutation hinges on the fact that the matter is compound, containing multiple receptacles. This teaches you that the same divine power can produce different outcomes based on the readiness of the recipient. Are there areas of your heart or deeds where you are 'single-natured'—resistant to receiving multiple forms of guidance? Which 'receptacle' in your life needs to be made more receptive to Allah’s transformative power? Identify one specific change you can make this week to become a more fertile ground for His diverse blessings.
?
When you see the smoothness of a leaf and the roughness of bark, do you remember that one power (quwwa muṣawwira) is behind both? This lesson challenges the habit of ascribing effects to immediate physical causes alone. In your daily routine, what is one action you are most likely to attribute to 'nature' or 'chance' rather than to the direct act of Allah? How can you reorient that perception today, starting with a conscious acknowledgment before you perform your next task?

Fourth Proof from Eccentric Spheres and Its Refutation
Al-Ījī presents the fourth proof from eccentrics and the response about unity of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof from eccentric spheres, its refutation, and the key distinction between differences in type and accidental differences in natural actions.

Core Idea
Not all differences in effects break the unity of an action's type.

In this passage, al-Ījī presents the fourth proof that attempts to use the variation in thickness of eccentric spheres to argue for variation in shape. The response clarifies what is meant by 'single action' and introduces the distinction between differences in type and accidental variations. The commentary further elaborates on specific forms and the relationship between parts and wholes in celestial spheres.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and its refutation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument: the proof from eccentric spheres and the counterargument about 'single action'.
Pay attention to the structure: first the proof, then the answer, then the commentary.
2
Understand the meaning of 'single action'
The response defines 'single action' as being similar and not differing in type, not as being absolutely uniform. Note the examples given (surface, line, point) and what they illustrate.
3
Examine the commentary on specific forms
The commentator explains that the two complements share a universal specific form, unlike epicycles which have their own specific forms. This supports the idea that accidental differences do not change the type.
Consider the water droplet analogy: droplets in air do not constitute real composition.
4
Distinguish between differences in type and accidental differences
Reflect on the key point: variations in thickness and concavity are accidental and do not negate the unity of the type (spherical shape). This addresses the common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه (الرابع: الأفلاك الخارجة المراكز كل من متمميها يختلف جانباه بالرقة والثخانة ) فقد فعلت الطبيعة الواحدة في كل من المتممين أفعالاً مختلفة في الثخن فيجوز أيضاً أن تختلف أفعالها في الشكل. وأجيب عن ذلك : بأن المراد بالفعل الواحد كما أومأنا إليه أن يكون متشابهاً غير مختلف بالنوع كالسطح والخط والنقطة، لا أنه لا يختلف أصلا . واختلاف الثخن والنقر أيضاً ، لا يوجب خروج فعل الطبيعة عن أن يكون نوعاً واحداً. قوله : ( واختلاف الثخن إلخ ) فإن هذا الاختلاف العارض بسبب دخول خارج المركز في ثخن الممثل لا يوجب خروج فعل الطبيعة عن أن يكون نوعاً واحداً وهو الشكل الكروي . قوله : ( إذ الصورة الأولى سارية في الكل والثانية مختصة ببعضه) يعني أن المتمم الحاوي جزء من الفلك الكلي، وكذا المتمم المحوي جزء منه وليس لشيء منهما وحده صورة نوعية لم توجد في الآخر، ولا لهما معاً صورة نوعية لم توجد في التدوير أو الخارج مثلاً، بل الصورة النوعية لهما أي المتممين هي الصورة النوعية للكل وهي السارية في جميع الأجزاء من حيث هو جميع. نعم قد كان لكل من التدوير والخارج صورة نوعية مختصة به، لكن ذلك القدر لا التركيب الحقيقي المعتبر فيما بينهم، كما أن قطرات الماء تكون مرتكزة في كرة الهواء ومختلطة مع الهواء في كرة الهواء، وهذا القدر لا يوجب تركيب كرة الهواء تركيباً حقيقياً كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( كالسطح والخط والنقطة هذا مثال للمختلف بالنوع لا لغير المختلف بالنوع كما يتوهم، وقوله : لا يوجب خروج الطبيعة عن أن يكون نوعاً واحداً، وأما كون الرقة في هذا الطرف والغلظ في ذلك الطرف، وكون النقرة في هذا الطرف دون طرف آخر، فسيجيء الكلام عليه إن شاء الله تعالى .
The fourth: the eccentric spheres; each of its two complements differs in its sides in thinness and thickness. So the single nature has produced in each of the two complements different actions in thickness, so it is also permissible that its actions differ in shape. And it was answered: that what is meant by the single action, as we have indicated, is that it be similar, not differing in type, like surface, line, and point, not that it does not differ at all. And the difference in thickness and concavity also does not necessitate that the action of nature leave [the class of] being one type. His words: 'and the difference in thickness etc.': for this accidental difference due to the entry of eccentricity into the thickness of the complement does not necessitate that the action of nature leave being one type, i.e., the spherical shape. His words: 'since the first form is pervading the whole and the second is specific to some of it' means that the containing complement is a part of the universal sphere, and likewise the contained complement is a part of it, and neither of them alone has a specific form not found in the other, nor do they together have a specific form not found in the epicycle or eccentric for example; rather, the specific form of the two complements is the specific form of the whole, which is pervading all parts qua whole. Yes, each of the epicycle and eccentric had a specific form proper to it, but that amount [of composition] does not count as real composition among them, just as water droplets are suspended in the air sphere and mixed with the air in the air sphere, and this amount does not necessitate that the air sphere is real composition, as is not hidden. His words: 'like surface, line, and point' this is an example of what differs in type, not of what does not differ in type as is imagined. And his words: 'does not necessitate that nature leave being one type'; as for the fact that thinness is on this side and thickness on that side, and the concavity is on this side and not on the other, the discussion on it will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof (الوجه الرابع) and its refutation, followed by commentary. The proof argues that since a single nature in eccentric spheres produces different thicknesses, it could also produce different shapes. The answer clarifies that 'single action' means similar in type, not identical in all respects; accidental differences like thickness do not disprove unity of type. The commentator elaborates on the concepts of 'specific form' (صورة نوعية) and explains that the complements share a universal form despite having individual characteristics, analogous to water droplets in air.

Key Terms
فلك خارج المركز
متمم
فعل واحد
اختلاف بالنوع
صورة نوعية
سارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any difference in effect indicates a different type of action.
Correction: Accidental differences like thickness can occur within one type; 'single action' means similarity in type, not absolute uniformity.

Checkpoint
What does the term 'فعل واحد' (single action) mean in the response to the fourth proof?
A) An action that does not differ at all
B) An action that is similar and not differing in type
C) An action that produces uniform thickness
D) An action that changes shape

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof from eccentric spheres fails because 'single action' does not mean absolute uniformity; it means similarity in type, and accidental differences like thickness do not break that unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world, do you tend to see every difference as a contradiction to God's oneness? How does this lesson's distinction between accidental and essential differences challenge you to rethink your assumptions about divine unity? What specific example in your life—like seeing both good and evil—needs this understanding the most?
?
You have just learned that a 'single action' can include accidental variations. Are you willing to apply this to your own problems, trusting that God's plan is one type (wise and merciful) even when its parts seem contradictory? What would it cost you to truly believe that today?
?
The commentator compares composition of spheres to water droplets in air. Are there 'compositions' in your own beliefs that you take for granted as real contradictions? Will you now examine them through this lens of accidental vs. essential, and if so, what is the first belief you will re-evaluate?

Objection: Motion to Non-Existent and the Answer
Jurjani presents and answers the objection that motion can be towards the non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the kalam scholars distinguish between two types of goals of motion to defend the proof of direction from motion.

Core Idea
Not all goals of motion must exist; only goals of attainment require existence.

This passage tackles a clever objection: if motion can aim at something non-existent (like blackness), then direction being a goal doesn't prove its existence. Jurjani's answer preserves the proof by distinguishing between goals of attainment (which must exist) and goals of acquisition (which must not). This distinction is grounded in rational necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection and its challenge
Read the passage's opening: the objector uses the example of motion from white to black to argue that the goal (blackness) is non-existent, so direction as a goal does not prove its existence.
The challenge is that if a non-existent can be a goal, then the proof from motion collapses.
2
Note the distinction between two types of goals
Jurjani's answer separates goals into 'al-ḥuṣūl fīh' (attainment) and 'al-taḥṣīl' (acquisition). Blackness is a goal of acquisition, direction is a goal of attainment.
The distinction is crucial: only attainment goals require the object to exist during motion.
3
Understand the role of rational necessity
Rational necessity (al-ḍarūra al-ʿaqliyya) dictates that one cannot seek nearness to a non-existent, and one cannot acquire what already exists. This reinforces the distinction.
Rational necessity is the ultimate arbiter in this argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال: الجسم يتحرك في الكيف من البياض الموجود إلى السواد المعدوم، فقد جاز أن يكون المعدوم مقصداً للمتحرك، فلا يمكن الاستدلال على وجود الجهة بكونها مقصداً للحركة، لأنا نقول: إن السواد المعدوم مقصد المتحرك، ولكن لا بالحصول فيه أو القرب منه بل بتحصيله بهذه الحركة، والضرورة العقلية تحكم بوجود ما يراد بالحركة الحصول فيه وعدم ما يراد بالحركة تحصيله، أي تحكم بأنه يجب أن يكون الأول موجوداً حال الحركة لامتناع أن يطلب بها القرب من المعدوم، والثاني يجب أن يكون حال الحركة معدوماً لاستحالة تحصيل الحاصل.
It is not said: The body moves in quality from existing whiteness to non-existent blackness, so it is possible for the non-existent to be the goal of the mover, thus it cannot be inferred that direction exists from its being the goal of motion. For we say: The non-existent blackness is the goal of the mover, but not by obtaining in it or being near it, but by acquiring it through this motion. Rational necessity judges that that which is intended by motion [such that] obtaining in it is required, must exist during the motion, because it is impossible to seek nearness to the non-existent through it; and that which is intended by motion [such that] acquisition is required, must be non-existent during the motion, because it is impossible to acquire what is already obtained.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that if motion can have a non-existent goal (e.g., blackness that does not yet exist), then direction being a goal of motion does not prove its existence. The answer distinguishes two types of goals: those intended for attainment (al-ḥuṣūl fīh) and those intended for acquisition (taḥṣīl). For attainment goals, the object must exist during motion; for acquisition goals, the object must be non-existent. Since direction is a goal of attainment (the mover seeks to reach it), it must exist. Rational necessity (al-ḍarūra al-ʿaqliyya) enforces this distinction.

Key Terms
المعدوم
الحصول فيه
التحصيل
الضرورة العقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All goals of motion must exist at the time of motion.
Correction: Only goals of attainment must exist; goals of acquisition must be non-existent.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does the non-existence of blackness not refute the proof of direction from being a goal?
A) Because blackness does not exist at all
B) Because direction is a goal of attainment, not acquisition
C) Because sensory indication is not involved
D) Because the motion is not real

Key Takeaway
Direction is a goal of attainment, so it must exist during motion, preserving the proof of direction from motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you strive for something in your life—whether a spiritual goal like nearness to Allah or a material goal—do you distinguish between wanting to 'attain it' (be in it) and 'acquire it' (bring it about)? How does this lesson's distinction challenge your approach to your most important goals?
?
Consider a time you doubted the reality of a promise from Allah because it seemed non-existent to you. According to this passage, is that promise a goal of attainment or acquisition? How should that affect your certainty?
?
If rational necessity demands that a goal of attainment must exist during motion, what does that say about your movement toward Allah? Are you moving toward a goal that already exists? How does that change your hope and patience?

Ibn Sīnā's Proof on Celestial Bodies' Temperature
Ibn Sīnā proves celestial bodies are not hot/cold via correlation, with objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Sīnā proves that celestial bodies are neither hot nor cold, and how he defends his argument against the objection that the correlation is not universal.

Core Idea
The correlation of heat/lightness and cold/heaviness is contingent on receptive matter.

Ibn Sīnā uses the observed correlations in sublunar elements to argue that celestial bodies, lacking heaviness and lightness, must also lack heat and cold. But an objector challenges this, and the response reveals a key principle about causality: the effect can be absent if the substrate is not receptive. This lesson walks through the argument, the objection, and the final resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structured argument: claim, proof, objection, response.
2
Identify the core correlation argument
Ibn Sīnā states that heaviness correlates with coldness and lightness with heat. Since celestial bodies have no heaviness or lightness, they must have no cold or heat.
This is the main proof.
3
Understand the objection
The objector says the correlation is only proven for the elements, not for celestial bodies. So maybe celestial bodies could be hot or cold without becoming light or heavy.
This challenges the universal application.
4
Follow the response and the causal principle
Ibn Sīnā replies that heat is the cause of lightness and cold of heaviness, but the cause may not produce its effect if the matter is not receptive. Just as motion causes heat in elements but not in celestial matter, heat and cold may not produce lightness and heaviness in celestial bodies.
This saves the argument by limiting the correlation to receptive substrates.
5
Reflect on the key takeaway
The lesson shows that causality is not always direct; the nature of the substrate matters. This is a fundamental concept in Aristotelian physics and Islamic theology.
Think about how this principle applies elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن ( أي المحدد، وكذا غيره الأفلاك ) لا حار ولا بارد قال ابن سينا ) وذلك ( لتلازم الثقل مع البرودة ) فإن المادة إذا اشتد بردها ثقلت وإذا ثقلت بردت (و) تلازم (الخفة مع الحرارة ) فإن المادة إذا أمعن فيها التسخين خفت وإذا خفت ،سخنت فحيث لا ثقل ولا خفة فلا برودة ولا حرارة، وقد وقع في بعض النسخ لفظ اليبوسة بدل الحرارة، وهو سهو من القلم ) ولمانع أن يمنع التلازم بين الثقل والبرودة وبين الخفة والحرارة ( مطلقاً بل ) ذلك التلازم ) في العناصر فقط دون الأفلاك، فجاز أن يكون فيها حرارة أو برودة بلا خفة وثقل ( فإن قال : ( ابن سينا ( الحرارة علة (الخفة كما أن البرودة علة الثقل ( فيمتنع التخلف ( فلو وجدنا في الأفلاك لترتب المعلولان عليهما ( قلنا : قد يتخلف الأثر عن العلة الفاعلية لعدم القابل كالحركة فإنها توجب الحرارة في العناصر القابلة لها ) والأفلاك متحركة وغير حارة لأن مادتها غير قابلة للحرارة عندكم فيجوز أن تتخلف الخفة والثقل عن الحرارة والبرودة، لأن مادة الفلك لا تقبلهما، وإن كانتا مقتضيتين لهما .
And among them is that (i.e., the determinate body, and likewise other celestial spheres) is not hot nor cold. Ibn Sīnā said: That is because of the correlation of heaviness with coldness: when matter becomes intensely cold it becomes heavy, and when it becomes heavy it becomes cold; and the correlation of lightness with heat: when matter is intensely heated it becomes light, and when it becomes light it becomes hot. So where there is no heaviness nor lightness, there is no coldness nor heat. And it has occurred in some copies the word 'dryness' instead of 'heat', and it is a slip of the pen. And an objector may object that the correlation between heaviness and coldness and between lightness and heat is not absolute, but rather that correlation is only in the elements, not in the celestial spheres, so it is possible that in them there is heat or coldness without lightness or heaviness. If he says: Ibn Sīnā says heat is the cause of lightness just as coldness is the cause of heaviness, so it is impossible for the effect to be absent [from the cause]; so if we found [those qualities] in the celestial spheres, the two effects would be consequent upon them. We say: The effect may be absent from the efficient cause due to the absence of a receptive substrate, like motion: it causes heat in the elements receptive to it, but the celestial spheres are moving yet not hot because their matter is not receptive to heat according to you [the objector]. So it is possible that lightness and heaviness are absent from heat and coldness, because the matter of the celestial sphere does not receive them, even if they [heat and coldness] necessitate them.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sīnā's proof that celestial bodies (the 'determinate body' and other spheres) are neither hot nor cold. The proof relies on the observed correlation in the sublunar elements: heaviness accompanies coldness, lightness accompanies heat. Since celestial bodies lack both heaviness and lightness (they have only circular motion, no rectilinear motion), they must lack coldness and heat. An objector raises the point that this correlation is only proven in the elements, not necessarily in the celestial realm; perhaps celestial bodies could be hot or cold without becoming light or heavy. The author then has Ibn Sīnā respond: heat is the cause of lightness and coldness the cause of heaviness, so if the effect (lightness/heaviness) is absent, the cause (heat/cold) must also be absent. But the objector persists: causality implies necessary connection. The final reply is that an efficient cause may fail to produce its effect if the receptive substrate is not prepared. Just as motion causes heat in elements but not in celestial matter (according to the opponent's own admission), so heat and coldness might not produce lightness and heaviness if the celestial matter does not accept them. Thus the correlation is not absolute; it holds only in the elements.

Key Terms
تلازم
ثقل
خفة
برودة
حرارة
العناصر
الأفلاك
قابل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the correlation of heaviness/cold and lightness/heat is universal across all types of matter.
Correction: The passage argues that the correlation is contingent on the receptive nature of matter; in celestial matter, heat and cold may exist without corresponding lightness or heaviness because the substrate does not accept those properties.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sīnā's argument in the passage, why can heat exist without lightness in celestial bodies?
A) Because celestial bodies are not made of matter.
B) Because the efficient cause may not produce its effect if the substrate is not receptive.
C) Because heat and lightness are not correlated at all.
D) Because celestial bodies are in circular motion, which prevents lightness.

Key Takeaway
The absence of an effect (lightness/heaviness) does not disprove the presence of its cause (heat/cold) if the matter is not receptive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because a cause exists, the expected result must follow, only to be disappointed? What does this lesson teach you about the role of your own inner receptivity in allowing divine guidance to take effect?
