The Equant Problem in Mercury's Motion
Refutation of Ptolemaic astronomy using Mercury's equant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific observational contradiction in the Ptolemaic model for Mercury and why it undermines the astronomers' foundational assumption.

Core Idea
Observation disproves the Ptolemaic model for Mercury's motion.

This passage critiques the Ptolemaic astronomical model by pointing out a concrete observational discrepancy. The astronomers assumed uniform circular motion, but for Mercury, the observed motion of the epicycle center contradicts that assumption. This sets the stage for a broader critique of celestial mechanics.

Guided Path
1
Understand the astronomers' assumption
The Ptolemaic astronomers believed all celestial motion must be circular and uniform. This led them to construct complex models like deferents and epicycles.
They were perplexed by observed irregularities.
2
Identify the Mercury problem
The passage states that Mercury's epicycle center moves uniformly around a point called the 'center of the equant', not around the center of the deferent as the model requires.
The equant point lies between the center of the world and the center of the eccentric.
3
Extend the problem to other planets
The same issue applies to the superior planets and Venus, showing it's not just a Mercury anomaly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنهم لما اعتقدوا أن حركة الأفلاك يجب أن تكون دورية متشابهة تحيروا في مبدأ هذه الاختلافات المعلومة بالمشاهدة أو الرصد في هذه الكواكب، ولم ينبسوا فيه بذات شفة. والذي ينحي بالهدم على قاعدتهم في هيئة أفلاك عطارد بعدما قدمناه من أن ما ذكروه استدلال باللازم على وجود الملزوم مع عدم العلم بالمساواة أنها تستلزم تشابه حركة مركز التدوير حول مركز الحامل لما نبهنا عليه والمدرك بالرصد خلافه فإنها وجدت لنقطة تسمى مركز معدل المسير وهي بين مركز العالم ومركز الخارج الذي هو المدير. ومثل هذا الإشكال وارد على أفلاك العلوية والزهرة أيضاً. والذي ينحي بالهدم على قاعدتهم في الكل أن حركات الأفلاك إرادية على رأيهم فماذا يمنع أن تختلف تلك الحركات بحسب اختلاف ما يتعاقب عليها من إرادات جزئية لا بد منها في تلك الحركات إذ قد علمت فيما سبق أنها لا تكفي في الحركة الجزئية التعقل الكلي والحق إحالة ذلك كله إلى القادر المختار فإنها منجاة عن هذه الإشكالات وأمثالها كما نبهت عليه.
And know that when they believed that the motion of the spheres must be circular and uniform, they were perplexed about the source of these differences known through observation or sighting in these planets, and they did not utter a single word about it. And what demolishes their foundation in the configuration of Mercury's spheres after we have mentioned that what they stated is inference from the necessary to the existence of the necessitated without knowledge of equality is that it necessitates uniformity of the motion of the epicycle center around the center of the deferent, as we pointed out, but what is perceived by observation is contrary to that, for it was found to be uniform around a point called the center of the equant, which is between the center of the world and the center of the eccentric (which is the director). And a similar problem applies also to the spheres of the superior planets and Venus. And what demolishes their foundation in all is that the motions of the spheres are volitional according to their view, so what prevents those motions from differing according to the difference of the partial volitions that successively occur to them—which are necessary in those motions—since it has been known previously that general intellection is not sufficient for partial motion? And the truth is to refer all that to the Powerful Choser, for it is deliverance from these problems and their likes, as I have pointed out.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Ptolemaic astronomical model from a kalām perspective. The commentator (al-Jurjānī) begins by stating that ancient astronomers assumed celestial motions must be uniform and circular, so they were baffled by observed irregularities (differences in speed, position, etc.) and could not provide an adequate explanation. He then presents a specific objection concerning Mercury: the Ptolemaic model posits a deferent and epicycle, but observation shows that the center of Mercury's epicycle moves uniformly not around the center of the deferent but around a different point—the equant point (markaz muʿaddil al-masīr)—which lies between the center of the world and the center of the eccentric. This contradicts the model's own requirement for uniform motion. The same problem applies to superior planets and Venus. The commentator then gives a general objection: since, according to the Ptolemaic astronomers, celestial motions are volitional (irādiyya), why could they not differ due to partial volitions that arise successively? The idea is that a perpetual universal intellection (al-taʿaqqul al-kullī) is insufficient for particular motions; partial volitions are needed, and if motions are volitional, variation is possible. Therefore, the commentator concludes that the correct approach is to refer all celestial phenomena to God (al-Qādir al-Mukhtār), the Powerful Choser, who can directly cause motions without the need for contrived astronomical models. This, he says, delivers one from all such problems.

Key Terms
مركز معدل المسير (markaz muʿaddil al-masīr)
مركز التدوير (markaz al-tadwīr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ptolemaic astronomy is fully consistent with observations.
Correction: For Mercury and other planets, the observed motion of the epicycle center contradicts the uniform circular motion assumption, as it is uniform around the equant point, not the deferent center.

Checkpoint
What does observation show about the motion of Mercury's epicycle center?
A) It moves uniformly around the center of the deferent.
B) It moves uniformly around the center of the equant.
C) It moves non-uniformly around the center of the deferent.
D) It moves non-uniformly around the center of the equant.

Key Takeaway
Observation of Mercury's epicycle center moving uniformly around the equant disproves the Ptolemaic model's assumption of uniform motion around the deferent center.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The astronomers knew observation contradicted their model but remained silent. When you face a contradiction between what you've been taught and what you clearly observe—in matters of faith, science, or life—are you brave enough to speak up and seek the truth, or do you stay silent to avoid conflict?
?
The author uses the observation of Mercury's motion to demolish a cherished assumption. What cherished assumption about God, yourself, or the world are you most afraid to test against reality? Will you examine it today, even if it humbles you?
?
They believed in uniform circular motion without proof, then saw evidence against it. Are there beliefs you hold without evidence, simply because 'everyone says so'? What would it take for you to let go of a false foundation?

Volitional Motion and Divine Attribution
General objection to Ptolemaic volitional motion and conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalām argument that since celestial motions are volitional, they can vary due to partial volitions, and thus all such motions are better attributed to God's direct action.

Core Idea
Volitional motion allows variation, so God directly controls celestial movements.

After pointing out specific observational problems, the passage now offers a more general theological critique. The astronomers themselves held that celestial motions are volitional; if so, then why can't they vary due to successive partial volitions? This leads to the conclusion that all celestial phenomena should be referred directly to God, the Powerful Choser, avoiding the need for elaborate models.

Guided Path
1
Recall the astronomers' view
According to the Ptolemaic astronomers, celestial motions are volitional (irādiyya), meaning they involve will or choice.
This is a key premise.
2
Question the need for uniformity
If motions are volitional, they can differ due to partial volitions that occur successively. There is no need for uniform circular motion.
General intellection is insufficient for partial motion; partial volitions are required.
3
Accept the correct conclusion
The truth is to attribute all celestial motions to God (al-Qādir al-Mukhtār), which resolves all such problems.
This avoids the astronomical complications and contradictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنهم لما اعتقدوا أن حركة الأفلاك يجب أن تكون دورية متشابهة تحيروا في مبدأ هذه الاختلافات المعلومة بالمشاهدة أو الرصد في هذه الكواكب، ولم ينبسوا فيه بذات شفة. والذي ينحي بالهدم على قاعدتهم في هيئة أفلاك عطارد بعدما قدمناه من أن ما ذكروه استدلال باللازم على وجود الملزوم مع عدم العلم بالمساواة أنها تستلزم تشابه حركة مركز التدوير حول مركز الحامل لما نبهنا عليه والمدرك بالرصد خلافه فإنها وجدت لنقطة تسمى مركز معدل المسير وهي بين مركز العالم ومركز الخارج الذي هو المدير. ومثل هذا الإشكال وارد على أفلاك العلوية والزهرة أيضاً. والذي ينحي بالهدم على قاعدتهم في الكل أن حركات الأفلاك إرادية على رأيهم فماذا يمنع أن تختلف تلك الحركات بحسب اختلاف ما يتعاقب عليها من إرادات جزئية لا بد منها في تلك الحركات إذ قد علمت فيما سبق أنها لا تكفي في الحركة الجزئية التعقل الكلي والحق إحالة ذلك كله إلى القادر المختار فإنها منجاة عن هذه الإشكالات وأمثالها كما نبهت عليه.
And know that when they believed that the motion of the spheres must be circular and uniform, they were perplexed about the source of these differences known through observation or sighting in these planets, and they did not utter a single word about it. And what demolishes their foundation in the configuration of Mercury's spheres after we have mentioned that what they stated is inference from the necessary to the existence of the necessitated without knowledge of equality is that it necessitates uniformity of the motion of the epicycle center around the center of the deferent, as we pointed out, but what is perceived by observation is contrary to that, for it was found to be uniform around a point called the center of the equant, which is between the center of the world and the center of the eccentric (which is the director). And a similar problem applies also to the spheres of the superior planets and Venus. And what demolishes their foundation in all is that the motions of the spheres are volitional according to their view, so what prevents those motions from differing according to the difference of the partial volitions that successively occur to them—which are necessary in those motions—since it has been known previously that general intellection is not sufficient for partial motion? And the truth is to refer all that to the Powerful Choser, for it is deliverance from these problems and their likes, as I have pointed out.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Ptolemaic astronomical model from a kalām perspective. The commentator (al-Jurjānī) begins by stating that ancient astronomers assumed celestial motions must be uniform and circular, so they were baffled by observed irregularities (differences in speed, position, etc.) and could not provide an adequate explanation. He then presents a specific objection concerning Mercury: the Ptolemaic model posits a deferent and epicycle, but observation shows that the center of Mercury's epicycle moves uniformly not around the center of the deferent but around a different point—the equant point (markaz muʿaddil al-masīr)—which lies between the center of the world and the center of the eccentric. This contradicts the model's own requirement for uniform motion. The same problem applies to superior planets and Venus. The commentator then gives a general objection: since, according to the Ptolemaic astronomers, celestial motions are volitional (irādiyya), why could they not differ due to partial volitions that arise successively? The idea is that a perpetual universal intellection (al-taʿaqqul al-kullī) is insufficient for particular motions; partial volitions are needed, and if motions are volitional, variation is possible. Therefore, the commentator concludes that the correct approach is to refer all celestial phenomena to God (al-Qādir al-Mukhtār), the Powerful Choser, who can directly cause motions without the need for contrived astronomical models. This, he says, delivers one from all such problems.

Key Terms
القادر المختار (al-Qādir al-Mukhtār)
إرادات جزئية (irādāt juz'iyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Celestial motions must be uniform because they are caused by a single universal cause.
Correction: If motions are volitional (as astronomers held), they can vary due to multiple partial volitions. There is no need for uniformity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct way to explain celestial motions?
A) Through complex Ptolemaic models
B) By attributing them directly to God, the Powerful Choser
C) By assuming they are uniform and circular
D) By denying their existence

Key Takeaway
Because celestial motions are volitional, they can differ due to partial volitions, so the best explanation is direct divine action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every motion in your life—from the beat of your heart to the lift of your hand—is directly caused by God's will? If you struggle with this, what does that reveal about your reliance on secondary causes? Will you consciously affirm 'al-Qādir al-Mukhtār' in your next action?
?
The passage says referring all to God delivers from problems. What 'problems' in your life—anxiety about outcomes, fear of failure—could be resolved by attributing everything to God's direct control? Are you willing to surrender the illusion of independent causality today?
?
If celestial motions, which seem so ordered, are actually governed by partial volitions, what does that imply about your own small, seemingly random experiences? How would your response to life's disruptions change if you saw every event as a direct volition from the Powerful Choser?

Two Opinions on the Moon's Light
Two opinions on the moon's illumination with al-Razi's preference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main opinions on the source of the moon's light and why Imam al-Razi preferred the view that the moon's substance becomes luminous.

Core Idea
The moon's light is borrowed from the sun; scholars debated whether it is reflected or the moon shines by its substance.

In this passage, the author presents two positions regarding how the moon receives its light from the sun. The first view likens the moon to a mirror that reflects sunlight, while the second holds that the moon's substance itself becomes illuminated. Imam al-Razi weighs in with his preference, providing a key argument based on the observation that all parts of the moon appear luminous.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two opinions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the two distinct views: reflection vs. illumination of the substance.
The first opinion uses the analogy of a mirror.
2
Understand al-Razi's reasoning
Focus on al-Razi's statement: 'the more likely is the latter'. He argues that on the first view not all parts would be luminous, but observations show the entire moon is lit.
The evidence is from the moon's appearance at rising and setting.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this debate matters in Islamic cosmology. It shows how early scholars used empirical observation to support doctrinal positions.
The passage also briefly mentions planetary colors, which is a related note.

First Observatory - Second Section: First Objective: The Moon and Its Illumination from the Sun. From the five divisions: (All planets are transparent, having no color, (luminous) by themselves) except the moon, for it is dull in itself, its dullness appearing—I mean its darkness close to blackness during eclipses—and it is not luminous by itself, but its light is from the sun, due to the difference of its [؟] shapes according to its proximity to and distance from it. And it was said: its substance becomes luminous. Imam al-Razi said: The more likely is the latter, because on the first view not all of its parts are luminous, but it is so, as appears from considering its state at rising and setting. And among them is one who said: the eclipse of some planets by others indicates that they have a color, though weak. For Mercury has yellowness, Venus has brightness—i.e., pure whiteness—Mars has redness, Jupiter has impure whiteness, and Saturn has darkness with turbidity. (And in it)—i.e., in this section—(are five objectives). The First Objective: The Moon and Its Illumination from the Sun. His statement: (luminous by themselves) contrary to some who said they derive their lights from the sun according to al-Shifa. His statement: (on the way of reflection) i.e., by reflection of the sun's light on its apparent surface, because it is dull and polished, like a mirror when faced with the sun. His statement: (not all of its parts are luminous) because of the lack of reflection on all parts due to their difference in position relative to the sun, as in a mirror and the clarification of water upon which the sun's light is reflected. His statement: (but it is so) i.e., each one of the parts of the moon is luminous, indicated by considering its state at rising. His statement: (for on the first view, not all of its parts are luminous, but then it would be like a mirror when faced with the sun, for the sun's ray is only seen in the middle of the mirror, not its other sides. And if it were said: if it were luminous then, it would necessarily be seen luminous also from the sides not facing the sun. We say: the necessity of this is denied.

Commentary
This passage presents two opinions on the cause of the moon's light. First, it establishes that the moon is not self-luminous but receives light from the sun. Then it presents two views: (1) the light is reflected from the moon's surface like a mirror, without the moon's substance becoming luminous; (2) the moon's substance itself becomes illuminated. Imam al-Razi states the second is more plausible because on the first view, not all parts of the moon would be luminous, but observations show all parts are illuminated (e.g., at rising and setting). The passage also mentions that planetary colors (e.g., Mars redness) indicate planets have colors. Then it moves to commentary on specific phrases, explaining the reflection theory and refuting an objection: the objection claims that if the substance becomes luminous, it should be seen from all sides, but the answer denies the necessity. This is a typical kalam-style objection-and-answer within a scientific discussion.

Objection: The Limits of Disproving One Cause
Objection that refutation is incomplete; alternative causes enumerated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the epistemological argument that disproving one possible cause does not eliminate all rational alternatives.

Core Idea
Disproving one explanation does not prove the nonexistence of others.

This passage presents a clever objection to a refutation. The objector accepts the logical framework but questions the conclusion: proving that one specific cause is impossible does not mean no other cause exists. Instead, multiple alternative explanations remain possible, and the objector lists several intriguing hypotheses about lunar phases.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice the structure: the objector first accepts the principles, then challenges the completeness of the refutation.
This is a common move in kalām debates.
2
Identify the epistemic point
The core argument is that negating one possibility does not negate all possibilities. This is a logical principle.
3
Examine the alternative causes
The objector lists several possible causes: a dim planet, direct divine action, hidden bright planets, and reflection from dull polished bodies.
These are all proposed as rational alternatives.
4
Consider the broader debate
Think about how this objection challenges the certainty of empirical arguments in theology.
This relates to the role of reason in religious matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the objection to what they mentioned, after accepting the principles upon which they built it, is that negating this possibility which Ibn al-Haytham introduced concerning the shapes of the moon by its contradiction with lunar eclipse does not negate all rational possibilities for those shapes. For perhaps there is another cause for the variation of the moon's light, different from what he mentioned and what you mentioned, but we do not know it. Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the refutation of Ibn al-Haytham's theory. The objector accepts the principles but argues that merely disproving one possibility does not exclude all other explanations for the varying shapes of the moon. The objector suggests several alternative causes: a dim planet that occasionally eclipses the moon, direct creation of light by God at different times, or illumination by hidden bright planets that we cannot see. The objector then extends the argument by suggesting that those hidden planets might not be intrinsically luminous; their light could be caused by reflection when they are opposite the observed planets, similar to how dull but highly polished bodies reflect light.

Objection: All Elements Could Be Light or Heavy
Objection and response on elemental lightness/heaviness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that all elements might be light or heavy with degrees, and the kalam response that such a scenario is refuted by observed motion patterns.

Core Idea
Degrees of lightness/heaviness cannot explain diverse elemental motions.

The author has just argued for exactly four elements based on their natural motions. But an objector raises a subtle counter: perhaps all elements are fundamentally light (seeking the periphery) or all heavy (seeking the center), and differences in position are due only to degrees of lightness/heaviness. This lesson examines both the objection and the rational response grounded in observable physics.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the objector's reasoning come through before engaging with the response.
Notice that the objector first grants the invalidity of other opinions, then questions the necessity of four distinct elements.
2
Understand the objector's reasoning
The objector suggests that all elements could be essentially light or all essentially heavy, with differences in position due to varying degrees of these qualities. Think of earthly bodies: all are heavy, yet some sink, some float, some settle below others.
This argument challenges the need for qualitative difference, proposing only quantitative variation.
3
Read the response
The response points to observed motion patterns: if all bodies were light, they would all rise to the periphery simultaneously; if all heavy, they would all sink to the center at once. The fact that celestial bodies rise and set in regular, distinct ways shows this is not the case.
The response also addresses the issue of homogeneity and attributes differences to the free agent's assignment of properties.
4
Connect to the broader argument
This objection and response illustrate the kalam method: rational arguments must be grounded in observable, necessary facts. The proposal of purely quantitative differences fails because it contradicts observed motion patterns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا بطلان هذه الأقوال بأسرها لكن ليس يلزم ذلك كونها أربعة إذ لقائل أن يقول : لما قلتم : إن الأجسام ليست متجانسة فيكون الاختلاف حينئذ فيما بينها لا في الصور المقومة والطبائع الجوهرية بل في الصفات للفاعل المختار سلمنا أنها أربعة لكن لا نسلم ما ذكر من أحوالها بل نقول : فلم لا يجوز أن تكون كلها خفيفة طالبة للمحيط أو كلها ثقيلة طالبة للمركز ويكون ما فيها من التفاوت في الأحياز لتفاوتها في الثقل والخفة فالأثقل أسبق إلى المركز من الثقيل الطالب له أيضاً والأخف أسبق إلى المحيط من الخفيف الذي يطلبه ألا ترى أن الأجسام الأرضية المتشاركة في أصل الثقل تتفاوت قوله: (فلم لا يجوز أن تكون اختلافها في الميل الصاعد والهابط يدل على أن كلها ليست خفيفة ولا ثقيلة أي يسجد به ظاهر سطحه على سطحه بنسبة واحدة. وذلك لأنها لو كانت فيما بينها لكان الطلوع على الجميع والغروب عنه دفعة ولو كانت مقعرة لانعكس الأمر في الارتفاع والانحطاط. قوله : (لم) قلتم : إن الأجسام ليست متجانسة مع تركبها من أجزاء فردة متجانسة؟ ويكون الاختلاف فيما بينهما لا في الصفة المقومة والطبائع الجوهرية حتى تكون متخالفة بالماهية، بل الصفات إذ للفاعل المختار أن يخص البعض بصفة والبعض الآخر بصفة أخرى . قوله : ( لتفاوتها في الثقل والخفة يعني أن التفاوت في أحيازها إنما هو لتفاوتها في الثقل والخفة لا لاختلافها في الصور النوعية وإن كان لها صور نوعية كما زعموا أحوالها بتفاوتها في مراتبه فبعضها يرسب في الماء إلى تحت وبعضها يغوص فيه ولا يرسب وبعضها يطفو عليه
We grant the invalidity of all these opinions entirely, but that does not necessitate that they are four, for one may say: Since you said that bodies are not homogeneous, then the difference between them is not in the constitutive forms and substantial natures, but in the attributes of the free agent. We grant that they are four, but we do not accept what you mentioned of their states. Rather, we say: Why is it not permissible that all of them are light, seeking the periphery, or all of them are heavy, seeking the center, and that the disparity in their places is due to their disparity in heaviness and lightness? For the heavier precedes the heavy that also seeks the center, and the lighter precedes the light that seeks the periphery. Do you not see that earthly bodies sharing in the principle of heaviness differ? His saying: (Why is it not permissible that their difference in ascending and descending inclination indicates that not all of them are light or heavy?), i.e., it prostrates with its apparent surface on its surface in one proportion. And that is because if they were between them, the rising would be upon all and the setting from them at once, and if they were concave, the matter would reverse in elevation and decline. His saying: (Why) did you say that bodies are not homogeneous while they are composed of homogeneous individual parts? And the difference between them is not in the constitutive attribute and substantial natures such that they differ in essence, but rather attributes, for the free agent may distinguish some with an attribute and others with another attribute. His saying: (For their disparity in heaviness and lightness) means that the disparity in their places is only due to their disparity in heaviness and lightness, not due to their difference in specific forms, even if they have specific forms as they claim. Their states by their disparity in degrees: Some settle in water below, some sink but do not settle, and some float on it.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and response regarding the nature of the four elements. The objector grants that alternative views are invalid but argues that this does not prove exactly four elements. He suggests that all elements could be light (seeking the periphery) or all heavy (seeking the center), with differences in position explained by degrees of heaviness and lightness—just as earthly bodies, all fundamentally heavy, show variation (some sink, some float). The commentary then clarifies the original text: the difference in ascending and descending motion indicates not all are light or heavy; otherwise rising and setting would occur simultaneously. It also addresses the homogeneity of composite bodies and attributes difference to the free agent's assignment of properties. The response concludes that positional differences arise from degrees of heaviness/lightness, not specific forms, with examples from water behavior.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All matter could be fundamentally light or heavy, with only degrees producing differences.
Correction: Observable ascending and descending motions show that celestial bodies rise and set consistently, which requires both light and heavy elements.

Checkpoint
What alternative explanation does the objector propose for the differences in elemental positions?
A) Different specific forms
B) Degrees of heaviness and lightness within a single category
C) The influence of celestial spheres
D) The free agent's direct intervention

Key Takeaway
The proposal that all elements share the same basic tendency (lightness or heaviness) and differ only in degree is refuted by the observed distinct patterns of ascending and descending motion.

?

Objections to the Fire Sphere and Its Dryness
Objections to fire sphere and dryness, with counterarguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational objections raised against the existence of a sphere of fire and the claim that fire is naturally dry, and how the argument from empirical effects is insufficient to prove intrinsic dryness.

Core Idea
Empirical effects do not prove intrinsic essence; air and sunlight also dry without being dry.

This passage presents a series of objections against the philosophers' claim that there is a sphere of fire at the celestial periphery and that fire is dry by nature. The objector challenges both the existence of such a sphere and the inference that fire's drying effect proves its essential dryness, pointing to counterexamples like air and sunlight. We will explore each objection and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument structure come through before you interpret.
The passage is a continuous argument with multiple objections and replies.
2
Identify the two main objections
The first objection doubts the existence of the fire sphere; the second questions the proof that fire is dry. Find where these objections begin and how they are supported.
Notice that the objector grants fire's existence but denies a sphere.
3
Analyze the counterexamples
The objector uses air (which is moist) and sunlight (which is not hot or dry) as agents that also dry. Consider how this undermines the inference from effect to essence.
4
Understand the clarification on moisture vs. wetness
The commentary distinguishes natural moisture (ruṭūba) from accidental wetness (bulla). This is crucial for understanding why air's moisture does not prevent it from drying.
The passage ends with a terminological clarification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : بعد تسليم وجود النار في الجملة لم يقم دليل على وجود كرة النار عند المحيط كما زعمتم وإنما المشاهد استحالات تحدث لبعض الأجسام إلى النار كما عند الإبراد والإحراق لا يقال : الشهب دالة على وجودها لأنا نقول : جاز أن يكون هناك هواء حار يقتضي استحالة الأدخنة المرتفعة إلى النار فلا يثبت وجود كرتها وإن سلم وجود كرة النار فما الدليل على أن البسيط منها يصعب تشكله حتى نثبت يبوسة النار ؟ وهل إلى ذلك طريق إلا التجربة ؟ وكيف تتصور التجربة فيها ؟ و أما إفناؤها الرطوبات عن الأجسام فلا يدل على كونها يابسة في جوهرها لأنه إفناء للأجزاء المائية التي هي رطبة بمعنى البلة ولا دليل فيه على اليبوسة الطبيعية فإن الهواء أيضاً يعمل ذلك الإفناء مع أنه رطب الجوهر فإن قلت ذلك أي إفناء الهواء للرطوبات عن الأجسام إنما هو لما فيه من أجزاء نارية قلنا فيجب أن لا يكون الهواء البارد فاعلاً لذلك إذ لا يتصور فيه الأجزاء النارية مع أنه يفني الرطوبة ويجفف الثوب المبلول وبالجملة فلا يمكن القطع به أي بأن إفناء الرطوبة بمعنى البلة يدل على يبوسة المفني في ذاته موجود بدونها كما في الهواء وعليكم الدليل الموجب للقطع به وكيف يقطع به وشعاع الشمس يفعل ذلك مع أنه لا يوصف في نفسه بحر ولا يبوسة ولا غيرهما من الكيفيات ثم لا نسلم أن الهواء حار بل هو بارد بطبعه، وإنما يستفاد أشعة الشمس المنعكسة إليه من الأرض. فلذلك كلما كان الهواء أرفع وأبعد عن الأرض كان أقل حراً لضعف الانعكاس إليه وهكذا كلما زاد ارتفاعه قل حره وظهر برده حتى يصير زمهريراً في غاية البرودة. فلم قلتم : أن ذلك البرد الشديد في الهواء ليس له بالطبع بل لمخالطة الأجزاء الرشية المائية التي عادت إلي برودتها الطبيعية ولم يصل إليها أثر الانعكاس؟ ولا نسلم أيضاً أنه رطب فإنكم اتفقتم على أن مخالطة الرطب باليابس تفيده استمساكاً قوله : التي هي الرطوبة بمعنى البلة قد مر قبيل مباحث الاعتماد أن الرطب هو الذي يكون صورته النوعية مقتضية لكيفية الرطوبة وهي سهولة الالتصاق والانفصال، والمبتل هو الذي التصق بظاهرة ذلك الجسم الرطب، فالهواء يعني رطوبة الثوب التي هي البلة لا رطوبة الماء، تدبر . قوله : (لا يوصف في نفسه بحر إلخ وإنما قيده بقوله : في نفسه لأن الكلام في الرطوبة واليبوسة الطبيعيتين كما لا يخفى .
Then we say: After granting the existence of fire in general, no proof has been established for the existence of the sphere of fire at the periphery as you claim. Rather, what is observed are transformations occurring in some bodies into fire, like in the case of kindling and burning. Let it not be said: Meteors indicate its existence, for we say: It is possible that there is hot air that necessitates the transformation of rising vapors into fire, so the existence of its sphere is not proven. And if the existence of the sphere of fire is granted, what is the proof that the simple part of it is difficult to shape so that we establish the dryness of fire? Is there any way to that except experience? And how can experience be imagined for it? As for its annihilation of moistures from bodies, it does not indicate that it is dry in its essence, because it is an annihilation of water parts that are moist in the sense of wetness, and there is no proof in it for natural dryness. For air also does that annihilation despite being moist in essence. If you say that—meaning that air's annihilation of moistures from bodies is only due to what it contains of fiery parts—we say: Then cold air should not be an agent thereof, since fiery parts cannot be imagined in it, while it still annihilates moisture and dries wet cloth. In summary, it cannot be decisively asserted that the annihilation of moisture in the sense of wetness indicates the dryness of the annihilator in itself, as it exists without it in air. And upon you is the proof that necessitates decisive judgment. How can it be decisive while the ray of the sun does that, even though it is not described in itself as hot or dry or any other qualities? Then we do not accept that air is hot; rather, it is cold by nature, and it only benefits from the sun's rays reflected from the earth. Therefore, whenever the air is higher and further from the earth, it is less hot due to the weakness of reflection to it. And likewise, whenever its altitude increases, its heat decreases and its cold appears until it becomes bitterly cold in extreme coldness. So why did you say that that intense cold in the air is not natural but due to the mixture of watery parts that returned to their natural coldness and the effect of reflection did not reach them? And we also do not accept that it is moist, for you have agreed that the mixture of moist with dry gives it cohesion. His saying: which is moisture in the sense of wetness—it has passed just before the topics of al-I'timad that the moist is that whose specific form necessitates the quality of moisture, which is ease of adhesion and separation, and the wet is that which adheres to the surface of that moist body. So air's meaning is the moisture of the cloth, which is wetness, not the moisture of water. Consider! His saying: (It is not described in itself as hot, etc.) and he only qualified it by saying 'in itself' because the discourse is about natural moisture and dryness, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections against the philosophers' claim about a sphere of fire at the periphery and the dryness of fire. The objector begins by granting fire's existence in general but denies evidence for a spherical shell. Observed fire comes from transformations of bodies, not from a fire sphere; meteors could be caused by hot air. Even if a fire sphere exists, its dryness is unproven, as empirical testing is impossible. The argument that fire dries bodies does not prove its own dryness, since air also dries while being moist; cold air dries without fiery parts. Sunlight dries without being hot or dry. Air is cold by nature, getting colder with altitude, not from watery particles. The passage also clarifies terminology: true moisture (ruṭūba) is a quality of a body, while wetness (bulla) is accidental adherence. The commentary notes that the discourse concerns natural moisture and dryness.

Key Terms
كرة النار
يبوسة
رطوبة
بلة
الهواء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fire's ability to dry bodies proves that fire is essentially dry.
Correction: Drying can be performed by agents that are not dry, such as air (which is moist) and sunlight (which is neither hot nor dry). Thus, effect does not entail essence.

Checkpoint
Why does the objector argue that fire's drying effect does not prove its dryness?
A) Because air, which is moist, also dries
B) Because fire is always moving
C) Because moisture is not affected by heat
D) Because drying is only an accidental property

Key Takeaway
Observed drying by fire does not entail fire's intrinsic dryness, as air and sunlight also dry without being dry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
You rely on empirical evidence daily—but how often do you mistake what you observe for the full truth? What is one belief about yourself or others that you hold based on appearances, yet could be false like the claim about fire's dryness?
?
The author argues that even sunlight dries without being hot or dry. In your life, what 'sunlight'—something that does not possess a quality—still produces results? How can you re-evaluate your assumptions about cause and effect in your relationship with Allah?

Two Proofs for Water's Sphericity and Refutation of Natural Philosophers
The author gives two proofs that water is spherical and refutes a counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments used to prove that water is spherical by nature, and the refutation of the natural philosophers' claim that water's center is the earth's center.

Core Idea
Water's nature tends to sphericity, but obstacles can prevent it from achieving that shape.

In this passage, the author presents two arguments from natural philosophers for the spherical shape of water. The second argument is based on observation of water droplets forming spheres when thrown upward or on fine dust, and the third is analogous to the argument for earth's sphericity using star observations. The author also critiques the natural philosophers' inference that water's spherical tendency proves a single center, noting that obstacles can prevent water from taking that shape.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Notice the structure: second argument, explanation, critique of natural philosophers, third argument.
2
Identify the second argument
What observations lead to saying water's nature is spherical? How does the author distinguish between nature and external causes?
3
Understand the natural philosophers' claim
They say water everywhere is part of a sphere centered at the world's center, using the basin story.
4
Analyze the refutation
Why does the author say the basin story does not prove the branch? What is the role of 'obstacle'?
5
Consider the third argument
Its brief reference to a previous argument for earth's sphericity.
This argument is analogical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه (الثاني): الماء المرمي إلى فوق يعود كروياً وكذلك الماء المصبوب على تراب لطيف جدا فإن قطراته تتشكل بشكل الكرة فدل على أن طبيعته تقتضي الكروية وإنما يتم ذلك إذا بين كونه كرة حقيقة، والحس لا يعتمد عليه في مثله و بين أيضاً) أن ذلك لطبعه لا لمصادمة الهواء إياه من جوانبه أو بدحرجة في الطريق أو بسبب آخر لا نعلمه. ثم إنهم (أي المتمسكين بالوجه الثاني وهم الطبيعيون) يزعمون أن الماء أينما كان فهو قطعة من كرة الماء مركزها مركز العالم الذي هو المركز الطبيعي للماء، وعليه بنوا حكاية الطاس في قلة الجبل وقعر البئر كما سبق وهذا المبني عليه لا يقطعه (أي لا يفيد الفرع الذي بنوه عليه) لجواز أن يكون هناك مانع يمنع الماء في الطاس عن مقتضى طبعه الذي هو الاستدارة. الوجه الثالث: مثل ما تقدم في الأرض من تقدم طلوع الكواكب وظهور القطب وارتفاعه وظهور الكواكب واختفائه.
The second argument: water thrown upward returns spherically, and likewise water poured onto very fine soil, for its droplets take the shape of a sphere, indicating that its nature requires being spherical. And that is only accomplished if it is shown that it is truly a sphere, and sense perception is not relied upon in such matters. And it was also shown that that is due to its nature, not due to the air colliding with it from its sides, or by rolling on the road, or due to some other cause unknown to us. Then they – i.e., those adhering to the second argument, who are the natural philosophers – claim that water wherever it exists is a piece of the water sphere whose center is the center of the world, which is the natural center for water. And upon this they built the story of the basin on the mountain peak and the bottom of the well as previously mentioned. But this foundation does not cut it, i.e., it does not benefit the branch they built upon it, because it is possible that there is an obstacle preventing the water in the basin from following its natural inclination, which is roundness. The third argument: like what was previously mentioned about the earth regarding the earlier rising of the stars, the appearance of the pole and its elevation, and the appearance and disappearance of the stars.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments from the natural philosophers for the spherical shape of water, and then refutes the natural philosophers' claim that water's sphericity is due to its nature aligning with the cosmic center. The second argument observes that water thrown upward or poured on fine dust forms spherical droplets, proving that its nature tends toward sphericity. The author notes that this is not due to external causes like air resistance or rolling, but to its own nature. Then the natural philosophers build on this to claim that water is part of a great sphere centered at the world's center, using the example of water in a basin on a mountain or in a well. The author refutes this by saying that even if water tends to be spherical, an obstacle could prevent it from achieving that shape, so the basin story does not prove the earth's center is the water's center. The third argument is simply an analogy to a previous argument for the earth's sphericity based on the rising and setting of stars.

Key Terms
كروية
طبيعة
مانع
طاس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Water becomes spherical only due to external forces like air pressure or rolling.
Correction: The passage argues that water's spherical shape is due to its own nature, not external causes. External causes are not the primary reason.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, what indicates that water's nature requires sphericity?
A) Water always forms a sphere when thrown upward.
B) Water droplets on fine dust form spheres.
C) Both A and B.
D) The basin on a mountain.

Key Takeaway
Water's natural tendency to be spherical does not entail that water bodies must be spherical because obstacles can prevent it.

The Earth at the Center: Proof and Reconciliation
The author proves the earth is at the center and reconciles its centers of volume and gravity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the earth must be at the center of the universe based on observation and how its center of volume and center of gravity coincide.

Core Idea
The earth's central position is proven by uniform star sizes, and its centers coincide naturally.

This passage from al-Mawaqif presents a classic argument for the earth's centrality: celestial bodies appear the same size from all directions. The commentator then addresses a subtle objection and reconciles the centers of volume and gravity. Let's explore the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Understand the main argument
Identify the key observational evidence used to prove the earth is at the center: the stars appear equal in size from all directions. Consider why an off-center earth would cause them to appear larger on the nearer side.
This is the core proof.
3
Examine the refutation
Consider the refutation: what if the earth were off-center by an amount that produces an imperceptible difference? Notice how the author responds—such an amount could still be large in itself.
This shows the depth of the discussion.
4
Reconcile the centers
Study the commentator's reconciliation of the center of volume and center of gravity. Understand that in a simple, unaccidental earth, they coincide because heaviness pervades the volume.
This resolves an apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[المقصد الرابع: موقع الأرض من الكواكب] الأرض في وسط (الكل) أي مركز حجمها منطبق على مركز العالم (لأن الكواكب في جميع الجهات والجوانب من الأرض ترى بقدر واحد لا تفاوت فيه، ولولا أنه أي الثقيل المطلق الذي هو الأرض في الوسط لكان في بعض الجوانب أقرب إلى السماء فترى الكواكب هناك أكبر وفي بعض الجوانب أبعد منها فترى الكواكب فيه أصغر. ونقول: نحن في رد ما ذكروه لم لا يجوز أن يكون خروجها عن الوسط بقدر لا يكون التفاوت الموجب (بفتح الجيم) له أي لذلك القدر محسوساً وهو أي قدر الخروج مع كونه موجباً لتفاوت غير محسوس في الكواكب مقدار غير قليل في نفسه بل هو كثير. قوله: (أي مركز حجمها منطبق على مركز العالم قيل إنه مخالف لما ذكره في المقصد الأول من أن الأرض تقتضي أن تكون مركز ثقلها منطبقاً على مركز العالم والحق هو هذا المذكور في المقصد الأول: لا يخفى عليك أن ثقل الأرض كيفية سارية في حجم الأرض كلها. فإذا فرضنا أن نقطة كانت مركزاً لحجم الأرض كانت تلك النقطة أيضاً مركزاً لثقلها. وأما إذا فرضنا أمراً عارضاً يكون بحيث قد أخرج الأرض عن مركزها الأصلي فذلك العارض أيضاً يكون بحيث قد أخرج ثقلها عن مركزها الأصلي، نعم يمكن أن يكون بعض أطراف الأرض أثقل من البعض الآخر مع تساويها في المقدار والحجم لكن الكلام في الأرض البسيطة، فتأمل.
The fourth section: the position of the earth among the celestial bodies. The earth is in the center of the whole, i.e., its center of volume coincides with the center of the world, because the celestial bodies in all directions and sides from the earth are seen at one magnitude with no difference. If it were not for that – i.e., the absolutely heavy which is the earth – being in the center, it would be closer to the sky in some directions, so the celestial bodies there would be seen larger, and farther in some directions, so the celestial bodies there would be seen smaller. And we say, in refuting what they mentioned: why is it not permissible that its departure from the center be by an amount such that the difference caused (with fatha on the jim) by that amount is not perceptible? And this amount, i.e., the amount of the departure, while being a cause for an imperceptible difference in the celestial bodies, is itself not a small amount, rather it is large. His statement: 'i.e., its center of volume coincides with the center of the world.' It is said that this contradicts what he mentioned in the first section that the earth requires that its center of gravity coincides with the center of the world. The truth is that which is mentioned in the first section: it is not hidden from you that the heaviness of the earth is a quality pervading the entire volume of the earth. So if we suppose that a point is the center of the earth's volume, that point is also the center of its heaviness. But if we suppose an accidental matter that would have removed the earth from its original center, then that accident would also have removed its heaviness from its original center. Yes, it is possible that some parts of the earth's sides are heavier than others while being equal in size and volume, but the discussion is about the simple earth, so reflect.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a combined argument and commentary. First, the author states that the earth is at the center of the universe because celestial bodies appear the same size from all directions; if the earth were off-center, they would appear larger on the nearer side. However, the author then raises a refutation: why couldn't the earth be off-center by an amount that produces an imperceptible difference? He notes that such an amount, though imperceptible, could be large in itself. Then the commentator addresses a potential contradiction: in an earlier section, it was said that the earth's center of gravity coincides with the world's center, but here it says center of volume. The commentator explains that heaviness pervades the volume, so normally the centers coincide. Only if an accident shifts the earth would both centers shift together. In the simple (unaccidental) earth, they are the same. He ends with a call for reflection.

Key Terms
مركز العالم
مركز الحجم
مركز الثقل
الثقيل المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the earth's center of volume and center of gravity are necessarily the same in all circumstances.
Correction: The author shows they coincide only in the simple earth without accidents. If an external accident removes the earth from its center, both centers shift together, but they remain coincident.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given for the earth being at the center?
A) The earth is heavier than all other elements.
B) Stars appear the same size from all directions.
C) The center of volume must equal the center of gravity.
D) The philosophers' basin story proves it.

Key Takeaway
The earth's central position is proven by the uniform apparent sizes of stars, and its center of volume and center of gravity coincide in the natural simple earth.

The Earth's Insensible Magnitude and Its Consequences
Sanusi argues the earth is insensible relative to spheres, responds to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the earth has no perceptible size relative to the outer spheres, how this causes the true and apparent horizons to coincide, and why this does not imply equal day and night lengths.

Core Idea
The earth's smallness makes the celestial sphere appear symmetrical, but day length depends on the sun's orbit, not horizon equality.

In this passage, Sanusi explains a key cosmological principle: the earth is so tiny compared to the celestial spheres that its size has no perceptible effect on our view of the sky. This leads to a surprising result: the true horizon and the apparent horizon coincide, and every star rises exactly as its opposite sets. However, this does not mean day and night are equal, as an objection might suggest. Sanusi clarifies the difference and also notes the exception of the lunar sphere.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation to get an overview of the argument. Pay attention to the geometric description and the objection.
2
Follow the geometric argument
Visualize the two lines from the earth's center and surface to a point on the celestial sphere. Notice how they diverge imperceptibly, causing the positions to appear identical in sense perception.
This is the core proof that the earth has no sensible magnitude.
3
Examine the objection and resolution
Read the objection that equality of apparent and hidden halves would require equal day and night. Then study Sanusi's response: day length is determined by the sun's apogee and perigee, not by horizon equality.
Understand the distinction between the rising/setting coincidence and the duration of daylight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[المقصد الخامس: مقدار الحس بين الأرض والأفلاك] ليس للأرض عند الأفلاك قدر محسوس فالخط الخارج من مركزها إلى نقطة ما على الفلك كمركز كوكب من الكواكب والخط الخارج من الباصرة التي في حكم سطح الأرض وإن كانا يتقاطعان على تلك النقطة ضرورة بزاوية حادة من جانب الأرض ثم يتفارقان على زاوية أخرى مساوية للأولى ذاهبين إلى سطح الفلك الأعلى فلا شك أنهما يقعان منه على موضعين بينهما بعد بحسب نفس الأمر لكنهما موقعهما لا يتفاوت في الحس، كان أحدهما انطبق على الآخر وصار موقعهما واحداً ولذلك أي ولأن الأرض ليس لها قدر محسوس بالنسبة إلى الأفلاك كان الظاهر والخفي من الفلك متساويين وكان الأفق الحقيقي المار بمركز العالم، والحسي المار بظاهر الأرض في حكم دائرتين متطابقتين مع أن مقدار نصف قطر الأرض واقع بينهما يدل على ذلك التساوي طلوع كل جزء مع غروب نظيره لا قبل حتى يكون الظاهر أكبر ولا بعد حتى يكون الخفي أكبر. وهذا الذي ذكرناه إنما هو بالنسبة إلى غير فلك القمر وأما فلك القمر فللأرض بل لنصف قطرها عنده قدر محسوس ولذلك يختلف في الحس موضع الخطين المذكورين. قوله: (يدل على ذلك التساوي طلوع كل جزء مع غروب نظيره إلخ) مثلاً إذا كان كوكب في سمت الأوج وكان كوكب آخر مقابلاً له في سمت الحضيض كان طلوع الكوكب الأول غروب الكوكب الثاني وكان أيضاً غروب الكوكب الأول مع طلوع الكوكب الثاني لا قبل ولا بعد. وكذا طلوع الجزء الأول من الحمل مثلاً يكون مع غروب الجزء الأول من الميزان لا قبل ولا بعد هذا الحكم بالنسبة إلى الأفق الحسي ظاهر معلوم بالمشاهدة وأما بالنسبة إلى الأفق الحقيقي فبالطريق الأولى. فإن قيل: لو كان الظاهر والخفي من الفلك متساويين وذلك التساوي يلزم تساوي الليل والنهار في الصيف والشتاء وليس كذلك، قلنا ممنوع بل اللازم من ذلك هو أن يكون طلوع الشمس مع غروب الجزء الذي يقابله وأن يكون غروبها مع طلوعه لا قبل ولا بعد، وأما كون النهار أطول من الليل أو بالعكس فمرجعه إلى صعود الشمس على الأوج وإلى هبوطها إلى الحضيض. وأما تساوي الليل والنهار في خط الاستواء في جميع السنة أو تساويهما في غير خط الاستواء حين ما حلت الشمس في إحدى الاعتدالين فذلك أيضاً لعدم القدر المحسوس للأرض بالنسبة إلى سائر الأفلاك العالية فلذلك كان الأفق الحقيقي والأفق الحسي هناك في حكم دائرتين متطابقتين ومتحدتين. قوله: (عند قدر محسوس) فعلى هذا يكون النصف الظاهر من فلك القمر أقل من النصف
The fifth section: the sensible magnitude between the earth and the spheres. The earth has no sensible magnitude relative to the spheres. So the line extending from its center to some point on the sphere, like the center of a planet, and the line extending from the eye, which is in the position of the earth's surface, although they intersect at that point necessarily at an acute angle from the side of the earth, then they diverge at another angle equal to the first going to the surface of the highest sphere; there is no doubt that they fall upon two points between which there is a distance in reality, but their positions do not differ in sense perception; it is as if one coincides with the other and their position becomes one. And therefore – i.e., because the earth has no sensible magnitude relative to the spheres – the apparent and hidden (parts) of the sphere are equal, and the true horizon passing through the world's center and the sensible horizon passing through the earth's surface are like two coincident circles, even though the amount of the earth's radius lies between them. Indicating that equality is the rising of every part coinciding with the setting of its counterpart, not earlier so that the apparent would be larger, nor later so that the hidden would be larger. And this that we have mentioned is only relative to other than the lunar sphere. As for the lunar sphere, the earth, rather its radius, has a sensible magnitude relative to it, and therefore the positions of the two mentioned lines differ in sense perception. His statement: 'Indicating that equality is the rising of every part with the setting of its counterpart, etc.' For example, if a star is at the zenith of apogee and another star opposite it at the zenith of perigee, the rising of the first star would be the setting of the second, and also the setting of the first star would be with the rising of the second, neither earlier nor later. Likewise, the rising of the first part of Aries, for example, is with the setting of the first part of Libra, neither earlier nor later. This judgment relative to the sensible horizon is apparent and known by observation. As for relative to the true horizon, it is even more so. If it is said: if the apparent and hidden of the sphere are equal, then that equality would necessitate equality of night and day in summer and winter, which is not the case. We say: it is denied; rather, what is necessary from that is that the rising of the sun be with the setting of the part opposite it, and its setting be with the rising of that part, neither earlier nor later. As for the day being longer than night or vice versa, its reference is to the sun's rising to apogee and its descending to perigee. As for the equality of night and day at the equator throughout the year, or their equality at places other than the equator when the sun is at one of the equinoxes, that is also due to the absence of sensible magnitude of the earth relative to the rest of the high spheres. Therefore the true horizon and the sensible horizon there are like two coincident and unified circles. His statement: 'having a sensible magnitude' – accordingly, the visible half of the lunar sphere is less than the half.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a cosmological argument about the earth's size relative to the celestial spheres. The author argues that the earth has no perceptible size compared to the outermost spheres, so lines from the center and from the surface to a star converge and diverge imperceptibly, making the true and apparent horizons coincide. The evidence is that every celestial body rises exactly as its opposite sets, without delay. However, the lunar sphere is an exception: the earth's radius is perceptible relative to the moon's orbit, causing a measurable difference. The author then addresses an objection: if the visible and hidden halves of the sphere are equal, night and day should always be equal. He explains that equality of rising and setting does not entail equal lengths; day length depends on the sun's position in its orbit. At the equator, day and night are equal all year, and elsewhere at equinoxes, due to the earth's small size relative to the higher spheres, the horizons coincide. The passage ends with a note on the lunar sphere.

الأفق الحقيقي
الأفق الحسي
فلك القمر
الأوج
الحضيض
الاعتداليين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because the apparent and hidden halves of the celestial sphere are equal, day and night would be equal everywhere.
Correction: The equality of halves only means that every rising coincides with an opposite setting; day length is determined by the sun's orbital position (apogee/perigee), not by horizon equality.

Checkpoint
What is the evidence for the earth having no sensible magnitude relative to the outer spheres?
A) The moon appears larger than the sun.
B) Every part of the celestial sphere rises as its opposite sets simultaneously.
C) The sun's path is tilted.
D) The stars never set.

Key Takeaway
The earth's size is negligible compared to the celestial spheres, but this does not dictate day length; day length is determined by the sun's orbit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that your perception of the horizon is not the absolute truth—it coincides with the true horizon only because of the earth's smallness. In what areas of your life do you assume your perception is complete, when in fact you are missing a deeper reality? Will you humbly seek that reality from Allah?
?
If the earth's magnitude is negligible before the spheres, how much more is your own significance? When you act arrogantly or feel self-important, what would it take for you to internalize your true size before the creation and the Creator?
?
Sanusi distinguishes between the higher spheres (where the earth is imperceptible) and the lunar sphere (where it is perceptible). Are there beliefs or practices in your faith that you treat as negligible (like the earth relative to the higher spheres) but that actually have a perceptible impact on your soul (like the earth relative to the moon)? What will you do to re-evaluate them?

Two Objections to Earth's Rotation Refuted by Air Accompaniment
Presents first two objections to Earth's rotation and their refutation using air accompaniment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical scholars refuted objections to Earth's rotation by arguing that the air accompanies the Earth's motion, carrying projectiles with it.

Core Idea
Air accompanying Earth's motion carries arrows and stones, so no relative motion is observed.

This passage presents two common-sense objections to the idea that the Earth rotates—an arrow shot east or west, and a stone thrown straight up—and then refutes both with a single elegant principle: the air moves with the Earth, carrying everything with it. This argument shows that apparent stationary behavior of objects is compatible with Earth's motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the two objections
Read the first part of the passage where the arrow and stone objections are presented. Note how each objection claims that if Earth rotates, projectiles should show obvious relative motion.
The passage uses a specific scenario: arrow shot in direction of motion and opposite, stone thrown upward.
2
Identify the refutation
Focus on the sentence starting with 'والوجهان ضعيفان'. It says both are weak because air accompanies the Earth and carries the arrow and stone.
The key term is 'مشايعية الهواء' (accompaniment of air).
3
Apply the refutation to each objection
Work through how the refutation resolves each objection: for the arrow, it moves with Earth via air so distances are equal; for the stone, it moves with Earth so it returns to the same spot.
Consider that the arrow's own motion is superimposed on Earth's motion, but since both are carried, only relative motion matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأبطلوا ذلك أي تحركها على الاستدارة كما زعمه هذا القائل بوجوه ثلاثة : الأول : إن الأرض لو كانت متحركة في اليوم بليلة دورة واحدة لكان ينبغي أن السهم إذا رمي إلى جهة حركة الأرض وهي الشرق أن لا يسبق موضعه الذي رمى منه بل تسبقه الأرض، وذلك لأن الأرض على ذلك التقدير تقطع في ساعة واحدة ألف ميل وفي عشر ساعة مائة ميل ولا يتصور في السهم وغيره من المتحركات السفلية حركة بهذه السرعة فيجب تخلفها عن الأرض. وينبغي للسهم إذا رمي إلى خلاف جهة حركتها أن يمر عن الموضع الذي منه رمي ويتجاوزه بقدر حركته وحركة الأرض جميعاً. واللازم باطل لاستواء المسافة التي يقطها السهم من الجانبين بالتجربة . الوجه الثاني : الحجر يرمي إلى فوق فيعود إلى موضعه الذي رمي منه راجعا بخط مستقيم ولو كانت الأرض متحركة إلى المشرق لكان الحجر ينزل من مكانه إلى جانب المغرب بقدر حركة الأرض في ذلك الزمان الذي وقع فيه حركة الحجر صاعداً وهابطاً. والوجهان ضعيفان لجواز أن يشايعها الهواء المتصل بها مع ما يتصل به من السهم والحجر وغيرهما في الحركة كما يقولون بمشايعية النار للفلك فلا يلزم شيء من ذلك فإن السهم حينئذ يتحرك بحركة الأرض تبعاً للهواء التابع لها فلا يتجاوز موضعه الذي رمي في الجانبين إلا بحركة نفسه فيتساوى المسافتان وكذلك الحجر يتحرك بحركتها فلا يتجاوز موضعه الذي رمي منه بل ينزل راجعاً إليه .
And they refuted that, i.e., its rotation on its axis as this claimant claimed, with three aspects: The first: If the Earth were moving in a day and a night one rotation, then it would be necessary that if an arrow is shot towards the direction of the Earth's motion, which is the east, it should not surpass its place from which it was shot, but rather the Earth would surpass it, because the Earth on that estimation traverses in one hour a thousand miles and in ten hours a hundred miles, and it is not conceivable for the arrow and other sublunary moving objects a motion of this speed, so they must lag behind the Earth. And it is necessary for the arrow if shot opposite the direction of its motion that it passes by the place from which it was shot and exceeds it by the amount of its own motion and the Earth's motion together. But the consequent is false because the distance that the arrow travels from both sides is equal according to experience. The second aspect: A stone is thrown upward and returns to its place from which it was thrown, coming back in a straight line. If the Earth were moving eastward, the stone would descend from its place to the west side by the amount of the Earth's motion in that time in which the stone's motion upward and downward occurs. And both aspects are weak because it is possible that the air attached to it accompanies it along with what is attached to it of the arrow and stone and others in motion, as they say regarding the accompaniment of fire to the sphere. So none of that follows. For then the arrow moves with the Earth's motion following the air that follows it, so it does not exceed its place from which it was shot on either side except by its own motion, so the two distances are equal. Likewise, the stone moves with its motion, so it does not exceed its place from which it was thrown, but rather descends back to it.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents two of the three arguments used to refute the claim that the Earth rotates, along with their refutation. First, it restates the claim that the Earth rotates as some have asserted. It then gives the first argument: if the Earth rotates once per day, an arrow shot eastward would be left behind by the Earth's rapid motion, and an arrow shot westward would overshoot its target, but experience shows the arrow travels equal distances in both directions. The second argument: a stone thrown straight up should land to the west if the Earth moves eastward during its flight, but it lands back at the same spot. However, both arguments are weak, the passage explains, because the air surrounding the Earth moves with it. The arrow and stone are carried along by the air, just as fire accompanies the celestial sphere. Therefore, the arrow and stone are effectively stationary relative to the Earth's surface, so no discrepancy occurs: they do not exceed their starting points except by their own motion independent of the Earth. Thus the two objections fail because they ignore the role of moving air.

Key Terms
حركة الأرض
السهم
الحجر
مشايعية الهواء
المتحركات السفلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If Earth rotates, objects would appear to move relative to the surface.
Correction: The air moves with Earth, so objects carried by air appear stationary relative to the surface.

Checkpoint
How does the passage refute the arrow and stone arguments against the Earth's rotation?
A) By claiming the Earth is stationary
B) By arguing that the air moves with the Earth and carries the arrow and stone
C) By denying that the arrow and stone move at all
D) By invoking the celestial sphere's motion

Key Takeaway
The apparent lack of relative motion of projectiles does not disprove Earth's rotation if the air moves with the Earth.

?

?
This passage argues that what seems like obvious disproof can be neutralized by a deeper mechanism like air accompaniment. Is there a belief you hold about your faith that you resist questioning because the counter-evidence seems too obvious?
?
The air accompanies the Earth, carrying everything along. In your spiritual life, what 'accompaniments'—like divine mercy or unseen support—have you overlooked that could transform how you see trials as part of a greater wisdom?

Causality and Created Things

Third Argument Against Earth's Rotation: Natural Inclination Refuted
Presents the third argument (natural rectilinear inclination) and refutes it by denying the premise and showing coexistence of inclinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against Earth's rotation based on natural inclination and its refutation through denying the premise and showing the coexistence of inclinations.

Core Idea
Rectilinear and circular inclinations can coexist, as the wheel and top show.

The third argument against Earth's rotation claims that since Earth has a natural rectilinear inclination, it cannot have a circular one. Al-Taftazani refutes this by questioning the premise and pointing to everyday examples where both motions coexist. This lesson unpacks the argument and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through.
Notice the structure: argument, then two refutation points.
2
Identify the argument's structure
The argument has a premise (Earth has rectilinear inclination) leading to a conclusion (no circular motion). Identify each part.
The word 'فلا' indicates the logical consequence.
3
Understand the first refutation
The first refutation denies that the Earth has any rectilinear inclination. It also criticizes the basis for that claim.
The author refers to earlier discussions on void and inclination.
4
Understand the second refutation
Even if both inclinations existed, they are not incompatible. The wheel and top are examples where both motions coexist.
Think of a rolling wheel: it moves forward (rectilinear) and rotates (circular).
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why the third argument fails. The passage shows that the premises are weak and the logic is flawed.
This strengthens the case for Earth's rotation being possible.

Source Text

وعمدتهم في بيان ذلك وهو الوجه الثالث : أن الأرض فيها مبدأ ميل مستقيم بالطبع فلا يكون فيها مبدأ ميل مستدير فلا تكون متحركة على الاستدارة حركة طبيعية والاعتراض عليه منع وجود ذلك المبدأ فيها وهو أي وجوده فيها مبني على أن ما لا ميل له أصلا لا يتحرك قسراً وإلا كانت الحركة مع العائق الطبيعي كهي لامعه. وقد عرفت ضعفه في مباحث الخلاء كما أشير إليه في مباحث الميل . ثم لا نسلم تنافيهما أي تنافي الميلين حتى يلزم المنافاة بين المبدأين لما بينا من اجتماعهما في العجلة والدخرجة .
And their main argument in proving that is the third aspect: That the Earth has a principle of rectilinear inclination by nature, so it does not have a principle of circular inclination, so it cannot be moving in a rotation naturally. And the objection to it is the denial of the existence of that principle in it, i.e., its existence in it is based on the fact that that which has no inclination at all cannot be moved by force, otherwise motion would be with a natural obstacle like something forced. And you have known its weakness in the discussions of void as indicated in the discussions of inclination. Then we do not concede their incompatibility, i.e., the incompatibility of the two inclinations such that the incompatibility between the two principles follows, because we have shown their coexistence in the wheel and the top.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents the third and main argument against Earth's rotation and its refutation. The argument states that the Earth has a natural rectilinear inclination (a tendency to move in a straight line downward), so it cannot have a natural circular inclination; therefore it cannot undergo natural circular motion. The refutation first objects to the premise: the existence of such a rectilinear inclination in the Earth is not proven. It is based on the idea that something without any inclination cannot be moved by force, but that premise has already been shown weak in earlier discussions on void and inclination. Second, even if both inclinations existed, they are not necessarily incompatible. The author points to the example of a wheel (العجلة) and a top (الدخرجة), where rectilinear and circular motions coexist: a rolling wheel moves forward (rectilinear) while rotating (circular). Thus the third argument fails to prove that the Earth cannot rotate.

Key Terms
مبدأ ميل مستقيم
مبدأ ميل مستدير
العجلة
الدخرجة
مباحث الخلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rectilinear and circular motions cannot coexist in a single body.
Correction: The passage uses the wheel and top as counterexamples, showing that a body can have both rectilinear and circular motions simultaneously.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the third argument (based on natural inclination) refuted?
A) By proving the Earth has no inclination at all
B) By denying the premise that rectilinear inclination exists in the Earth and showing that both inclinations can coexist
C) By asserting that circular motion is impossible for any body
D) By appealing to the authority of earlier scholars

Key Takeaway
The third argument fails because rectilinear inclination is not proven and both inclinations can coexist, as seen in the wheel and top.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The refutation challenges the assumption that if you have one inclination, you cannot have another. In your spiritual life, do you ever assume that if you have a certain weakness (like a tendency to sin), you cannot also possess a strength (like sincere repentance)? How does the example of the wheel and top challenge you to reconsider such dichotomies? What false either/or might you be holding onto today?
?
The first step of the refutation is to deny the premise that the Earth has rectilinear inclination—a premise that seemed obvious to the opponents. What premises about yourself or your faith do you accept without question? Are there beliefs you hold that, upon scrutiny, might be based on weak foundations? Will you take time to reexamine one such premise this week?

Refuting the Argument from Straight Inclination Against Earth's Rotation
Al-Saylkuti refutes the third argument against earth's rotation based on straight inclination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Saylkuti refutes the claim that the earth cannot rotate because it has a natural straight inclination.

Core Idea
A straight inclination does not prevent circular motion, as seen in a spinning wheel.

Al-Saylkuti continues his defense of the earth's rotation by tackling a third argument from opponents: that the earth's natural tendency to fall straight down (straight inclination) precludes any circular motion. In this passage, he systematically dismantles this objection by questioning the existence of such an inclination in the earth and then showing that straight and circular inclinations can coexist, using the example of a wheel or top.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal English translation to get the gist.
The passage includes both the main argument and marginal commentary.
2
Identify the third argument (الوجه الثالث)
Find where the author presents the opponents' third argument: that the earth has a natural straight inclination so it cannot have a circular inclination, thus it cannot move in a circle.
The argument is based on the idea that straight inclination requires exiting the space, while circular inclination does not.
3
Examine the two refutations
Notice the two ways the author refutes the argument: first by denying that the earth necessarily has a straight inclination, and second by showing that straight and circular inclinations are not incompatible.
The author mentions that the premise about inclination has been shown weak earlier.
4
Consider the example of the wheel and top
Reflect on the example of the wheel (العجلة) and top (الدخرجة) which combine both straight and circular motion. This demonstrates the compatibility of the two inclinations.
This example is crucial to understanding the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجهان ضعيفان لجواز أن يشايعها الهواء المتصل بها مع ما يتصل به من السهم والحجر وغيرهما (في ) الحركة كما يقولون : بمشايعة النار للفلك فلا يلزم شيء من ذلك فإن السهم حينئذ يتحرك بحركة الأرض تبعاً للهواء التابع لها فلا يتجاوز موضعه الذي رمي في الجانبين إلا بحركة نفسه فيتساوى المسافتان وكذلك الحجر يتحرك بحركتها فلا يتجاوز موضعه الذي رمي منه بل ينزل راجعاً إليه . ( وعمدتهم في بيان ذلك) وهو الوجه الثالث : ( أن الأرض فيها مبدأ ميل مستقيم بالطبع ( فلا يكون فيها مبدأ ميل مستدير ) فلا تكون متحركة على الاستدارة حركة طبيعية ) والاعتراض عليه منع وجود ذلك المبدأ فيها وهو) أي وجوده فيها ( مبني على أن ما لا ميل له أصلا ( لا يتحرك قسراً وإلا كانت الحركة مع العائق الطبيعي كهي لامعه.
