قوله : فلا يكون فيها مبدأ ميل (مستدير لأن مبدأ الميل المستقيم يقتضي الخروج عن الحيز مبدأ لميل المستدير يقتضي عدم الخروج.
قوله : ( والاعتراض إلخ) مدفوع بما ذكرناه وقد مر تحقيقه في بحث الميل.
The two arguments are weak because it is possible that the air connected to it accompanies [it] along with what is connected to it of the arrow and stone and others in motion, as they say: by the accompaniment of fire to the celestial sphere, so nothing of that follows. For the arrow then moves with the motion of the earth following the air that follows it, so it does not exceed its place from which it was thrown in the two directions except by its own motion, so the two distances are equal. Likewise the stone moves with its motion, so it does not exceed its place from which it was thrown, but descends back to it. And their proof for that is the third argument: that the earth has a principle of straight inclination by nature, so it does not have a principle of circular inclination, so it cannot move in a circle naturally. And the objection against it is to deny the existence of that principle in it, and its existence in it is based on the fact that that which has no inclination at all cannot be moved forcibly, otherwise motion would be against the natural obstacle like... And you have known its weakness in the discussions of void as indicated in the discussions of inclination. Then we do not accept their incompatibility, i.e., the incompatibility of the two inclinations, so that a contradiction follows between the two principles, because we have shown their coexistence in the wheel and the top. 
His saying 'principle of straight inclination' because of what is observed of the motion of its parts towards the downward direction, and the whole resembles the part in reality. His saying: 'so it does not have a principle of circular inclination' because the principle of straight inclination necessitates exiting the space, while the principle of circular inclination necessitates not exiting. His saying: 'and the objection etc.' is rebutted by what we mentioned, and its verification has passed in the discussion of inclination.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument against the earth's rotation. The author first dismisses two previous arguments as weak, then presents the third argument (الوجه الثالث) used by opponents: that the earth has a natural straight inclination (مبدأ ميل مستقيم) towards the center, so it cannot have a circular inclination, thus it cannot move in a circle naturally. The objection to this is to deny that such a straight inclination exists in the earth, which is based on the premise that what has no inclination cannot be moved forcibly. The author notes that this premise has been shown weak in the discussions of void and inclination. Then the author argues that the two inclinations are not contradictory because they can coexist in a wheel and a top (العجلة والدخرجة), meaning a rotating object can have both a tendency to fall (straight) and rotational motion simultaneously. The footnotes clarify the terms: the straight inclination is inferred from the downward motion of parts, and the whole resembles the part; the circular inclination does not require exiting the space; and the objection is already refuted.

Misconception: Many assume that a natural straight inclination towards the center precludes any circular motion.
Correction: The author shows that straight and circular inclinations can coexist, as in a spinning wheel or top, which both falls (straight tendency) and rotates.

Key Takeaway
The earth's natural straight inclination does not rule out circular motion; both can coexist, as demonstrated by a spinning wheel or top.

?

Hills, Valleys, and Divine Care
The author explains the formation of hills and valleys and the role of divine care.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the formation of hills and valleys is explained through external causes and ultimately attributed to divine care.

Core Idea
The uncovered land that supports life is a sign of Allah's care.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif discusses the geological formation of hills and valleys, tracing their origin to astronomical causes and the natural flow of water. The author then highlights that the emergence of dry land, which is essential for life, is not a mere accident but a manifestation of divine care. Let's explore the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of parentheses in the Arabic; they indicate glosses or insertions.
2
Identify the causal chain
The passage presents a chain: astronomical connections lead to water flow, which uncovers hills and valleys. Trace these steps and note how each depends on the previous.
3
Recognize the theological point
The author states that the uncovering of land is not a natural necessity but is due to Allah's care for living beings. Reflect on why this is important in the context of kalam.
This counters purely naturalistic explanations.
4
Examine the linguistic note
The passage ends with a clarification of the phrase 'except by breathing air'. Understand the root word and its usage.
The note helps ensure precise understanding of the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الأرض تلال ووهاد لأسباب خارجية ومعدات متلاحقة لا بداية لها) مستندة إلى الاتصالات الفلكية التي لا تتناهى فسال الماء بالطبع إلى الوهاد) والمواضع الغائرة، (فانكشفت ) عن الماء (التلال) والمواضع العالية كجزيرة بارزة من وسط البحر ( معاشاً للنبات والحيوان الذي لا يمكن أن يعيش إلا باستنشاق الهواء. وهذا المنكشف هو المعمور من الأرض الذي كان حقه بمقتضى طبيعة الأرض والماء أن يكون مغموراً فيه كسائر أجزائها ( ولم يذكر له سبب إلا عناية الله تعالى بالحيوانات والنباتات إذا كان لا يمكن تكونها وبقاؤها إلا بذلك ) الانكشاف. قوله : ( إلا باستنشاق الهواء يقال : استنشقت الماء وغيره إذا أدخلته في الأنف .
In the earth there are hills and valleys due to external causes and successive preparations having no beginning, based on infinite astronomical connections. So water naturally flowed into the valleys and depressed places, and the hills and high places were uncovered from water, like an island emerging from the middle of the sea, as a living space for plants and animals that cannot live except by breathing air. This uncovered part is the inhabited portion of the earth, which by the nature of earth and water would have been submerged like other parts of it, and no cause is mentioned for it except the care of Allah Almighty for animals and plants, since their coming into being and survival is not possible except by that uncovering. His saying: (except by breathing air): it is said: 'istanshaqtu al-ma'a wa ghayrahu' (I inhaled water and other things) when you insert it into the nose.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage discusses the formation of hills and valleys on Earth. The author states that these topographical features arise from external causes and a series of successive preparations that have no beginning—these causes are based on infinite astronomical connections. As a result, water naturally flows into valleys and depressed areas, uncovering hills and high places, which then stand like islands in the sea. This uncovered land provides a habitat for plants and animals that require air to breathe. The author notes that by the nature of earth and water, this land should have been submerged, but it is uncovered solely due to the care (عناية) of Allah for living beings, without which such beings could not exist or survive. The passage ends with a brief linguistic note clarifying the phrase 'except by breathing air': the verb 'istanshaqa' (استنشق) is used for inhaling water or other things into the nose.

Correction: The passage explicitly states that no cause is mentioned except the care of Allah for living beings.

?

?

The Principle of Specification: Reason Needs a Free Agent
Sanusi explains that reason cannot specify without God's free choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rational speculation about natural causes must ultimately lead to the free choice of God as the specifying cause.

Core Idea
Without a free agent, reason cannot explain why things are the way they are.

The author begins by establishing a foundational principle: given equal natural preparations, the intellect has no way to explain why one part of the earth is hard and another soft. This sets the stage for the argument that only a freely choosing agent can account for such specification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense parenthetical style.
2
Identify the problem and solution
Find the statement of the problem: 'the intellect has no way to know its cause.' Then find the solution: 'whoever abandons these burdens and attributes all to His power... are the successful ones.'
3
Reflect on the core idea
Ask yourself: Why does equal proportion lead to the need for a specifier? The commentary will clarify this.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - القسم الثالث : المقصد العاشر : سبب تكون الجبال
والخروج من الماء إلى الهواء (وهذا الذي ذكروه ( رجوع إلى القادر المختار) وإسناد الفعل إلى مجرد مشيئته ) فإن اختصاص جزء من البسيط الذي هو الأرض باستعداد دون جزء آخر منه ( مع استواء نسبة المعدات إليها ) أي إلى أجزائه ( مما لا سبيل للعقل إليه ) في معرفة سببه . ( وإذا كان ( الشأن (كذلك) وهو أنه لا بد في الآخرة من الرجوع إلى استناد الأشياء إليه فمن طرح هذه المؤونات) التي تكلفوها ) ووفق للاسترواح إليه واستناد الجميع إلى قدرته واختياره فأولئك هم المفلحون ) عن الحيرة التي ربما تؤدي إلى الضلالة .
السيول الحادثة
من
[ المقصد العاشر : سبب تكون الجبال ]
حجرا
قالوا في سبب تكون الجبال : إن الحر الشديد يعقد الطين اللزج تحققه التجربة، وما يرى له من نموذج ) أي نموذج ( له في كير الخزافين ثم تتواتر الأمطار و تواتر ( الرياح العواصف تتحفر الأجزاء الرخوة فيظهر الحجر قليلاً قليلاً) بتزايد الانحفار من جوانبه شيئاً فشيئاً ( حتى يصير جبلاً شامخاً ) . قال الإمام الرازي: الأشبه أن هذه المعمورة كانت في سالف الزمان مغمورة في البحار فحصل فيها طين لزج كثير فتحجر بعد الانكشاف وحصل الشهوق بحرف السيول والرياح ولذلك كثرت فيها الجبال ومما يؤكد هذا الظن أنا نجد في كثير من الأحجار إذا كسرناها أجزاء الحيوانات المائية كالأصداف والحيتان ) ولا يخفى أن اختصاص بعض من أجزاء الأرض ) بالصلابة وبعض ( آخر منها ( بالرخاوة مع استواء النسبة ) أي نسبة تلك الأجزء كلها ) إلى الفلكيات ( التي زعموا أنها المعدات لها ( قطعاً ) أي جزماً لا يشوبه شبهة ( للمجاورة الملاصقة الحاصلة بين الأجزاء الصلبة ) والرخوة ( يستدعي سبباً مخصصاً. ( وعنده ) أي عند هذا الاستدعاء ( يقف العقل ويحيله أي يحيل ذلك الاختصاص ) على سبب من خارج ) هو الفاعل المختار ) ( فليت شعري لم لا نفعل ذلك أولاً ) حذفاً للمؤونة ؟ ( نعم لا يبعد أن يكون ذلك) أي تكون الجبال ونظائرها من أسباب تكونها ) بإرادة الله تعالى عند من يقول ) من المليين وغيرهم ( بالوسائط لا عندنا ) إذ الكل مستند إليه ابتداء فلا يتصور واسطة حقيقة على رأينا .
First Observatory - Third Section: Tenth Purpose: Cause of the formation of mountains. And emergence from water to air (and this which they mentioned (a return to the Powerful, the Chooser) and attribution of the act to His mere will). For the specification of a part of the simple substance which is the earth with a preparation without another part of it (while the proportion of the preparations is equal to it) i.e., to its parts (is something to which the intellect has no way) in knowing its cause. (And if the matter is ( thus) which is that in the end there must be a return to the dependence of things upon Him, then whoever abandons these burdens) which they have undertaken ( and is granted ease towards Him and attributing all to His power and choice, those are the successful ones) from the confusion that may lead to misguidance. Floods arising from [Tenth Purpose: Cause of the formation of mountains] as stone. They said regarding the cause of the formation of mountains: Intense heat congeals viscous clay, confirmed by experience, and what is seen of its model (i.e., a model) for it in the kiln of potters. Then frequent rains and frequent stormy winds excavate the soft parts, so the stone appears little by little, with increasing excavation from its sides gradually, until it becomes a lofty mountain. Imam al-Razi said: It is more likely that this inhabited world was in past times submerged in seas, then much viscous clay formed in it, then it turned to stone after the uncovering, and the elevation occurred through the erosion of floods and winds, therefore mountains are abundant in it. What confirms this conjecture is that we find in many stones, when we break them, parts of aquatic animals like shells and fish. And it is not hidden that the specification of some parts of the earth with hardness and other parts with softness, while the proportion (i.e., the proportion of all those parts) to the celestial bodies (which they claim are the preparations for them) is equal, absolutely (i.e., decisively without any doubt), due to the adjacent contact between the hard and soft parts, demands a specifying cause. (And with it) i.e., with this demand (the intellect stops and refers it, i.e., refers that specification) to a cause from outside (which is the Powerful, the Chooser). (Would that I knew why we do not do that first) eliminating the burden? (Yes, it is not unlikely that that) i.e., mountains and similar things from their causes of formation (are by the will of God according to those who say) from the millis and others (with intermediaries, but not according to us) for everything is dependent on Him from the beginning, so no intermediary is truly conceivable in our view.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This is a continuous argument. It begins by stating the problem: reason cannot account for why one part of the earth is prepared for hardness while another is not, given equal exposure to natural preparations. This leads to the necessity of referring all things to God's power and choice, and those who do so are saved from confusion. Then it presents the philosophers' explanation of mountain formation through heat, rain, and wind, and Imam al-Razi's hypothesis that mountains formed from marine sediments after water receded. The core argument follows: the specific distribution of hardness and softness, despite equal proximity to celestial influences, demands a specifying cause. Reason thus stops and refers that specification to a cause from outside, which is the free agent. The conclusion is a rhetorical question: why not do this from the start? Finally, a clarification: for Muslims who accept intermediaries, mountains may be caused by God through them, but for those who reject intermediaries, everything is directly from God.

Key Terms
القادر المختار
المعدات
الاختصاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natural causes are sufficient to explain specific distributions without need for God.
Correction: The passage argues that natural causes equally disposed to all parts cannot account for the specific distribution, so a freely choosing agent is necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the intellect have no way to know the cause of specification?
A) Because the cause is unknown.
B) Because natural preparations are equally related to all parts.
C) Because the intellect is weak.
D) Because the cause is God's will directly.

Key Takeaway
Given equal natural conditions, the specific differences in the world point directly to God's free choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the differences in your own life—why you were born where you were, with your particular talents and trials—do you truly see them as the result of God's free choice, or do you secretly attribute them to 'luck' or 'circumstances'? How would your gratitude and patience change if you fully embraced that every specification came from His will?
?
The intellect, when left alone, falls into confusion about why things are the way they are. Have you ever felt that confusion—questioning why bad things happen to good people or why your duas aren't answered? Are you willing to 'abandon the burdens' of trying to figure it all out and simply attribute it to God's power and choice, trusting His wisdom?
?
Sanusi says those who 'return to Him and attribute all to His power and choice' are saved from misguidance. Is there an area of your life where you are still insisting on a natural explanation or blaming secondary causes, refusing to let go and submit to God's decree? What would it look like for you to 'return' today?

Geological Evidence and the Specifying Cause
Sanusi presents naturalistic explanations of mountains and then argues they require a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how empirical evidence from geology leads reason to posit a free agent as the specifying cause.

Core Idea
Even natural explanations require a free choice to account for distribution.

After establishing the principle, the author presents the philosophers' theory of mountain formation and al-Razi's marine hypothesis. He then shows that even with these explanations, the specific distribution of hardness and softness demands a cause beyond natural processes, leading reason to the free agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the detailed explanation
Read the philosophers' explanation and al-Razi's hypothesis. Notice how they attempt to account for mountain formation through natural processes.
2
Identify the core argument
Find the sentence that states the specification of hardness and softness demands a specifying cause. Underline it.
This is the key move.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author says 'the intellect stops and refers to a cause from outside' – that is the free agent. Then he asks why not do this from the start.
The final remark about intermediaries clarifies the position.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this argument applies to all natural phenomena, not just mountains.

First Observatory - Third Section: Tenth Purpose: Cause of the formation of mountains. And emergence from water to air (and this which they mentioned (a return to the Powerful, the Chooser) and attribution of the act to His mere will). For the specification of a part of the simple substance which is the earth with a preparation without another part of it (while the proportion of the preparations is equal to it) i.e., to its parts (is something to which the intellect has no way) in knowing its cause. (And if the matter is ( thus) which is that in the end there must be a return to the dependence of things upon Him, then whoever abandons these burdens) which they have undertaken ( and is granted ease towards Him and attributing all to His power and choice, those are the successful ones) from the confusion that may lead to misguidance. Floods arising from [Tenth Purpose: Cause of the formation of mountains] as stone. They said regarding the cause of the formation of mountains: Intense heat congeals viscous clay, confirmed by experience, and what is seen of its model (i.e., a model) for it in the kiln of potters. Then frequent rains and frequent stormy winds excavate the soft parts, so the stone appears little by little, with increasing excavation from its sides gradually, until it becomes a lofty mountain. Imam al-Razi said: It is more likely that this inhabited world was in past times submerged in seas, then much viscous clay formed in it, then it turned to stone after the uncovering, and the elevation occurred through the erosion of floods and winds, therefore mountains are abundant in it. What confirms this conjecture is that we find in many stones, when we break them, parts of aquatic animals like shells and fish. And it is not hidden that the specification of some parts of the earth with hardness and other parts with softness, while the proportion (i.e., the proportion of all those parts) to the celestial bodies (which they claim are the preparations for them) is equal, absolutely (i.e., decisively without any doubt), due to the adjacent contact between the hard and soft parts, demands a specifying cause. (And with it) i.e., with this demand (the intellect stops and refers it, i.e., refers that specification) to a cause from outside (which is the Powerful, the Chooser). (Would that I knew why we do not do that first) eliminating the burden? (Yes, it is not unlikely that that) i.e., mountains and similar things from their causes of formation (are by the will of God according to those who say) from the millis and others (with intermediaries, but not according to us) for everything is dependent on Him from the beginning, so no intermediary is truly conceivable in our view.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This is a continuous argument. It begins by stating the problem: reason cannot account for why one part of the earth is prepared for hardness while another is not, given equal exposure to natural preparations. This leads to the necessity of referring all things to God's power and choice, and those who do so are saved from confusion. Then it presents the philosophers' explanation of mountain formation through heat, rain, and wind, and Imam al-Razi's hypothesis that mountains formed from marine sediments after water receded. The core argument follows: the specific distribution of hardness and softness, despite equal proximity to celestial influences, demands a specifying cause. Reason thus stops and refers that specification to a cause from outside, which is the free agent. The conclusion is a rhetorical question: why not do this from the start? Finally, a clarification: for Muslims who accept intermediaries, mountains may be caused by God through them, but for those who reject intermediaries, everything is directly from God.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natural processes like heat and erosion are sufficient to fully explain the distribution of hardness and softness.
Correction: The passage argues that these processes do not account for why certain parts are selected over others given equal conditions; a specifying cause is needed.

Checkpoint

C) It concludes that the cause is unknown.

Key Takeaway
Empirical evidence forces reason to recognize a free agent as the ultimate source of specific distribution in nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the variety in creation—from mountain ranges to your own physical features—do you treat the specific details as random accidents or as deliberate choices? If you believe they are chosen, why do you still worry about the future as if chance governs it?
?
You have just reasoned that specification demands a free agent. But in your daily life, do you live as though God is the direct cause of what befalls you, or do you attribute events to secondary causes and forget the primary? What habit could you change to make this truth real for you?
?
Sanusi laments why we do not refer to God first. Reflect: when something good or difficult happens to you, what is your immediate reaction? Do you instinctively recognize God's hand, or do you attribute it to nature, effort, or luck? What would it mean to refer all things to Him from the start?

The Transformation of the Four Elements
Defines the transformation of elements into each other with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam concept of generation and corruption (كون وفساد) as applied to the four elements, and how elements transform into each other based on shared qualities.

Core Idea
Elements are not eternal; they shed one form and gain another, transforming into each other.

In this passage, the author defines the four elements as subject to generation and corruption, meaning they can change from one element to another. This is a key concept in Islamic cosmology, demonstrating the contingency of the physical world. The passage then lists the twelve possible transformations and explains which occur directly based on shared primary qualities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's systematic approach.
2
Identify the two processes
Find where the author defines 'كون' (generation) and 'فساد' (corruption). Understand that corruption is shedding a form, generation is acquiring a new one.
These terms are central to kalam discussions on change.
3
Note the condition for direct transformation
The author says some transformations happen 'بلا وسط' (without intermediary). Look for the condition: sharing one quality and differing in another.
The four qualities are hot, cold, wet, dry.
4
Study the examples
Examine the examples of earth-water (alchemy, natural springs) and water-air (evaporation, condensation). Understand the refutation of seepage.
The refutation shows the author's empirical reasoning.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - القسم الثالث : المقصد الحادي عشر : العناصر الأربعة تقبل الاختلاط
) العناصر الأربعة تقبل الكون والفساد أي تخلع صورة ذلك العنصر) وهو معنى الفساد ) وتلبس صورة عنصر آخر وهو معنى الكون ( فينقلب كل ) من الأربعة ( إلى الآخر الذي هو أحد الثلاثة الباقية فتكون الانقلابات اثنتي عشرة لكن (بعضها ) ينقلب إلى بعض آخر ( بلا وسط وهو كل عنصر يشارك ) عنصر (آخر في كيفية ) واحدة من كيفيتيه اللتين هما من الكيفيات الأربع ) ويخالفه في كيفية أخرى منهما ( فينقلب الأرض والماء كل منهما ) إلى الآخر ابتداء لاشتراكهما في البرد ) . وإن اختلفا في اليبوسة (وذلك كما يجعل بعض أهل الحيل) من طلاب الإكسير ( الأحجار مياهاً سيالة ) فإنهم يتخذون مياهاً حارة ويجعلون فيها أجساداً صلبة حجرية حتى تصير جارية ( وينقلب الماء في بعض المواضع حجراً صلباً كعين سيهكوه) وهي قريبة من بلدة مراغة وماؤها ينقلب حجراً مرمراً، وعين غيره من المواضع ( وكذلك الماء والهواء ) ينقلب كل منهما إلى الآخر بلا وسط ( لاشتراكهما في الرطوبة ) وإن كانا متخالفين في الحرارة ) كما يصير الماء هواء بالتسخين وهو النشف) في الثياب المبلولة المطروحة في الشمس (و) كما يصير ( الهواء ماء بالتبريد كما في ظاهر كوز لا مسام له يوضع في الجمد ) فإنه يحدث على ظاهره ) حيث لا يلاقيه الجمد قطرات من الماء وكظاهر الطاس يكب على الجمد مع عدم الملاقاة ( بينهما فإنه تركب قطرات منه ( وليس ذلك لأن الماء ينتقل إليه ) بالرشح (لأنه لا يصعد بالطبع وإذ لو كان كذلك كان باطن الطاس أولى به من ظاهره ) . وأيضاً الترشح على سبيل التصاعد أنسب بالماء الحار ( وكذلك النار والهواء ) ينقلب
معنى
قوله: (تكونها) متبدأ خبره بإرادة الله تعالى، والجملة صفة أسباب . وقوله : ما عرض لها
فاعل قوله : يعد .
First Observatory - Third Section: Eleventh Purpose: The four elements accept mixing. The four elements accept generation and corruption, i.e., they strip off the form of that element (which is the meaning of corruption) and put on the form of another element (which is the meaning of generation). So each of the four transforms into the other which is one of the other three, so the transformations are twelve. But some transform into another directly (without a middle), and it is every element that shares with another element in one quality from its two qualities, which are among the four qualities, and differs from it in another quality from them. So earth and water each transform into the other directly because of their sharing in coldness, even if they differ in dryness. And that is like what some practitioners of artifice among seekers of the elixir do: they make stones into flowing water, for they take hot water and put in it solid stony bodies until they become flowing. And water transforms into hard stone in some places, like the spring of Sīhakūh, which is near the town of Marāgha, and its water turns into marble stone; and other springs in other places. Similarly, water and air each transform into the other directly because of their sharing in moisture, even if they differ in heat, just as water becomes air by heating, which is the drying of moist clothes placed in the sun; and just as air becomes water by cooling, as on the surface of a jug without pores placed in ice, for it produces on its surface, where the ice does not touch it, drops of water; and like the surface of a bowl placed over ice without contact between them, for it forms drops of it. And that is not because water transfers to it by seepage, because water does not ascend naturally; if it were so, the inside of the bowl would be more deserving of it than its outside. Also, seepage by way of ascending is more appropriate for hot water. Similarly, fire and air transform... Meaning of His saying: 'its formation' is a subject whose predicate is 'by the will of God,' and the sentence is a description of the causes. And His saying: 'what occurred to it' is the subject of His saying: 'it is counted.'
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This is a classification with examples. It defines the four elements as subject to generation and corruption: they shed one form (corruption) and acquire another (generation). There are twelve possible transformations (each of the four into the other three). Some transformations occur directly (without a middle term) when the two elements share one quality among the two that characterize each element (the four qualities are hot, cold, wet, dry). Earth and water transform directly because they share coldness, even if they differ in dryness; examples are given from alchemy and natural springs. Water and air transform directly because they share moisture (even if differing in heat); examples are water evaporating by heat (drying cloths) and air condensing into water on a cold surface. The text clarifies that the water on the surface of a bowl over ice is not due to seepage from inside, because water does not naturally rise. Finally, it begins to discuss fire and air, but the text is cut off. The last sentence contains a grammatical note about the phrase 'its formation.'

Misconception: That the four elements are eternal and unchanging.
Correction: The passage shows that elements undergo generation and corruption, meaning they change from one to another, indicating they are contingent and dependent on a creator.

When in your life have you experienced an unexpected change that seemed 'natural' but required a deeper recognition of Allah's power? How can that memory now serve as a reminder of your constant dependence on Him?
?

Refutation of the Necessity of Heat for Composition
Refutes necessity of heat for composition and validity of correlational reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that intense heat is necessary for composition is rationally unsound, and how the author critiques both the causal necessity and empirical correlation arguments.

Core Idea
Heat separates unlike things; causality cannot be inferred from correlation.

The author challenges a physical theory that compounds require intense heat to unify their elements. By examining the nature of heat, adjacency, and the limits of empirical reasoning, he shows that the argument fails both on its own premises and on methodological grounds.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
Read the opening lines describing why heat is deemed necessary: elements seek their natural places, so a unifier (heat) is required to cook and ripen them into a stable compound.
The opponent argues that only intense fire-heat can achieve this.
2
First refutation: behavior of heat
Note the author's initial response: heat separates unlike things and unites similar things, so it cannot serve as a unifier for different elements.
This directly undermines the opponent's premise.
3
Second refutation: adjacency and causality
Follow the reasoning that heat acts only by adjacency, and that permanent adjacency requires a cause. This cause could itself be the unifier, eliminating the need for heat.
This shifts the focus from heat to the cause of adjacency.
4
Alternative explanations
Consider the alternative proposals: moist-dry cohesion provides stability, and air could serve as a ripener. The author also questions the existence of airy parts in compounds.
These show that heat is not the only possible unifier.
5
Critique of empirical reasoning
Examine the plant example: the author argues that the observed correlation between heat, air, and growth does not prove causation. God could directly create the plant from nothing, breaking the usual pattern.
This highlights the methodological flaw of reasoning by correlation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - القسم الثالث : المقصد الثاني عشر : العناصر الأربعة والمركبات مختلفة بالطبع يطلب كل منها حيزه الطبيعي وذلك يوجب التفرق في المركب وعدم بقائه فلا بد فيه من جامع يفيده طبخاً ونضجاً يوجب حصول مزاج يستتبع له صورة نوعية مانعة من التفرق وما هو ذلك الجامع الذي يطبخ وينضج إلا الحرارة الشديدة القائمة بالنار فلا بد من وجودها فيه. قلنا : الحرارة لا تجمع المختلفات بل تفرقها وتجمع المتماثلات كما مر. ثم الحرارة القائمة لا تؤثر في الجزء الآخر إلا بمجاورة ولذلك الجوار دوام وذلك الجوار الدائم لا بد له من سبب فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك السبب سببا للاجتماع في حال بقاء المركب ومانعاً من التفرق ابتداءً أي بلا توسط شيء فلا يحتاج حينئذ إلى الجزء الناري وحرارته الطابخة المؤدية إلى المزاج المستتبع للصورة النوعية الحافظة للتركيب. على أن اختلاط الرطب باليابس يفيده استمساكاً عن التفرق فلا حاجة إلى جامع آخر. وقد يقال : الهواء حار فجاز أن يكون منضجاً ووجود الأجزاء الهوائية في المركب مما لم يتحقق إذ يجوز أن يكون تخلخل أجزاء المركب بوقوع الخلاء فيما بينها وكون تلك الأجزاء الباقية بعد التحليل ماء أو تراباً بالحقيقة غير معلوم لجواز أن يكون التشابه في الصورة المحسوسة دون الحقيقة. والثاني : وهو التركيب أنه يتكون من اجتماع الماء والأرض النبات وذلك ظاهر ولا بد في النبات من هواء يتخلل بين أجزائه ومن حرارة طابخة إذ لو فقد أحدهما أو لم يكن على ما ينبغي فسد الزرع كما إذا ألقينا البذر في موضع لا يصل إليه الهواء وحر الشمس، أو لا يكونان على ما ينبغي فإنه يفسد ولا ينبت. فدل ذلك على أن النبات مركب من الأربعة ومن النبات يحصل بعض الحيوان لأنه غذاؤه، ومنها يحصل الإنسان لأنه متولد من المني المتكون من الدم المتكون من الغذاء الذي هو نبات أو حيوان وكذا يحصل منهما بعض الحيوان الذي غذاؤه منهما كالجوارح فالكل أي جميع المركبات حتى المعادن فإنها في حكم النبات آيل أي راجع إلى حصولها من العناصر الأربعة وأنت تعلم أن ذلك الذي استدلوا به على تكون النبات من اجتماع هذه الأربعة استدلال بالدوران وأنه لا يفيد العلية حتى يعلم أن اجتماعها سبب لتكونه منها فلم لا يجوز أن يكون تكونه في حال اجتماعها لا منها بل يخلق الله إياه من العدم في تلك الحال على خرق العادة.
First Observatory - Third Section: Twelfth Aim: The four elements and compounds are different by nature, each seeking its natural place, and that necessitates separation in the compound and its non-continuance. So there must be in it a unifier that gives it cooking and ripening that necessitates the occurrence of a temperament that entails a specific form preventing separation. And what is that unifier that cooks and ripens except intense heat subsisting in fire? So its existence in it is necessary. We said: Heat does not unite different things, rather it separates them and unites similar things, as has passed. Then the subsisting heat does not affect the other part except by adjacency, and that adjacency has duration, and that permanent adjacency must have a cause. So why may it not be that that cause is a cause for union in the state of the compound's continuance and a preventer of separation initially, i.e., without any intermediary, so then there is no need for the fiery part and its cooking heat leading to the temperament that entails the specific form preserving the composition? Moreover, the mixing of moist with dry gives it cohesion against separation, so there is no need for another unifier. And it may be said: Air is hot, so it may be a ripener, and the existence of airy parts in the compound is not verified, for it is possible that the rarefaction of the compound's parts is by the occurrence of void between them, and that those parts remaining after analysis are in reality water or earth is unknown, for it is possible that the similarity is in the perceived form and not in reality. The second: composition: that the plant is formed from the aggregation of water and earth, and that is apparent, and there must be in the plant air permeating its parts and a cooking heat, for if either is missing or not as it should be, the crop spoils, as when we sow seed in a place where air and the heat of the sun do not reach it, or they are not as they should be, then it spoils and does not grow. So that indicates that the plant is composed of the four. And from the plant some animals are obtained because it is their food, and from them humans are obtained because he is generated from sperm formed from blood formed from food which is plant or animal, and likewise some animals whose food is from them, like birds of prey, are obtained from them. So all—that is, all compounds, even minerals, for they are in the ruling of the plant—are returning to being obtained from the four elements. And you know that what they adduced to prove the plant's formation from the aggregation of these four is reasoning by correlation (al-dawrān), and it does not prove causality, so that one knows that their aggregation is a cause for its formation from them. So why may it not be that its formation at the time of their aggregation is not from them, but rather God creates it from nothing in that state, breaking the custom?
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation of the argument that intense heat is necessary for the composition of bodies from the four elements. The author presents the opponent's claim: since elements naturally seek their own place, a compound would disintegrate without a unifier that cooks and ripens them into a temperament with a specific form, and only intense fire-heat can do that. The author responds first by denying the premise: heat, especially intense heat, separates unlike things and unites like things, as stated earlier. Moreover, heat only acts on an adjacent part, and that permanent adjacency itself needs a cause; why could that cause not be the very cause of union and prevention of separation, eliminating the need for a fiery part? Additionally, mixing moist and dry provides cohesion without a separate unifier. The author then considers a possible objection: air is hot and could serve as a ripener, but the existence of airy parts in the compound is unproven; rarefaction could be due to void, and the similarity of parts after analysis to water or earth might be only in appearance. Next, the author examines the second type of composition, namely that plants are formed from water and earth, noting that plants require air and heat for growth, and if either is absent or improper, the seed fails. This might seem to prove composition from four elements, but the author argues that this is reasoning by correlation (al-dawrān), which does not establish causality. It is possible that when these elements are present, God creates the plant directly from nothing, breaking the normal custom. Thus, the passage systematically dismantles both causal necessity and empirical arguments for elemental composition.

Key Terms
الحرارة الشديدة
المزاج
الصورة النوعية
استدلال بالدوران
خرق العادة
الجامع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intense heat is necessary to unify the four elements in compounds, and observable correlations prove causation.
Correction: Heat separates unlike things; permanent adjacency requires a cause that could be the unifier; correlation does not imply causation; God can create directly from nothing.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument against the necessity of intense heat for composition?
A) Heat separates unlike things rather than unites them.
B) Heat is not found in all compounds.
C) The cooking process is unnecessary.
D) Air is hotter than fire.

Key Takeaway
The author demonstrates that necessity cannot be proven by empirical correlation, and that divine causality transcends physical laws.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that when the four elements come together, a plant necessarily grows from them. But what if at this very moment, God could create that plant from nothing, without any cause? Would your heart still rely on secondary causes, or would it turn directly to the Creator? Today, identify one area—sickness, provision, or even the next breath—where you habitually trust the means more than the Maker. Will you consciously invoke His name as the true origin of that result?
?
When you see a pattern in your life—prayer brings peace, charity brings increase—do you treat it as a law that God must follow? The lesson shows that correlation is not causation. What if God chose to break the pattern tomorrow? Would your faith remain unshaken? Reflect: are you worshipping the pattern, or the One who can break it? Commit to one act today that intentionally depends on God breaking His usual custom.
?
The author argues that heat separates unlike things—yet the opponent insisted it unites them. Is there a belief you hold so tightly that you ignore evidence it contradicts? Ask yourself: 'Am I more attached to my own reasoning than to the truth Revelation brings?' Write down one conviction you have about how God works, and check if it is based on correlation or divine scripture. If it is the former, repent of limiting the All-Powerful.

How Substantial Forms Act on Matter and Adjacent Matter
Principle of substantial form action and proximity condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how substantial forms act first on their own matter and then on adjacent matter, and why adjacency (not necessarily direct contact) is the key condition for interaction between bodies.

Core Idea
Substantial forms act locally first, then propagate to adjacent matter via adjacency.

This passage from the kalam tradition explains the causal mechanism of substantial forms. Using the example of fire, it shows how a form affects its own matter first and then neighboring matter. The key principle is that adjacency (mujāwarah) is the necessary condition for interaction, not necessarily direct contact. The passage also distinguishes between simple adjacency and the more effective mutual contact (mumāssah).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the key terms: corporeal form, matter, adjacency, contact.
2
Identify the order of action
Notice that the passage says the form acts first on its own matter, then on adjacent matter. This is a two-step causal sequence.
The example of fire illustrates this.
3
Understand the condition of adjacency
The passage states that adjacency (al-mujāwarah) is a condition for interaction. It also distinguishes between mere adjacency and direct contact (mumāssah).
Contact is more effective but not required.
4
Relate to the bigger picture
This explains how bodies influence each other in the physical world, which is foundational for discussions about mixture and causality in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : الصورة الجسمية ) أي الصورة الحالة في الجسم التي هي مبدأ الآثار وهي الصورة النوعية ) تفعل أولاً في (مادتها التي حلّت هي فيها (ثم في مادة ما يجاورها ) . فالصورة النارية تسخن مادتها ثم مادة ما يجاورها، وكذا الحال في سائر الكيفيات وباقي العناصر ) فالمجاورة شرط للتفاعل ) الواقع بين الأجسام. ألا ترى أنّ النار لا تسخن إلا ما له وضع مخصوص وقرب معين بالنسبة إليها فإذا حصلت المجاورة بلا مماسة أمكن التفاعل بين الجسمين ) وأبلغ من ذلك ) التفاعل الحاصل بمجرد المجاورة. ( ما كان أي التفاعل الذي كان ( بالمماسة ) التي هي الغاية في المجاورة ( والمماسة إنما تكون بالسطوح و لا شك في أنه ( كلما كان السطوح أكثر كانت المماسة بها ( أتم، وذلك ) أي تكثر السطوح (إنما هو بحسب تصغر الأجزاء
They said: The corporeal form – meaning the form residing in the body which is the principle of effects and is the specific form – acts first on its matter in which it inheres, then on the matter of what is adjacent to it. So the form of fire heats its matter then the matter of what is adjacent to it, and the same holds for the rest of the qualities and the other elements. Thus adjacency is a condition for the interaction that occurs between bodies. Do you not see that fire does not heat except that which has a specific position and a determinate proximity relative to it? So if adjacency occurs without contact, interaction between the two bodies is possible. And more effective than that is the interaction that occurs by mere contact. That is, the interaction that occurs by contact, which is the ultimate degree of adjacency. Contact only occurs through surfaces, and there is no doubt that the more surfaces there are, the more complete the contact is. And that – i.e., the multiplication of surfaces – is only due to the smallness of the parts.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage explains how a substantial form affects its environment. It states that the form first acts on its own immediate matter and then on adjacent matter, using fire as an example. It then establishes that adjacency (al-mujāwarah) is a necessary condition for interaction between bodies. Even without direct contact, interaction is possible if the bodies are close enough. However, direct contact (al-mumāssah) is the strongest form of adjacency and yields more effective interaction. The completeness of contact depends on the number of surfaces involved, which in turn depends on how small the parts are. This sets the stage for discussing mixture.

Correction: The passage states that adjacency (mujāwarah) is sufficient for interaction; contact is more effective but not necessary.

C) The bodies must have the same form.

Key Takeaway
Substantial forms act first on their own matter and then on adjacent matter; adjacency is the key condition for interaction, and direct contact enhances it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Interaction of Opposite Qualities and Production of Mizaj
Describes how opposite qualities break each other's intensity and produce an intermediate quality called mizaj

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two philosophical interpretations of how opposite qualities interact in a mixture to produce a uniform temperament (mizaj).

Core Idea
Mixture does not destroy elements; their qualities are moderated to produce a balanced temperament.

This passage from a work on natural philosophy explains the mechanism by which four primary qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness) interact when elements are finely mixed. The author presents two competing accounts: one from physicians, where qualities remain but intensities are broken, and another from philosophers, where a new quality emanates from a higher principle. Understanding this helps clarify how physical mixture leads to the temperament that characterizes composite substances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions for mixture
Notice that the passage specifies that the parts of elements must be very small and completely mixed until full contact. This sets the stage for the interaction.
The term 'imtizaj' (mixture) implies a thorough blending at the particle level.
2
Understand the two probabilities
Read the two interpretations carefully. The first (physicians) says the original qualities remain but their intensities are broken. The second (philosophers) says the original qualities are replaced by a single quality from the First Principle.
Both agree that the specific forms of the elements persist.
3
Trace the interaction of heat and cold
Follow how heat and cold affect each other: the hot element loses intensity and acquires a lesser heat that is cold relative to its original and hot relative to the cold. This illustrates the breaking and moderation.
This process is symmetric: both sides are affected.
4
Recognize the final product: mizaj
When the effect from both sides is complete, all parts share a uniform intermediate quality at one degree among infinite potential degrees. This quality is called mizaj or temperament.
The similarity is real, not just sensory, contrasting with the view of 'al-khalīṭ'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و إذا تحققت ما صورناه لك فنقول : ( العناصر المختلفة الكيفية ) التي هي الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة ( إذا) تصغرت أجزاؤها جداً واختلطت اختلاطاً تاماً ( حتى حصل التماس الكامل ( بين أجزائها فعل صورة (كل) منها ( في مادة الآخر فكسرت منه سورة كيفية ) المضادة لكيفيتها ( حتى نقص ( العنصر البارد بفعل صورته قوله: فكسرت من سورة (كيفيته هاهنا احتمالان أحدهما وهو الأنسب بظاهر العبارة أن يبقى كيفية كل من العناصر الممتزجة. وإنما تنكسر سورة تلك الكيفية فقط بحيث تلتئم من الكيفيات المنكسرة كيفية متشابهة في جميع الأجزاء. وثانيهما أن ينخلع كيفية كل واحد منها ويفيض على مجموعها من المبدأ الفياض كيفية واحدة متشابهة في جميع أجزاء ذلك الممتزج ومع كل واحد من الاحتمالين كانت صورها النوعية باقية في أنفسها. هذا ثم إن الاحتمال الأول ينسب إلى الأطباء والاحتمال الثاني ينسب إلى الحكماء. ( من حر) العنصر الحار فتزول تلك الكيفية التي هي الحرارة الشديدة عن ذلك الحار ( ويحصل ) له كيفية ) حر أقل تستبرد ) هذه الكيفية الحاصلة بل محلها ( بالنسبة إلى الحار وتستسخن بالنسبة إلى البارد فإنها كيفية متوسطة بينهما ) إلى بين الحرارة الصرفة والبرودة الصرفة، فإذا قيست إلى إحداهما عدت من الأخرى ) وكذلك لا ينقض ( العنصر الحار بفعل صوته (من برد) العنصر ( البارد فيحصل ) له ( برد أقل مما كان (كما قررنا فإذا اشتد (التأثير) من الجانبين (حتى حصل في جميع الأجزاء ) من العنصر الحار والبارد ( كيفية متشابهة متوسطة هي في درجة واحدة من الدرجات الغير المتناهية بالقوة لا بالفعل أعني الدرجات ( التي هي بين غاية الحر وغاية البرد ) أي هي واقعة بين هاتين الغايتين ( وحصل التشابه بينها ) أي بين الأجزاء المذكورة ( في نفس الأمر بأن تكون أجزاء العنصر البارد موافقة في الكيفية لأجزاء العنصر الحار بلا تفاوت في الواقع فلا يكون التشابه حينئذ بحسب إدراك الحس فقط . كما أشار إليه بقوله : ( لا أنها للمجاورة يحس منها بكيفية متوسطة وإن كان كل واحد منها باقياً على صرافته ) في كيفيته كما يقول به أصحاب الخليط، وقس على ذلك حال الأجزاء الرطبة واليابسة فإذا استقر الكل على كيفية واحدة متوسطة توسطاً ما بین الكيفيات الأربع ) فهذه الكيفية المتشابهة تسمى مزاجا.
And when you have realized what we have depicted for you, we say: The elements differing in quality – which are heat, cold, moisture, and dryness – when their parts become very small and mix completely until complete contact occurs between their parts, the form of each acts on the matter of the other and breaks the intensity of the form of the opposite quality, so that the cold element decreases by the action of its form. His saying: 'It broke from the form of its quality' – here there are two probabilities. One of them, which is more appropriate to the apparent wording, is that the quality of each of the mixed elements remains, and only the intensity of that quality is broken, such that from the broken qualities a similar quality is brought together in all parts. The second is that the quality of each one is stripped off, and from the First Principle a single similar quality flows upon their totality in all parts of that mixture. And with each of the two probabilities, their specific forms remain in themselves. Then the first probability is attributed to the physicians, and the second to the philosophers. From the heat of the hot element, that intense heat of the hot element disappears, and it acquires a lesser heat which is considered cold relative to the hot and hot relative to the cold, because it is an intermediate quality between them – between pure heat and pure cold. So when compared to one of them, it is counted from the other. Similarly, the hot element is not broken by the action of the form of the cold element from the cold element, so it acquires a lesser cold than it had. When the effect intensifies from both sides until all parts of the hot and cold elements acquire a similar intermediate quality that is at one degree among the infinite degrees potentially not actually – meaning the degrees that lie between the extreme of heat and the extreme of cold – and similarity is achieved between those parts in reality, such that the parts of the cold element agree in quality with the parts of the hot element without any actual difference, so the similarity is not merely according to sensory perception. As he indicated by his saying: 'It is not that due to adjacency one senses an intermediate quality while each of them remains pure in its quality' as the proponents of al-khalīṭ say. And analogize that to the case of the wet and dry parts. So when everything settles on a single intermediate quality with a mean among the four qualities, that similar quality is called mizāj.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage details the process of mixture (imtizāj) leading to temperament (mizāj). It begins with the conditions: elemental particles must be very small and thoroughly mixed until complete contact. Then each form acts on the other's matter, breaking the intensity of the opposing quality. Two interpretations are given: (1) the original qualities remain but their intensities are broken, yielding a blend of broken intensities; (2) the original qualities are replaced by a single quality emanating from the First Principle. Both maintain that the specific forms of the elements persist. The text then describes how heat and cold interact: each extreme moderates the other, producing an intermediate quality that is relative to the extremes. This process continues until all parts share a uniform intermediate quality, not just in appearance but in reality. The passage concludes by stating that this resulting quality is called mizāj.

Misconception: Some think that mixture destroys the original elements or their forms entirely.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that with both probabilities, the specific forms (صورها النوعية) of the elements remain in themselves. Only the qualities or their intensities are affected.

Temperament (mizaj) arises from the interaction of opposite qualities, resulting in a uniform intermediate quality without destroying the underlying forms of the elements.

?

?

Which model better describes how God has refined your character—through gradual internal struggle or through a sudden grace that replaced old patterns? What does that say about your reliance on Him?

First Objection to Contact Requirement and Its Refutation
Presents and refutes the first objection that interaction can occur without contact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first objection to the necessity of contact for interaction is raised and refuted, and why empirical observation is preferred over pure theoretical argument in this context.

Core Idea
Interaction without contact is rationally possible, but empirical facts show contact is required in mixtures.

The first objection attacks the core claim that interaction requires contact. It offers everyday examples—like the sun heating the earth or a visible object affecting the eye—that seem to involve action at a distance. The author refutes this not by denying the examples' possibility, but by limiting the discussion to empirical observation of actual mixtures.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its examples
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how the objector presents two examples: the sun affecting the earth, and a visible object affecting the eye, both without contact.
The objector also notes that the sun does not affect intermediate bodies, which is puzzling.
2
Understand the potential counter-response
A hypothetical counter-argument is given: these are one-way actions, not mutual interactions. But the author dismisses this, saying even one-way action shows interaction without contact is not impossible.
The author's purpose is only to deny the necessity of contact, not to prove actual interaction.
3
Follow the author's reply and shift to observation
The author cites the 'Eastern Investigations' and advises reliance on observation. In the specific case of mixtures, we see that parts are in contact and only affect each other when in contact.
Thus the rational possibility of action-at-a-distance is irrelevant here.
4
Grasp the final resolution and marginal note
The marginal note clarifies that the rational possibility suffices for denial, and the author acknowledges such possibility only in other contexts, not in the mixture under discussion.
The state of the mixture is empirically known, so theoretical speculation is unnecessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإشكال عليه من وجوه أربعة: الأول: لا نسلم أن التفاعل بين الأجسام لا يكون إلا بالتماس، بل قد يكون بلا تماس كما تؤثر الشمس فيما يقابلها من الأرض بالتسخين والإضاءة ولا تماس بينهما مع أنها لا تؤثر بذلك في الأجسام القريبة منها المتوسطة بينهما والمبصر ليس في الباصرة قطعا مع أنه يؤثر فيها ولا يؤثر فيما بينهما فكيف يجزم بأن الفعل والانفعال بين الأجسام لا يوجدان إلا بالتلاقي والتماس. لا يقال المدعى نفي التفاعل بلا تجاوز وتماس وما ذكرتم من صورة النقض لا تفاعل إذ الفعل من جانب واحد فقط، لأن الشمس وإن أفادت الأرض سخونة وضوءاً لكنها لم تؤثر في الشمس شيئاً أصلاً، وكذا المرئي أثر في العين ولم تؤثر هي فيه قطعاً لأنا نقول: الغرض مما ذكرناه إنه لا مانع في العقل من تفاعل من غير ملاقاة كما نراه من جانب واحد وإنه يفيد هذا القدر وهو يكفينا. وفي المباحث المشرقية الصواب أن يترك هاهنا الاحتجاج ويعول على المشاهدة. فيقال: الكلام إنما وقع في أجزاء الممتزج لا محالة متلاقية ويشاهد أيضاً أن بعضها لا يؤثر في بعض ولا يتأثر عنه إلا بالتلاقي والتماس فلا يتجه أن يقال: لم لا يجوز في العقل تأثير عنصر في آخر من غير ملاقاة ومماسة فإن ذلك غير محتاج إليه فيما نحن بصدده بل الحق أن التأثير بينهما بلا تلاق محتمل وإن كان نادراً. قوله: ( وهو يكفينا إذ نحن بصدد المنع وليس غرضنا إيراد النقض الإجمالي فما ذكر من الاحتمال العقلي يكفينا . وقوله : إن التأثير بينهما أي بين العنصرين بلا تلاق محتمل أي محتمل في غير ما نحن فيه بعدده لا فيما نحن فيه بصدده إذ قد كان حاله مكشوفاً كما ذكره.
The objection to it (i.e., to what they said) has four aspects. First: We do not accept that interaction between bodies occurs only by contact; rather, it may occur without contact, as the sun affects the earth facing it by heating and illumination, and there is no contact between them, even though it does not affect the closer bodies between them. The visible object is not in the eye (i.e., not in contact) yet it affects it, and does not affect what is between them. So how can one be certain that action and passion between bodies only occur by meeting and contact? It cannot be said: The claim is the negation of interaction without contact and touching, and what you have presented as a counterexample is not interaction, since the action is only from one side. Because although the sun gives the earth heat and light, it does not affect the sun at all, and similarly the visible object affects the eye but the eye does not affect it. For we say: The purpose of what we mentioned is that there is no rational obstacle to interaction without contact, as we see it from one side, and that suffices us. In the Eastern Investigations, the correct approach is to abandon argumentation here and rely on observation. It is said: The discussion is only about the parts of the mixture, which are necessarily in contact, and it is also observed that some do not affect others and are not affected except by contact and touching. Thus it is not appropriate to say: Why is it not logically permissible for one element to affect another without contact and touching? For that is not needed in our current discussion. Rather, the truth is that interaction between them without contact is possible, even if rare. He said: (And that suffices us, for we are in a position of denial, and our aim is not to present a comprehensive refutation; what we have mentioned of rational possibility suffices us. And his saying: 'Indeed, interaction between them without contact is possible' means possible in other than our current context, not in our current context, for its state is revealed as he mentioned.)
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents the first of four objections against the theory that interaction (tafa'ul) between bodies requires direct contact (tamass). The objector argues that we observe clear cases of action without contact: the sun heats and illuminates the earth without touching it, and a visible object affects the eye without physical contact. Moreover, the sun does not affect the bodies in between, which are closer. The objector demands why one is certain that action and passion only occur through contact. A potential counter-response is dismissed: these examples are not mutual interactions but one-way actions (the sun affects the earth but not vice versa). However, the author replies that their purpose is merely to show that there is no rational impossibility in interaction without contact; even one-sided action demonstrates this possibility, and that is sufficient to refute the claim of necessity. Then the author refers to the 'Eastern Investigations' (al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya), suggesting that theoretical argument should be abandoned here and empirical observation relied upon. The observed reality is that the parts of a mixture are in contact, and it is observed that they only affect each other when in contact. Therefore, the possibility of action-at-a-distance is theoretically admissible but irrelevant to the present discussion, because the case at hand involves contact. The marginal note clarifies that the mentioned rational possibility suffices for the purpose of denial, and the possibility is acknowledged for other contexts, not for the mixture under discussion where the state is empirically known.

Key Terms
تفاعل
تماس
تلاقي
مباحث مشرقية
مشاهدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection proves that interaction can occur without contact, disproving the need for contact entirely.
Correction: The author grants the rational possibility of action-at-a-distance but limits the discussion to empirical observation of mixtures, which require contact.

Checkpoint
How does the author ultimately resolve the first objection regarding the necessity of contact for interaction?
A) By proving that action-at-a-distance is impossible
B) By citing the Eastern Investigations and relying on observation that mixture parts are in contact
C) By denying the examples of the sun and the visible object
D) By affirming that contact is necessary in all cases

Key Takeaway
The first objection is refuted by distinguishing theoretical possibility from empirical fact: mixtures require contact even if action-at-a-distance is not impossible.

First Objection: Interaction Without Contact
First objection: interaction can occur without contact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first objection against the necessity of contact for interaction is raised and answered, distinguishing between intellectual possibility and empirical observation.

Core Idea
Interaction without contact is intellectually possible, but observation shows mixture components require contact.

The claim that physical interaction always requires contact faces a strong objection: how can the sun heat the earth without touching it? This passage presents that objection and the nuanced response. The scholar concedes the intellectual possibility but insists on empirical observation for the specific case of mixture components.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its examples
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice the objector uses examples of the sun heating the earth and the visible object affecting the eye without contact.
These examples challenge the claim that contact is necessary for all interaction.
2
Identify the initial counter-response
The counter-response argues these are one-sided actions, not mutual interaction. Understand why the objector rejects this distinction.
3
Follow the final answer
The scholar accepts that interaction without contact is intellectually possible but argues that in the specific case of mixture components, observation shows contact is necessary.
This is a key methodological point: relying on empirical evidence.
4
Reflect on the overall debate
Notice how the objection is not fully refuted in principle but its relevance is limited to the context at hand.
This shows the precision of kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: لا نسلم أن التفاعل بين الأجسام لا يكون إلا بالتماس، بل قد يكون بلا تماس كما تؤثر الشمس فيما يقابلها من الأرض بالتسخين والإضاءة ولا تماس بينهما مع أنها لا تؤثر بذلك في الأجسام القريبة منها المتوسطة بينهما والمبصر ليس في الباصرة قطعا مع أنه يؤثر فيها ولا يؤثر فيما بينهما فكيف يجزم بأن الفعل والانفعال بين الأجسام لا يوجدان إلا بالتلاقي والتماس . لا يقال المدعى نفي التفاعل بلا تجاوز وتماس وما ذكرتم من صورة النقض لا تفاعل إذ الفعل من جانب واحد فقط، لأن الشمس وإن أفادت الأرض سخونة وضوءاً لكنها لم تؤثر في الشمس شيئاً أصلاً، وكذا المرئي أثر في العين ولم تؤثر هي فيه قطعاً لأنا نقول : الغرض مما ذكرناه إنه لا مانع في العقل من تفاعل من غير ملاقاة كما نراه من جانب واحد وإنه أي ما ذكرناه يفيد هذا القدر وهو يكفينا وفي المباحث المشرقية الصواب أن يترك هاهنا الاحتجاج ويعول على المشاهدة. فيقال : الكلام إنما وقع في أجزاء الممتزج لا محالة متلاقية ويشاهد أيضاً ويشاهد أيضاً أن بعضها لا يؤثر في بعض ولا يتأثر عنه إلا بالتلاقي والتماس فلا يتجه أن يقال : لم لا يجوز في العقل تأثير عنصر في آخر من غير ملاقاة ومماسة فإن ذلك غير محتاج إليه فيما نحن بصدده بل الحق أن التأثير بينهما بلا تلاق محتمل وإن كان نادراً.
First: We do not accept that interaction between bodies occurs only by contact; rather it may be without contact, as the sun affects the part of the earth facing it by heating and illumination while there is no contact between them, yet it does not affect the nearby bodies between them. And the visible object is not in the eye at all, yet it affects it and does not affect what is between them. So how can one affirm that action and passion between bodies exist only by meeting and contact? It cannot be said that the claim is the negation of interaction without adjacency and contact, and what you mentioned as a counterexample is not interaction because the action is from one side only. For the sun gives heat and light to the earth but does not affect the sun at all; similarly the visible affects the eye while the eye does not affect it. We say: The purpose of what we mentioned is that there is no intellectual impediment to interaction without contact, as we see one-sided interaction. And this suffices us. In the Eastern investigations, the correct view is to abandon argumentation here and rely on observation. So it is said: The discussion concerns the parts of a mixture which necessarily meet, and it is also observed that some do not affect others nor are affected except by meeting and contact. So it is not appropriate to say: Why is it not intellectually possible for an element to affect another without meeting and touching? For that is not needed in our present context. Rather, the truth is that effect between them without contact is possible, though rare.

Commentary
This passage presents and answers the first objection against the claim that interaction requires contact. The objector cites examples: the sun heats the earth without touching it, and the visible object affects the eye without contact. The initial counter-response says these are one-sided actions, not mutual interaction. The answer concedes that intellectual possibility exists but argues that in the specific case of mixture components, observation shows contact is necessary. The response from Eastern investigations suggests relying on empirical observation rather than pure intellectual argument.

Third Objection: Quality as Condition for Action Leads to Circularity
Third objection: Quality as condition leads to impossible coexistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical impossibility that arises when a quality is considered a condition for action in the context of form-causality.

Core Idea
A quality cannot be both agent and patient in the same act.

In this objection, the critic argues that attributing action to forms through qualities leads to an absurdity: the breaking quality and the broken quality would have to coexist. This sets up a deeper debate about where change actually occurs—in the matter or in the quality. Let's read the passage to see the full argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objector sets up the problem: the form acts through a quality, making that quality a condition for the action.
The key phrase is 'فالشرط يجب أن يكون مع المشروط'.
2
Identify the impossible consequence
The objector claims that if the quality is a condition, then the breaking quality and the produced broken quality must coexist, which is impossible.
This is the core of the circularity argument.
3
Understand the response and rebuttal
The response argues that the broken is the matter, not the quality. The objector rebuts that breakage of matter is realized through its quality.
The debate hinges on what 'breakage' means.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider why this objection matters for causality and the nature of forms. The discussion shows the complexity of attributing agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : أن يقال : المحذور الذي يلزم من جعل الكيفية فاعلة لازم أيضاً من نسبة الفعل إلى الصور، إذ الصورة إنما تفعل أي تكسر كيفية غير مادتها بواسطة الكيفية القائمة بها. فإن الصورة النارية لا تؤثر بذاتها في كسر البرودة بل بواسطة حرارتها فتكون الكيفية شرطاً في التأثير فيلزم اجتماع الكيفية الكاسرة مع الحادثة المنكسرة ، وذلك لأن الانكسارين يجوز أن يكونا متعاقبين وإلا انقلب المغلوب غالباً كما مر بل يكونان معاً والشرط يجب أن يكون المشروط فتوجد الكيفيتان الصرفتان مع الانكسارين فيلزم وجود الصرافة مع الانكسار وإنه محال لا يقال : المنكسر هو المادة لا الكيفية فلا محذور. لأنا نقول : انكسار المادة ليس في ذاتها بل في كيفيتها .
The third aspect: It may be said: The absurdity that follows from taking the quality as agent also follows from attributing the act to forms, because the form acts—i.e., breaks a quality not of its own matter—through the quality inhering in it. For the fire form does not affect the breaking of coldness by itself but through its heat. So the quality is a condition for the action, so that the breaking quality would coexist with the newly produced broken quality. This is because the two breakings may be successive; otherwise the defeated would become the victor, as previously. Rather, they occur together, and the condition must accompany the conditioned. Thus the two pure qualities would exist with the two breakings, requiring the coexistence of purity with breakage, which is impossible. It cannot be said that the broken is the matter, not the quality, so no absurdity. For we say: The breakage of the matter is not in itself but in its quality.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and its response. The objector argues that even if forms are the agents, they act through a quality (e.g., heat). This quality is a condition for the action, causing the breaking quality and the newly broken quality to coexist, which is impossible. The response states that the breakage occurs in the matter, not the quality. The objector's rebuttal clarifies that the breakage of matter is realized through its quality, so the problem remains. The passage ends with the response that the breakage of matter is not in itself but in its quality, which is the same point.

Key Terms
شرط
مشروط
انكسار
مادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The response successfully avoids the absurdity by shifting the breakage to matter.
Correction: The objector points out that breakage of matter is realized through its quality, so the problem remains; the response simply reiterates the initial claim.

Checkpoint
What impossibility does the objection highlight?
A) The form cannot act without matter.
B) The breaking quality and the resultant broken quality would coexist.
C) The matter cannot be broken.
D) The fire form cannot break coldness.

Key Takeaway
The objection shows that if a quality is a condition for action, then agent and patient qualities would coexist, leading to an absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The argument shows that a quality cannot be both agent and patient in the same act. In your spiritual life, are there areas where you try to be both the source of change and the one being changed? Are you willing to surrender this contradiction and rely solely on Allah?

Fourth Objection: Hot Water Mixed with Cold Water
Objection and answer regarding water mixing and agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection to causality based on the mixing of hot and cold water and how it is answered by appealing to accidental forced heat rather than a separate emanative principle.

Core Idea
Water's single form cannot explain both heating and cooling; only accidental heat can.

This passage presents a subtle objection: if both hot and cold water share the same substantial form, that form cannot be the agent of heating or cooling. The answer shows that the form acts indirectly through an accidental quality—forced heat—thereby preserving the unity of the form while explaining the observed change.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage's initial statement describing the mixing of hot and cold water and the conclusion that the form cannot be the agent.
Notice that the objection assumes water has only one form.
2
Examine the implied denial
The phrase 'إذا خلط' introduces the objection; the following sentence rejects the possibility that water has a form necessitating heat and breaking coldness.
3
Study the answer
Read the response: the form can be the true agent through the preparation of accidental forced heat, avoiding the need for a separate emanative principle.
This answer preserves the role of the substantial form while accounting for temporary qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع: الماء الحار إذ خلط بالماء البارد يكسر الحار من برده ومن المحال أن يقال للماء صورة توجب الحرارة وتكسر البرودة، بل ليس للماءين إلا صورة واحدة ( فعلم أن الفاعل ) فإن قيل : نحن إلخ هذا إشارة إلى منع . قوله : ( ومن المحال أن يقال إلخ ) فيه أن يقال لم لا يجوز أن يكون الفاعل الحقيقي لكسر برودة الماء هو الصورة لكن بواسطة إعداد الحرارة القسرية العارضة للماء؟ ولا حاجة إلى أن يقال : الفاعل الحقيقي لكسر البرودة هو المبدأ الفياض.
The fourth aspect: Hot water, when mixed with cold water, breaks the hot by the cold, and it is impossible to say that water has a form that necessitates heat and breaks coldness; rather, the two waters have only one form. Thus it is known that the agent—if it is said: 'We...' etc., this indicates a denial. His statement: 'And it is impossible to say, etc.' It may be said: Why is it not permissible that the true agent of breaking the coldness of water is the form, but through the preparation of the accidental forced heat that occurs to the water? And there is no need to say that the true agent of breaking coldness is the emanative principle.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection and its answer. The objection: when hot water is mixed with cold water, the hot breaks the cold, but water has only one form, not one that causes heat and another that breaks cold. Therefore the agent cannot be the form. The commentary anticipates a denial and provides a response: it is possible that the form is the true agent of breaking coldness, but through the preparation of accidental forced heat. This avoids positing a separate emanating principle as the agent.

Correction: The form acts indirectly via an accidental quality (forced heat), not directly.
