The Third and Fourth Arguments: Why Qualities Cannot Be Agents
Presents the third and fourth arguments against qualities as agents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key arguments (the impossibility of coexisting opposites and the water mixing example) that demonstrate why qualities cannot be the agents of change in kalam.

Core Idea
If a quality were the agent, opposites would have to coexist – but nature shows otherwise.

The author presents two decisive arguments against the claim that qualities (كيفيات) are the true agents of change. The first shows the logical absurdity of a quality acting on its opposite, while the second uses a concrete example of mixing hot and cold water to prove that the form cannot be the agent. These arguments lay the groundwork for the correct understanding of causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument carefully
Focus on the logical reasoning: if a quality is the agent of breaking its opposite, then both qualities must exist simultaneously, which is impossible.
Notice the condition and conditioned must be simultaneous.
2
Read the fourth argument
Consider the example of hot and cold water: there is only one form for water, so it cannot be responsible for both heat and cold.
This is a concrete illustration.
3
Understand the refutation of the potential objection
The author anticipates an objection that the broken is matter, not quality, and refutes it: the breaking of matter is in its quality, not essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثالث : ( أن يقال : المحذور الذي يلزم من جعل الكيفية فاعلة لازم أيضاً من نسبة الفعل إلى الصور، إذ ( الصورة إنما تفعل أي تكسر كيفية غير مادتها ) بواسطة الكيفية ) القائمة بها. فإن الصورة النارية لا تؤثر بذاتها في كسر البرودة بل بواسطة حرارتها ( فتكون الكيفية شرطاً في التأثير فيلزم اجتماع الكيفية الكاسرة مع الحادثة المنكسرة ) ، وذلك لأن الانكسارين يجوز أن يكونا متعاقبين وإلا انقلب المغلوب غالباً كما مر بل يكونان معاً والشرط يجب أن يكون المشروط فتوجد الكيفيتان الصرفتان مع الانكسارين فيلزم وجود الصرافة مع الانكسار ( وإنه محال لا يقال : المنكسر هو المادة لا الكيفية فلا محذور. لأنا نقول : انكسار المادة ليس في ذاتها بل في كيفيتها . الوجه (الرابع: الماء الحار إذ خلط بالماء البارد يكسر الحار من برده ومن المحال أن يقال للماء صورة توجب الحرارة ) وتكسر البرودة، بل ليس للماءين إلا صورة واحدة ( فعلم أن الفاعل ) لا
قوله : ( فإن قلت إلخ ) الأظهر أن هذا إثبات للمقدمة الممنوعة. وقوله : هي النارية إلخ أي فحينئذ لا يكون لها صور سوى هذه الكيفيات ومن قال بتفاوت الكيفيات مع بقاء الصور لا بد لها من دليل وقوله : الوجه الثالث إلخ نقض إجمالي . وقوله : الوجه الرابع معارضة وكل واحد من هذين الوجهين على تقدير تسليم وجود الصورة وراء الكيفية. وقوله ومن المحال أن يقال : إلخ فيه أن يقال لم لا يجوز أن يكون الفاعل الحقيقي لكسر برودة الماء هو الصورة لكن بواسطة إعداد الحرارة القسرية العارضة للماء؟ ولا حاجة إلى أن يقال : الفاعل الحقيقي لكسر البرودة هو المبدأ الفياض. وقوله : فإن قيل : نحن إلخ هذا إشارة إلى منع .
قوله : ( ومن المحال أن يقال : للماء صورة توجب الحرارة إلخ ) فإن قيل : إذا جاز إطلاق الفاعل على الصورة مجازاً بسبب الإعداد المذكور، كان جواز إطلاق الفاعل على الكيفية المعدة مجازاً بسبب ذلك الإعداد بالطريق الأولى فما وجه اختيار الإطلاق الأول وترك الإطلاق الثاني . قلنا : لعل الوجه في ذلك هو بقاء الصورة عند وجود الكيفية المنكسرة وعدم بقاء الكيفية المعدة عند ذلك وقوله : فإن هذا أهون من المنافاة ولعل (المصنف) إنما ذكر المنافاة هاهنا لأنها الصورة المائية منافية للحرارة بناءً على أن مقتضاها إنما هي البرودة المنافية للحرارة مطلقاً. وقوله : بل إن جعل الكيفيات أنفسها إلخ . يعني أن قوله : والمعد . قد ينافي في الأثر إنما يناسب
لكسر البرودة ( هي الكيفية دون الصورة، فإن قيل: نحن نطلق عليها ) أي على الصورة ( الفاعل (مجازاً) لا حقيقة فإنها ليست موجدة للكيفية المنكسرة . ( وإنما ذلك ) أي الحاصل من الصورة (إعداد) لمادة المجاور لقبول الكيفية المنكسرة، ( و ) أما ( الكيفية ) المنكسرة (المتوسطة ) فإنها ( تفيض ) على المركب ( عن مفيض المبدأ الفياض المسمى عندهم بالعقل الفعال ( والمعد قد ينافي الأثر الصادر من الفاعل بتوسط إعداده ( كالحركة والحصول في الطرف ) من المسافة فإن الحركة معدة لذلك الحصول مع امتناع اجتماعهما وحينئذ نقول : الصورة المائية بتوسط الحرارة العارضة تعد مادة الماء البارد لقبول الحرارة. وإن لم تكن تقتضيها بالذات، فإن هذا أهون من المنافاة بل إن جعل الكيفيات أنفسها معدة لمواد ما يضادها لم يلزم منه محال ) مما ذكر إذ المعد قد لا يجامع الأثر . ( قلنا : فالنزاع ) على هذا التقدير عائد إلى أن المبدأ فاعل ) مختار) فلا حاجة إلى إعداد ( أو موجب بالذات ) فيتوقف تأثيره على الإعداد ) وسنقيم الدلالة على أنه فاعل مختار) فيبطل القول : بأن الصورة أو الكيفية معدة لصدور المزاج عن المبدأ .
The third point: It may be said: The impossibility that follows from making the quality the agent also follows from attributing the action to forms, since the form only acts—i.e., breaks a quality not of its own matter—by means of the quality subsisting in it. Thus the fiery form does not effect by itself the breaking of coldness but by its heat, so the quality becomes a condition in the effect, and it would necessitate the co-existence of the breaking quality with the occurring broken quality. That is because the two breakings may be successive; otherwise the overcome would become the overcomer as previously mentioned, but they are together, and the condition must be concomitant with the conditioned, so the two direct qualities would exist with the two breakings, necessitating the existence of the directness with the breaking, which is absurd. It cannot be said: The broken is the matter, not the quality, so no problem. For we say: The breaking of matter is not in its essence but in its quality. The fourth point: Hot water, when mixed with cold water, breaks the hot from its cold, and it is impossible to say that water has a form that necessitates heat and breaks coldness; rather, the two waters have only one form. So it is known that the agent is not...
His saying: (If you say etc.) The apparent is that this is a proof of the denied premise. His saying: They are fiery etc., i.e., then they have no forms except these qualities, and whoever says that qualities differ while forms remain must have a proof. His saying: The third point etc., is a summary refutation. His saying: The fourth point, is a counterargument, and each of these two points is on the assumption of the existence of a form behind the quality. His saying: And it is impossible to say etc. In it, it may be said: Why is it not permissible that the real agent of breaking the coldness of water is the form, but by means of the preparation of the accidental heat of the water? And there is no need to say that the real agent of breaking the coldness is the emanating principle. His saying: If it is said: We etc., this indicates a denial.
His saying: (And it is impossible to say: Water has a form that necessitates heat etc.) For if it is said: If it is permissible to call the form an agent metaphorically due to the mentioned preparation, then calling the preparing quality an agent metaphorically due to that preparation would be more appropriate, so what is the reason for choosing the first usage and leaving the second? We say: Perhaps the reason is the persistence of the form at the occurrence of the broken quality and the non-persistence of the preparing quality at that time. His saying: For this is easier than opposition, and perhaps the author only mentioned opposition here because the water form is opposed to heat, based on the idea that its requirement is coldness, which is absolutely opposed to heat. His saying: Rather, if the qualities themselves are made etc. Meaning his saying: The preparer may be opposed in effect, but it is suitable for breaking coldness (i.e., the quality without the form). If it is said: We call it, i.e., the form, an agent metaphorically, not truly, for it is not the bringer into existence of the broken quality. Rather, that, i.e., what comes from the form, is a preparation of the matter of the adjacent to accept the broken quality. And as for the broken intermediate quality, it flows upon the composite from the emanator of the emanating principle, called by them the active intellect. And the preparer may oppose the effect emanating from the agent by means of its preparation, like movement and obtaining at the endpoint of the distance, for movement is a preparation for that obtaining while their coexistence is impossible. Then we say: The water form, by means of the accidental heat, prepares the matter of the cold water to accept heat, even if it does not necessitate it essentially, for this is easier than opposition. Rather, if the qualities themselves are made to prepare matters for what opposes them, no impossibility follows from what was mentioned, since the preparer may not accompany the effect. (We say: The dispute) on this estimation returns to that the principle is an agent by choice, so there is no need for preparation, or it is necessitating by essence, so its effect depends on preparation. And we will establish the proof that it is an agent by choice, so the statement that the form or the quality is a preparation for the emanation of the temperament from the principle is invalid.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument containing two 'points' (وجوه) and then an extended objection-answer exchange. The third point argues that if a quality were the agent of change, impossible consequences would follow: the breaking quality (e.g., heat) would have to coexist with the broken quality (e.g., coldness) because the quality is a condition for the effect, and condition and conditioned must be simultaneous. The fourth point uses the example of mixing hot and cold water: since there is only one water form, the form cannot be responsible for both heat and cold; the agent must be the quality. The author then anticipates an objection: why not say the form causes the breaking via its quality? The response is that if the form is called an agent only metaphorically due to its preparation, then the quality would be more deserving of that label because it persists with the effect. The discussion then shifts to the nature of the ultimate agent: the dispute reduces to whether the First Principle acts by free choice (فاعل مختار) or by necessity (موجب بالذات). If by choice, no preparation is needed; if by necessity, preparation is required. The author promises to prove that the First Principle is a free agent, thereby invalidating the claim that forms or qualities are merely preparers for the emanation of temperament.

Key Terms
كيفية
صورة
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the broken entity is the matter itself, not the quality, so there is no problem.
Correction: The author clarifies that the breaking of matter is actually a change in its quality, so the coexistence problem remains.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, why cannot the quality be the agent of change?
A) Because the quality would have to annihilate itself.
B) Because the quality and its opposite would have to coexist simultaneously.
C) Because the form prevents it.
D) Because matter cannot be acted upon.

Key Takeaway
The third and fourth arguments together demonstrate that qualities cannot be agents because of the impossibility of simultaneous opposites and the concrete evidence from water mixing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe a natural event – ice melting, water boiling – do you instinctively attribute the change to the qualities themselves (e.g., heat melts ice) without acknowledging that these qualities are not independent agents? How can you train your mind to see beyond secondary causes to the true Agent?
?
This lesson argues that qualities cannot be agents because opposites would have to coexist. Have you ever been content with a superficial explanation for an event, ignoring the logical contradictions it implies? What would it take for you to examine your own beliefs about cause and effect with the same rigor?
?
The example of hot and cold water shows that the form of water alone cannot be the agent of change. In your life, do you sometimes rely on 'forms' – your status, abilities, or circumstances – as if they act independently? Will you today remind yourself that only the Creator brings about change, and that you are utterly dependent on Him?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
8 min
The Objection and Resolution: Metaphorical Agency and the Ultimate Principle
Responds to objections about metaphorical agency and traces the dispute to the nature of the First Principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that forms are agents metaphorically is refuted, and how the dispute reduces to whether the First Principle acts by free choice or by necessity.

Core Idea
The real dispute is not about forms vs. qualities, but about whether God acts by choice or necessity.

After presenting the arguments, the author anticipates an objection: perhaps the form is called an agent metaphorically due to its preparation. He refutes this by showing that if any metaphor is allowed, the quality is more deserving. The discussion then deepens into the nature of the ultimate cause: does it act by free will, in which case no preparation is needed, or by necessity, requiring preparation? The author promises to prove that God is a free agent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector says that the form is called an agent metaphorically because it prepares the matter. The author questions why the quality is not more deserving of that label.
2
Follow the refutation
The author responds that the form persists while the preparing quality disappears, so the form's persistence makes it more plausible as a metaphor. Then he gives an example of movement and arrival.
3
Recognize the deeper dispute
The author concludes that the real dispute is not about whether forms or qualities are agents, but about whether the First Principle is an agent by choice or by necessity. He promises to prove that God is a free agent.
This sets up future lessons.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثالث : ( أن يقال : المحذور الذي يلزم من جعل الكيفية فاعلة لازم أيضاً من نسبة الفعل إلى الصور، إذ ( الصورة إنما تفعل أي تكسر كيفية غير مادتها ) بواسطة الكيفية ) القائمة بها. فإن الصورة النارية لا تؤثر بذاتها في كسر البرودة بل بواسطة حرارتها ( فتكون الكيفية شرطاً في التأثير فيلزم اجتماع الكيفية الكاسرة مع الحادثة المنكسرة ) ، وذلك لأن الانكسارين يجوز أن يكونا متعاقبين وإلا انقلب المغلوب غالباً كما مر بل يكونان معاً والشرط يجب أن يكون المشروط فتوجد الكيفيتان الصرفتان مع الانكسارين فيلزم وجود الصرافة مع الانكسار ( وإنه محال لا يقال : المنكسر هو المادة لا الكيفية فلا محذور. لأنا نقول : انكسار المادة ليس في ذاتها بل في كيفيتها . الوجه (الرابع: الماء الحار إذ خلط بالماء البارد يكسر الحار من برده ومن المحال أن يقال للماء صورة توجب الحرارة ) وتكسر البرودة، بل ليس للماءين إلا صورة واحدة ( فعلم أن الفاعل ) لا

قوله : ( ومن المحال أن يقال : للماء صورة توجب الحرارة إلخ ) فإن قيل : إذا جاز إطلاق الفاعل على الصورة مجازاً بسبب الإعداد المذكور، كان جواز إطلاق الفاعل على الكيفية المعدة مجازاً بسبب ذلك الإعداد بالطريق الأولى فما وجه اختيار الإطلاق الأول وترك الإطلاق الثاني . قلنا : لعل الوجه في ذلك هو بقاء الصورة عند وجود الكيفية المنكسرة وعدم بقاء الكيفية المعدة عند ذلك وقوله : فإن هذا أهون من المنافاة ولعل (المصنف) إنما ذكر المنافاة هاهنا لأنها الصورة المائية منافية للحرارة بناءً على أن مقتضاها إنما هي البرودة المنافية للحرارة مطلقاً. وقوله : بل إن جعل الكيفيات أنفسها إلخ . يعني أن قوله : والمعد . قد ينافي في الأثر إنما يناسب

The third point: It may be said: The impossibility that follows from making the quality the agent also follows from attributing the action to forms, since the form only acts—i.e., breaks a quality not of its own matter—by means of the quality subsisting in it. Thus the fiery form does not effect by itself the breaking of coldness but by its heat, so the quality becomes a condition in the effect, and it would necessitate the co-existence of the breaking quality with the occurring broken quality. That is because the two breakings may be successive; otherwise the overcome would become the overcomer as previously mentioned, but they are together, and the condition must be concomitant with the conditioned, so the two direct qualities would exist with the two breakings, necessitating the existence of the directness with the breaking, which is absurd. It cannot be said: The broken is the matter, not the quality, so no problem. For we say: The breaking of matter is not in its essence but in its quality. The fourth point: Hot water, when mixed with cold water, breaks the hot from its cold, and it is impossible to say that water has a form that necessitates heat and breaks coldness; rather, the two waters have only one form. So it is known that the agent is not...
His saying: (If you say etc.) The apparent is that this is a proof of the denied premise. His saying: They are fiery etc., i.e., then they have no forms except these qualities, and whoever says that qualities differ while forms remain must have a proof. His saying: The fourth point, is a counterargument, and each of these two points is on the assumption of the existence of a form behind the quality. Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument containing two 'points' (وجوه) and then an extended objection-answer exchange. The third point argues that if a quality were the agent of change, impossible consequences would follow: the breaking quality (e.g., heat) would have to coexist with the broken quality (e.g., coldness) because the quality is a condition for the effect, and condition and conditioned must be simultaneous. The fourth point uses the example of mixing hot and cold water: since there is only one water form, the form cannot be responsible for both heat and cold; the agent must be the quality. The author then anticipates an objection: why not say the form causes the breaking via its quality? The response is that if the form is called an agent only metaphorically due to its preparation, then the quality would be more deserving of that label because it persists with the effect. The discussion then shifts to the nature of the ultimate agent: the dispute reduces to whether the First Principle acts by free choice (فاعل مختار) or by necessity (موجب بالذات). If by choice, no preparation is needed; if by necessity, preparation is required. The author promises to prove that the First Principle is a free agent, thereby invalidating the claim that forms or qualities are merely preparers for the emanation of temperament.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you genuinely trust that Allah can act in your life without any preparation or means, or do you secretly believe He needs your efforts to set the stage? Reflect on a situation where you waited for conditions to be perfect before relying on Him. What does that reveal about your belief in His free agency?
?
When you see causes and effects in the world, do you attribute real agency to created things, or do you recognize them as mere preparations for Allah's direct action? How would your daily gratitude change if you saw every good as coming directly from Him, with no secondary cause truly producing it?
?
The passage states that the dispute reduces to whether the First Principle acts by choice or necessity. Consider your own life: do you live as though Allah’s actions are bound by the 'laws' of nature He set, or do you expect Him to act freely and directly in answer to your prayers? What is one step you can take this week to align your trust with the belief that He is a free agent?
